The Architecture of Harm (v2)

The State of Women's and Children's Health in the United States

A Labora Collective Intelligence Report — March 2026

Dr. Yamicia Connor, MD, PhD, MPH OB-GYN Physician Scientist | CEO & Founder, Diosa Ara | Creator, The Labora Collective

The Frame

The Labora Collective is a research and intelligence initiative of Diosa Ara, an emergency obstetrical infrastructure company. Dr. Yamicia Connor is an OB-GYN physician-scientist who founded Diosa Ara because she kept watching women die from things that should not kill anyone — and she understood that the deaths were not clinical failures. They were systems failures. The medicine exists. The protocols exist. The knowledge exists. What does not exist — what has never existed at sufficient scale in the United States — is the infrastructure to deliver that medicine to every woman who needs it, regardless of her race, her income, her zip code, or her immigration status. That is what Diosa Ara is building. That is what the Labora Collective was created to inform.

We built a weekly intelligence system in 2025 because clinical practice was showing us that the deterioration was accelerating at a rate that anecdotal observation could not keep up with. Every week, across ten research domains — clinical medicine, political and legal developments, economic analysis, health policy, innovation and research funding, reproductive rights, social determinants, the Trump administration, menopause, and sexual violence — we systematically track what has changed, how fast it is changing, and what specific policy decision caused the change. This report is the synthesis of everything that system has found.

The Thesis

Through two terms in the White House, a pandemic marked by federal failure, the orchestrated fall of Roe v. Wade, and now the systematic dismantling of the nation's public health infrastructure, Donald Trump and the conservative political movement he leads have had a more detrimental effect on the health, lives, and economic security of women and children in the United States than any administration in modern American history.

This is not an opinion. It is a claim we will prove — domain by domain, data point by data point, dated action by dated action — across every dimension of women's and children's health: mortality, clinical care, infrastructure, funding, disease prevention, research, child welfare, sexual violence, and economic security.

Why This Matters to You Personally

You have a mother. You have a daughter, a sister, a wife, a partner. Someone in your life has been pregnant, will be pregnant, or is caring for someone who is. That person lives inside the system this report documents.

You go on vacation. You drive to your cabin. Your wife is seven months pregnant. She starts bleeding. Do you expect there to be a hospital that can help her? There might not be. One-third of all U.S. counties have no obstetric services at all. Over 500 hospitals have closed their maternity units since 2010. If your cabin is in rural Idaho, 43% of the OB-GYNs have left the state. If it is in rural Texas, the nearest labor and delivery unit may be two hours away. If your wife is Black, she is three and a half times more likely to die than if she is white — and that ratio is getting worse, not better, in every state in the country.

There is not a single person connected to the human race that this crisis does not touch.

Why It Matters for the Nation

Infant mortality rates are among the most closely watched indicators in global development. They are not sentimental statistics. They are a direct measure of national infrastructure — of whether a country can keep its youngest citizens alive. Infant mortality captures everything: the quality of the healthcare system, the strength of the safety net, the investment in public health, the stability of the economy, the equity of the society. When infant mortality rises, it is not because babies got sicker. It is because the nation got weaker.

Women and children are not talking points for ideology. They are not culture war ammunition. They are data representing the infrastructure of a nation. And we are systematically destroying that infrastructure — in the obvious ways, through budget cuts and agency dismantlement, and in the less obvious ways, by allowing women and children to sicken, suffer, and die while the systems that should protect them are deliberately taken apart.

This report will show you exactly how it is being done. With data. With dates. With names.

The Arc: How We Got Here

Black maternal mortality was ignored for decades. Black women in the United States have been dying at two, three, four times the rate of white women for as long as anyone has been counting. This was known. It was published. It was presented at conferences. And the system did nothing — for decades. That neglect was not neutral. It allowed the entire maternal health infrastructure to weaken, because the deaths were concentrated among women the system did not prioritize. By the time anyone with power decided to act, the foundation was already cracked. Finally — after years of advocacy, after mounting data, after it became politically impossible to ignore — there were the beginnings of a response. Maternal Mortality Review Committees. State-level quality improvement. California proved you could cut maternal mortality in half. There was a window.

Then Trump 1.0 happened (2016–2020). The first Trump administration was the instrument of a fifty-year conservative legal strategy. The Federalist Society. The Heritage Foundation. Decades of TRAP laws and trigger bans, staged and waiting. Three Supreme Court appointments in four years — Gorsuch, Kavanaugh, Barrett. The judicial infrastructure was locked into place. The "Domestic Gag Rule" gutted Title X, stripping 1,000 clinics from the program. The fuse was lit.

Then COVID happened (2020–2022). The pandemic struck a system that had no reserves. Maternal mortality hit 32.9 per 100,000 — the highest in modern history. Black maternal mortality reached 69.9 per 100,000. Prenatal care collapsed. Schools closed and child abuse detection disappeared. Mental health among adolescent girls cratered. The healthcare workforce broke. Nothing recovered before the next blow.

Then Dobbs happened (June 2022). The fall of Roe was not a surprise. It was the culmination of everything Trump 1.0 set in motion. Trigger bans activated in 13 states. Providers fled. Hospitals closed. Sepsis rates for miscarriage care jumped 50% in Texas. Domestic violence spiked 7-10% in ban counties. Women denied abortions fell into poverty at four times the rate. 412 women were criminally charged for pregnancy outcomes. The system that was already failing entered structural collapse.

Then Trump 2.0 happened (January 2025). And Trump 2.0 did not just fail to fix the damage. It stripped the car for parts. Fired the teams tracking pregnancy outcomes. Froze the clinics. Terminated the research grants. Gutted the institute studying why Black women die. Proposed $900 billion in Medicaid cuts. Withdrew from the WHO. Put a vaccine skeptic in charge of HHS and a TV doctor in charge of Medicaid. Reduced the health workforce by 20,000. Labeled the research into racial health disparities as "DEI" and defunded it.

And what you are going to see — what the data will prove across every chapter of this report — is that mothers and babies are not well. They are not thriving. They are dying at rates that are rising, in patterns that are predictable, from causes that are preventable, because of decisions that are documented. We are going to show you how bad it is, with the numbers right in front of your eyes, and walk you through exactly how the connections work — from the defunding to the death, from the policy to the patient, from the decision in Washington to the woman bleeding in a car on a highway two hours from the nearest hospital that can help her.

How This Report Works

We organized this report by domain — each chapter covers one critical dimension of women's and children's health. Within every domain, we trace the same arc: where things stood before the current crisis began → what Trump 1.0 did → what the pandemic did → what began to recover → what Dobbs did → what Trump 2.0 is doing now.

Every data point in this report is held to the same standard: what was the number before, what is it now, when did it change, and what specific policy action caused the change. We do not say "things are getting worse." We show the slope — the rate of deterioration — and we connect it to the dated federal action that bent the curve. We analyze every finding through six equity lenses: race and ethnicity, income and class, geography, age and life stage, immigration status, and disability. A national average is a lie. The disparities are the story.

Chapter 1 covers maternal mortality — who is dying, at what rate, by which cause, and in which state.

Chapter 2 covers healthcare infrastructure — the hospitals closing, the providers leaving, the behavioral health workforce collapsing, the clinics that no longer exist.

Chapter 3 covers funding and the safety net — the money that kept the system alive and the deliberate decisions that took it apart.

Chapter 4 tracks disease acceleration — congenital syphilis, vaccination collapse, drug shortages, neonatal abstinence syndrome, cervical cancer gaps — the epidemics surging while the systems designed to contain them are being eliminated.

Chapter 5 documents the substance use crisis — overdose as the leading medical cause of maternal death, fentanyl-xylazine, and the destruction of the treatment infrastructure.

Chapter 6 covers perinatal mental health — the ACEs pipeline, untreated mood disorders, the psychiatric workforce shortage, and the doubling mechanism that turns childhood trauma into maternal death.

Chapter 7 follows pregnancy behind bars — shackling, denied care, the carceral pipeline, and what happens to children when their mothers are incarcerated.

Chapter 8 turns to the children — because every one of these policies was justified in the name of protecting children, and this chapter demonstrates with data that the children are not being protected.

Chapter 9 documents sexual violence — the prevalence numbers, the violence-to-pregnancy pipeline, the SANE shortage, sextortion, campus and military assault, Missing and Murdered Indigenous Women, and the collapse of federal protections.

Chapter 10 covers the immigration crisis — prenatal care avoidance, ICE raids near clinics, the erosion of the immigrant health advantage, and what is happening to pregnant women in detention.

Chapter 11 confronts the corruption — where the stripped funding went, the IVF contradiction, the Comstock Act, digital surveillance of pregnancy, and how the two-tier system has been formalized.

Chapter 12 documents the destruction of research and surveillance infrastructure — PRAMS, the CDC cuts, the instruments that made measurement possible and improvement achievable.

Chapter 13 maps the demographic collapse — falling fertility, the sterilization surge, and what happens to a nation's population when its reproductive policies produce the opposite of their stated intent.

Chapter 14 looks at what comes next — projections, the verdict, and the path forward.

What follows is everything we know.

Chapter 1: Maternal Mortality — Who Is Dying and Why

The defining measure of a nation's commitment to its women.

A young mother, pregnant with her second child, comes in for a fever and flank pain. She has a history of recurrent infections that were never properly worked up—a failure of primary care spanning a decade. Her blood pressure is dangerously high, spiking to 170. She had been seen the week before for the same issue, but no medication was started and she was sent home. Now she has headaches and is seeing spots. The facility where she is sitting does not have a neonatal intensive care unit equipped to handle the extremely premature delivery that is about to become necessary. While the medical team starts magnesium sulfate to prevent seizures and scrambles to arrange a transfer to a hospital that can actually keep her two-pound baby alive, the real reason she hasn't been able to manage her health comes out.

Her partner had loaded a gun on her. He had jumped her while she was pregnant, then sent other people to jump her again days later. He had burned all of their ultrasound pictures. She lives alone with her older child. She has no family support, no safe place to go, and her rent is about to double. When I tell her that the number one cause of death for pregnant women is murder—more than hemorrhage, more than preeclampsia—she is not surprised. She already knows. The failing infrastructure isn't just the lack of a NICU. It's the absolute absence of a safety net.

The hospital's social work department cannot help her. "You just don't have great success with our social workers," I tell the medical team later. Instead, I text my own nonprofit contacts. I print out local domestic violence hotline numbers. I counsel the patient to hide her documents—passport, birth certificates, cash—at a friend's house. To set aside ten dollars a week. To tell someone. The clinical note becomes a safety plan. The prescription pad becomes a resource list. And then the next patient is waiting.

These are not outliers. This is a Monday morning.

The Number That Reframes Everything

The leading cause of pregnancy-associated death in the United States is not hemorrhage. It is not hypertension. It is not infection. It is homicide — at a rate of 3.62 per 100,000 live births, exceeding every obstetric cause of maternal death combined by more than two times.[^1] Sixty-eight percent involve firearms.[^2] The killer is almost always a male intimate partner. Black women aged 18 to 24 die from pregnancy-associated homicide at 8 per 100,000 — four times the national average.[^3]

The leading medical cause — surpassing hemorrhage, preeclampsia, and sepsis since 2020 — is overdose. Overdose deaths among pregnant and postpartum women increased 81 percent between 2017 and 2020.[^4] Mental health conditions account for 23 percent of all pregnancy-related deaths.[^5] Together, violence, overdose, and mental health — none of which are treated by the obstetric interventions that dominate federal maternal health policy — kill more pregnant women than every clinical cause combined.

This is not a failure of obstetric medicine. It is a failure of the entire system surrounding the woman — the partner who kills her, the substance use treatment she cannot access, the mental health infrastructure that does not exist, the economic trap that prevents her from leaving, and the policies that have made every one of those failures worse.

Before

The United States has the highest maternal mortality rate of any high-income country in the world. This has been true for decades. While peer nations were reducing maternal death to low single digits — Sweden at 4 per 100,000, Norway at 2, Japan at 3[^6] — the United States was going the wrong direction. Between 2000 and 2014, the U.S. maternal mortality ratio increased 26.6%, from 18.8 to 23.8 deaths per 100,000 live births.[^7] No other developed nation was experiencing increases. The United States was alone.

The United States spends more on healthcare than any country in the world — over $4.5 trillion per year, approximately $13,500 per person.[^8] No other high-income nation comes close. The average among OECD countries is roughly half that. And for this investment — the largest healthcare expenditure in human history — the United States produces the worst maternal mortality, the worst infant mortality, and the lowest life expectancy of any wealthy democracy. The Commonwealth Fund's international comparison, updated regularly, consistently ranks the United States last or near-last among high-income countries in healthcare access, equity, and outcomes. Americans die younger. American babies die more often. American mothers die at rates that other wealthy nations eliminated a generation ago. And Americans go bankrupt doing it.

The average total healthcare cost of pregnancy and childbirth in the United States is $18,865 — $26,200 for a cesarean section.[^9] Employees with employer-sponsored insurance still pay roughly $2,854 to $3,200 out of pocket. Without insurance, the range is $9,000 to $25,000. The United States is the most expensive country in the world in which to have a baby. It is also the most dangerous wealthy country in which to have a baby. Those two facts are not unrelated. They are the same failure expressed in different currencies.

The United States remains the only industrialized wealthy nation on earth without a guaranteed federal paid family leave policy. It is the only wealthy nation where women can lose their jobs for taking time to recover from childbirth. It is the only wealthy nation where the decision to have a child is, for millions of women, a calculation about whether they can afford to survive the experience.

Women hold 64% of all U.S. student loan debt[^10] — between $833 billion and $929 billion. Black women carry an average of $58,252 in educational debt versus $29,323 for white women.[^11] After twelve years of repayment, the average Black borrower still owes 112% of their original loan.[^12] This debt suppresses homeownership, delays family formation, and compounds every other economic barrier that pregnancy and childbirth impose.

By 2021, the national maternal mortality rate had reached 32.9 per 100,000[^13], driven upward by the pandemic. It came down to 22.3 in 2022, then 18.6 in 2023. Provisional 2024 data shows the national rate at approximately 17.9 — 649 women died.[^14] The CDC's own maternal mortality review committees have found that 84% to 93% of maternal deaths in the United States are preventable.[^15] Preventable does not mean "unfortunate." It means the system failed. The medicine existed. The protocols existed. Something in the chain between the woman and the intervention broke down — a missed diagnosis, a delayed referral, a closed unit, a missing provider, a transportation gap, an insurance lapse, a language barrier, a provider who did not listen.

The Racial Chasm. National averages conceal a stratification by race that is among the most severe health disparities documented in modern medicine. In 2023, Black non-Hispanic women died at 50.3 per 100,000 live births — 3.5 times the rate for white non-Hispanic women at 14.5.[^16] In 2024, provisional data shows Black women at 44.8 per 100,000 versus white women at 14.2 — a ratio of 3.2 to 1.[^17] Between 2021 and 2023, the Black-to-white maternal mortality ratio widened from 2.6x to 3.5x. The gap is not closing.

Native American and Alaska Native women have a maternal mortality rate of 118.7 per 100,000 — 4.9 times the white rate.[^18] The Indian Health Service, the only federal healthcare system serving these communities, was already chronically underfunded before 2025.

The Racial Wealth Gap as Structural Foundation. The racial disparity in maternal death does not exist in a vacuum. It sits on top of a wealth gap that has been functionally stagnant for three decades: for every $100 that white households hold, Black households hold $15.[^19] The mean racial wealth gap grew 38 percent between 2019 and 2022 alone — from $842,000 to $1.15 million.[^20] In 1992, the gap was 86 percent. In 2022, it was 85 percent.[^21] Thirty years of economic policy produced zero structural change. This is the economic architecture beneath the mortality disparity: women who cannot afford to leave violent partners, who cannot afford the $2,854 out-of-pocket cost of insured childbirth, who carry $58,252 in average educational debt (versus $29,323 for white women), who after twelve years of repayment still owe 112 percent of their original loans. The wealth gap does not explain the mortality gap by itself — Black women with household incomes above $100,000 still die at higher rates than white women below the poverty line — but it compounds every other barrier the system places between a woman and survival.

These disparities are not explained by individual behavior, insurance status, or prenatal care utilization in isolation. They are explained by structural racism — by a healthcare system that systematically undertreats Black women's pain, dismisses their symptoms, delays their diagnoses, and provides them lower-quality care at every stage of pregnancy and postpartum recovery. Black women are up to 50% more likely to experience severe maternal morbidity.[^22] They face higher rates of emergency cesarean sections, the highest rates of preterm birth, and are overwhelmingly more likely to receive delayed or absent prenatal care. 54% of Black women report feeling the effects of racial and gender bias[^23] in the healthcare system. One in five say a provider did not take their pain seriously because of their race. A failure of provider active-listening has been repeatedly cited as the core clinical trigger for delayed diagnosis of conditions like preeclampsia in Black and Brown patients.

In 2023, leading clinical voices formalized a mandate to abandon "race-based medicine" — which assumes biological racial differences drive outcomes — in favor of "race-conscious medicine," a framework that identifies racism and discriminatory care structures as the primary drivers of disparity. The disparities persist even when controlling for every variable except the system itself. The system is the variable.

A Note on Data Reliability. The National Center for Health Statistics stopped producing national maternal mortality rates for approximately a decade — citing concerns over the accuracy of the "pregnancy checkbox" on death certificates — only resuming publication in 2018. This history of data volatility is not a footnote. It is the context in which all current "stabilizations" must be read: some portion of apparent improvement may reflect shifting reporting methodology rather than improved clinical outcomes. Provisional figures are routinely revised upward as late death records are submitted and as inclusion guidelines shift. The NCHS and leading public health researchers explicitly caution that recent apparent stabilization is statistically insignificant — a plateau masking deep structural rot rather than a meaningful clinical improvement. The aggregate figure of 649 maternal deaths in 2024 compared to 669 in 2023 represents a plateau in a system that remains catastrophically behind peer nations where maternal mortality ratios are often three to four times lower than those found in the United States.

Age and the Mortality Gradient. The age-stratified picture is largely absent from national discourse. For women aged 40 and older, the maternal mortality rate in 2024 stood at 62.3 per 100,000[^24] live births — nearly five times the rate for women younger than 25, which remained at 13.7. This age-related disparity intensified sharply during the pandemic, when the mortality rate for women 40 and older reached 106.3 per 100,000 at peak — a figure that would constitute a declared public health emergency in any peer nation. The mechanisms driving these age-related disparities include higher rates of underlying cardiovascular conditions, greater likelihood of multiple gestation, and the compounding effects of inadequate housing, environmental hazards, and decades of disparate access to quality prenatal care. These social drivers — not individual clinical choices — form the true foundation of the crisis beneath the clinical surface.

Trump 1.0 (2016–2020)

The first Trump administration was the decisive instrument of a fifty-year conservative legal strategy — the Federalist Society, founded in 1982, building the pipeline of conservative legal minds at every level of the federal judiciary; the Heritage Foundation and allied organizations producing model legislation, TRAP laws, waiting periods, and trigger bans written and placed in state legislatures across the country for decades, staged and waiting. By the time the Supreme Court was ready, 13 states had trigger bans on the books[^25], written and waiting for the day Roe fell. This was not reactive. It was strategic.

Three Supreme Court vacancies in four years — Neil Gorsuch (2017), Brett Kavanaugh (2018), Amy Coney Barrett (2020) — gave the conservative movement the supermajority it had been building toward for fifty years. Barrett's confirmation, eight days before the 2020 election, replacing Ruth Bader Ginsburg, was the final piece.

The Domestic Gag Rule gutted Title X, forcing approximately 1,000 clinics out of the program[^26] and eliminating not just family planning but co-located prenatal care, cancer screening, and STI testing — the upstream services that prevent the downstream emergencies that kill women. The fetal tissue research ban in 2019 disrupted clinical trials studying preeclampsia and placental development. The judicial fuse was lit.

The Pandemic (2020–2022)

Maternal mortality surged to 32.9 per 100,000 in 2021[^27] — the highest recorded rate in modern American history. Black maternal mortality reached 69.9 per 100,000 in 2021 — a number that would constitute a public health emergency in any peer nation. Maternal deaths surged 46% in states that had refused to expand Medicaid versus 21% in expansion states.[^28] Unvaccinated pregnant women experienced catastrophic outcomes: higher rates of ICU admission, ventilation, and death from COVID-19 than any other demographic group.

The pandemic exposed what clinicians already knew: the American obstetric system operates with no surge capacity. There are no backup labor and delivery units. There are no reserve OB-GYNs. When a hospital diverts obstetric nurses to COVID wards — which happened in hospital after hospital — there is nobody to replace them. Eighteen rural maternity units closed in 2020 alone.[^29]

Prenatal care — the single most effective intervention for preventing maternal and infant death — collapsed. Women missed appointments because clinics closed, because they were afraid of COVID exposure, because they could not arrange childcare, because they lost their jobs and their insurance in the same week. The shift to telehealth worked for women with smartphones, reliable internet, and private spaces to talk. It did not work for women without any of those things. Every missed prenatal visit was a missed screening: undetected preeclampsia progresses to seizures, stroke, and death; undetected gestational diabetes produces macrosomic babies, birth injuries, and neonatal ICU admissions; undetected syphilis during pregnancy produces congenital syphilis in the infant — blindness, bone deformity, organ damage, death.

What Began to Recover

Under the Biden administration, 48 states extended postpartum Medicaid coverage from 60 days to 12 months — a critical policy change adopted even in conservative states under pressure from their own mortality data. HIPAA reproductive privacy protections were updated. EMTALA guidance was clarified to require emergency abortion care. Title X funding was restored. Fetal tissue research restrictions were reversed.

Overall maternal mortality came down: 32.9 (2021) → 22.3 (2022) → 18.6 (2023). Black maternal mortality: 69.9 → 49.5 → 50.3. Still catastrophic. Still 3.5 times the white rate. But the trajectory was bending — there was evidence that the system could improve if the infrastructure was supported.

Dobbs (June 2022 Forward)

Trump's judicial appointments made Dobbs possible. Dobbs made everything worse.

When the Dobbs decision came on June 24, 2022, it did not merely reverse Roe v. Wade. It eliminated the constitutional right to abortion that had been the legal floor beneath reproductive healthcare for 49 years. Within weeks, abortion was banned or severely restricted in 21 states. Trigger bans activated. Providers fled. Hospitals closed labor and delivery units. Malpractice premiums spiked. Medical students stopped applying to residencies in states where they could not be trained in the full scope of obstetric care.

States with abortion bans now show maternal death rates 62% higher than states preserving access[^30] — 28.8 versus 17.8 per 100,000 births. Bans in 14 states have produced an estimated 22,180 additional live births and 478 additional infant deaths above what would have been expected without the bans. Black infants died at 11% higher than expected — 265 additional Black infant deaths. A total national ban would produce an estimated 140 additional maternal deaths annually: a 21% overall increase and a 33% increase for Black women.

Texas: A 56% increase in maternal mortality between 2019 and 2022[^31] — five times the 11% national increase over the same period. Texas maternal mortality is now 155% higher than California's. The racial breakdown destroys the assumption that this is only a racial disparity story: white women saw a 95% increase, Black women 38%, Hispanic women 30%. The single largest increase was among white women — meaning the policy itself, not the pre-existing racial health gap, is driving the deaths. The Texas six-week ban was associated with a 12.9% increase in infant and neonatal mortality. Infant deaths with congenital anomalies spiked 12.7% above expected baselines in 2022. Sepsis rates for women treated for second-trimester miscarriages jumped 50% because physicians were too afraid of prosecution to provide timely care. In Texas, clinical documentation must now read as a legal defense brief. Providers must document precisely why an intervention is required to prevent "substantial and irreversible impairment," using explicit statutory language. The Texas Attorney General has filed suits to block federal HIPAA protections regarding out-of-state medical records, creating near-total surveillance over maternal charting.

The specific clinical mechanism driving the sepsis surge has been documented by ProPublica and reviewed by dozens of maternal health experts: the most pronounced increase occurred among patients admitted while the fetus still had a detectable heartbeat — cases in which the pregnancy was no longer viable or was actively endangering the mother's life, but in which the presence of cardiac activity created legal ambiguity that physicians chose to wait out rather than risk criminal prosecution. The delay was not clinical uncertainty. It was legal fear. Case counts: 67 sepsis cases related to second-trimester pregnancy loss in 2021, rising to 99 in 2023, even as national maternal mortality rates were declining over the same period. Pre-pandemic baseline maternal hospital deaths in Texas were approximately 79; by the post-ban period of 2022-2023, that number rose to 120 — a 51.9% increase. Regional variation within Texas further documents the role of institutional protocol: Houston-area hospitals, which adopted more restrictive interpretations of the state law, saw a 63% increase in sepsis rates; Dallas-area hospitals, which modified their protocols after witnessing adverse outcomes, saw a more moderate but still alarming 29% increase. The Texas state government has declined to review maternal deaths from 2022 and 2023 — a decision that maternal health researchers characterize as a systematic effort to sever the documented causal link between legislative action and maternal fatality. When the data would prove the policy is lethal, do not produce the data.

Idaho: Maternal mortality surged 121.5% between 2019 and 2021[^32] leading into the ban. Between 22% and 43% of all practicing OB-GYNs left the state. Idaho's maternal-fetal medicine specialists — the physicians who manage the highest-risk pregnancies — went from 9 to fewer than 5. Complete labor and delivery unit closures followed. The Attorney General attempted to legally block doctors from even referring patients to out-of-state clinics.

Georgia: Rate: 48.4 per 100,000.[^33] Florida: The six-week ban, effective May 2024, produced an immediate 30-35% drop in total clinician-provided abortions and a catastrophic 80% decline in care from Planned Parenthood clinics — severing the entire Southeastern abortion access pipeline, since Florida had been the destination for women fleeing bans in Georgia, Alabama, Mississippi, and Louisiana.

We know the names: Amber Thurman in Georgia. Josseli Barnica. Nevaeh Crain. Candy Miller. Women who died because they could not access timely medical care in their states. In Texas, a woman experiencing a miscarriage waited 40 hours for treatment while bleeding and in pain as hospital staff consulted lawyers. In Missouri, doctors told a woman whose water broke at 18 weeks that "current Missouri law supersedes our medical judgment." In Idaho, the state's largest hospital system stopped delivering babies altogether.

The Criminalization of Pregnancy. In the two years following Dobbs, 412 criminal charges were filed against individuals for pregnancy outcomes[^34] — doubling from year one to year two. Alabama accounted for 192 charges. Oklahoma: 112. 75% of the defendants were low-income. 97% of charges included substance use allegations. 264 of those 412 cases originated from medical disclosure — doctors and nurses reported their own patients to law enforcement. A woman went to her doctor. The doctor reported her. She was charged with a crime. Women in border regions have reduced their prenatal visits by 40-60%. The women who most need prenatal care are avoiding it because they are afraid that seeing a doctor will result in criminal charges. Prenatal care is the single most effective intervention for preventing maternal and infant death. When women avoid it, they die.

The Dobbs-Violence Connection. The relationship between abortion access and violence is now quantified: a 7-10% increase in intimate partner violence in ban counties[^35], a 3.4% increase in the intimate partner homicide rate per additional restriction, and an estimated 9,000 to 14,000 additional domestic violence incidents nationally. When a woman cannot leave a pregnancy, she cannot leave the relationship. Chapter 9 documents this connection in full — from prevalence through mechanism to the geographic concentration that compounds every disparity for Black women in ban states.

The Comstock Threat. The Comstock Act — an 1873 law that prohibits sending "obscene" materials through the mail — is being positioned as a mechanism to ban the mailing of mifepristone and other reproductive healthcare materials without any new legislation, without any vote of Congress. Project 2025 explicitly directs the Department of Justice to weaponize this statute. Nearly 1 in 4 U.S. abortions were telehealth-delivered by late 2024[^36], with over 40,000 provided directly into ban states under the protection of shield laws enacted by 18 states. Comstock enforcement would collapse that entire infrastructure overnight.

Trump 2.0 (January 2025 Forward)

The systematic dismantling of maternal health infrastructure accelerated rapidly in 2025 and 2026. The PRAMS team — the only people in the federal government tracking pregnancy outcomes across all 50 states for 38 years — was fired on April 1, 2025.[^37] PRAMS funding expires April 2026. Two of three branches of the CDC Division of Reproductive Health were eliminated. The Women's Health and Fertility Branch — gone. The Contraceptive Best Practices Team — gone.

In March 2025, an executive restructuring directed by the Department of Government Efficiency (DOGE) mandated that the Health Resources and Services Administration (HRSA), SAMHSA, and several CDC programs be absorbed into a new American Health Administration (AHA) with a 10,000-person staff reduction.[^41] The Alliance for Innovation on Maternal Health (AIM) — a federal program that had successfully reached over 1,000 hospitals and reduced severe maternal morbidity by 15-20% through standardized care bundles — was slated for elimination in the FY2026 budget cuts.[^42]

The federal legislative response effectively ceased. The "Momnibus" package, intended to comprehensively address Black maternal mortality, failed for a third consecutive Congress and was not even reintroduced in the 119th Congress (2025-2026) under the Republican majority.[^43] During an April 2026 House Energy & Commerce Committee hearing, when Representative Summer Lee asked HHS Secretary Robert F. Kennedy Jr. how the department planned to address the Black maternal mortality crisis after instructing grant applicants to remove the word "Black" from applications, the Secretary did not deny the instruction, instead reframing the documentation of racial disparity itself as a divisive act.[^44]

These infrastructure cuts collided with emerging clinical crises. Between 2022 and 2024, maternal syphilis rose by 28%, driving catastrophic outcomes in congenital syphilis. Simultaneously, the only safe treatment during pregnancy — Bicillin L-A — entered a severe shortage projected to last through late 2026, exacerbated by the dismantling of HHS drug supply chain monitoring.[^45]

Congress reauthorized the Preventing Maternal Deaths Act through 2030 with $113.5 million for Maternal Mortality Review Committees. So the committees that investigate maternal deaths are funded. But the surveillance system that feeds them data is being destroyed. They funded the ability to count how women die while defunding the ability to prevent those deaths. And in states like Idaho, the response to the data was even more cynical — Idaho actively tried to defund and disband its own MMRC to stop publishing data showing that its abortion ban is killing women. When the data proves your policy is lethal, destroy the data.

The NIMHD — the only federal institute specifically tasked with studying why Black women die at three to four times the white rate — had 77 grants terminated ($223.5 million), a 29.6% cut to its funding.[^38] It received 61% fewer competitive grants. The institute itself was targeted for elimination. The framing: "anti-DEI." The function: defunding the only research infrastructure specifically designed to understand the patients who are dying the most. Title X funding was frozen under the guise of reviewing grantee compliance with anti-DEI executive orders — weaponizing anti-diversity language to strip funding from the clinics that serve the women with the worst outcomes.

Even in California — the best-performing state, with a maternal mortality rate of 4.0 per 100,000[^39] and a 55% reduction achieved through the CMQCC — Black women still die at three to four times the white rate. Only 17% of California hospitals complied with a 2019 racism-in-medicine training law. Approximately 70 maternal deaths per year, roughly 60% preventable. The racial gap survives even the best systems.

In Louisiana — 58.1 per 100,000[^40], fourteen times California's rate — 59% of Black maternal deaths are preventable versus 9% of white deaths. And Louisiana's Governor Jeff Landry — dispatched by Trump as special envoy to Greenland — announced in February 2026 he was sending a Navy hospital ship to Greenland "to take care of the many people who are sick." Greenland, which has universal free healthcare, said no thank you. Both Navy hospital ships were in drydock in Alabama. Landry's own state — 73% of residents lack sufficient primary care, 86% lack dental providers, 93% lack mental health providers — has a maternal mortality rate fourteen times California's. He offered healthcare to a country that already has it while presiding over a maternal health catastrophe at home.

Full 2024 Demographic Breakdown. The national rate of 17.9 masks a more complex picture when disaggregated fully. The NCHS provisional 2024 data shows:

Demographic Group 2023 Rate 2024 Rate Percent Change
National Average (per 100k births) 18.6 17.9 -3.7%
Non-Hispanic Black Women 49.5 44.8 -9.5%
Non-Hispanic White Women 14.9 14.2 -4.7%
Hispanic Women 12.1 12.1 0.0%
Non-Hispanic Asian Women 14.5 18.1 +24.8%
Women Aged 40 and Over 61.2 62.3 +1.8%
Total Maternal Deaths 669 649 -3.0%

Three findings from this table demand attention. First, Hispanic women saw zero improvement — a flat line at 12.1 while the national rate declined. Second, Non-Hispanic Asian women saw a 24.8% increase — moving from near-white rates to significantly elevated rates in a single year. This increase is not explained by existing narratives about racial disparity and warrants immediate investigation; it does not appear in most public reporting on the 2024 data. Third, women aged 40 and over saw rates increase, not decrease, from 61.2 to 62.3. The reported national "decline" is carried almost entirely by improvements among Black and white women while other groups moved in the wrong direction.

Provisional 2024 data carries an additional caveat: the NCHS and leading researchers warn that late death records and shifts in inclusion guidelines routinely produce upward revisions of preliminary figures. 12-month rolling rates dropping to approximately 16.7 by September 2025 suggest a continued slight downward trend, but researchers caution these figures should be treated as estimates pending final reconciliation. The slight improvement in the aggregate gap masks the approaching data blackout: 2023 is the last year for which we will have reliable national data. The instrument that measured the damage was destroyed while the damage was accelerating.

Chapter 2: Healthcare Infrastructure — The Hospitals, the Workforce, the Clinics

What is left to provide the care.

A patient delivers in the wrong place. Not a planned home birth, not a birth center—she delivers somewhere that is not the labor and delivery unit because the system around her is breaking down in real time. The unit cannot admit her properly. The documentation system has no field for what just happened. A nurse cannot log into the computer because someone swapped her phone without updating her credentials. Another nurse prints lab labels on a printer that produces blank stickers—no ink—and walks the floor looking for one that works. They are seriously lacking in people. There is no provider on the unit until the assigned physician arrives. This is the second time recently that a patient has been walked up from the emergency department without proper triage or transfer, because the system does not have the staff or process to receive her correctly.

The team manages. They always manage. They manage the way understaffed clinical teams always manage—with ad hoc workarounds, texted favors, and improvised solutions. Every day I am surprised more accidents don't happen, because the healthcare system is kept running through sheer force of will and the ingenuity of charge nurses doing unpaid, unrecognized work to hold the patchwork together.

The absurdity of this environment is best captured by a single image: a junior resident, a grown physician with two decades of education and training, sitting at a counter-height desk in a well-resourced hospital. The chair he was given was broken; it couldn't rise to the height of the desk. He looked like a toddler trying to use an office desk to write his notes. That is modern medicine. We waste immense amounts of money on inefficiency, but no one takes a second to pause and say, this isn't working. Nobody wants to take the time to reorganize, so we just keep stacking patches on top of patches. Before you know it, the mess you've created is ten times worse than if you had just paused and fixed the root problem.

You see the strain in the operating room, too. A cesarean section on a woman with extensive adhesions—thick bands of scar tissue—from a prior surgery. The procedure is complicated. It requires blunt dissection through the scar tissue, electrocautery to control bleeding, and an O'Leary stitch (ligation of the uterine artery) to manage hemorrhage from uterine atony. The fascia is thickened. Space is insufficient. Additional adhesiolysis (breaking down more scar tissue) is required just to reach the uterus. The baby is delivered. The placenta is adherent. The uterus will not contract.

And through all of it, the anesthesia is inadequate. The patient feels the surgery. Not discomfort—actual pain. The spinal does not work well enough. The anesthesia team provides additional medication that offers "a moderate amount" of improvement. Local anesthetic is injected at the end. My primary postoperative concern isn't infection or surgical complications. It's pain control—because the infrastructure has failed to provide the most basic prerequisite for major abdominal surgery: the patient not feeling it.

These are not catastrophes. No one died. These are simply the baseline conditions of American obstetric care in a system built on workarounds.

Before

More than 330 labor and delivery units have closed in the United States since 2011.^1 The closures are concentrated in rural areas and low-income communities. Over one-third of all U.S. counties — 1,104 counties — are now maternity care deserts[^2]: counties with no hospital offering obstetric services, no birth center, and no OB-GYN. Women in these counties who go into labor drive two to four hours to deliver. Women in active labor, in a car, on a highway, for hours. Some of them do not make it. 2.3 million women of childbearing age and 150,000 babies born every year[^3] have essentially no local maternity care. Since 2010, over 500 hospitals have closed their OB units; 5.5 million women now live in counties with no or limited maternity care.[^4]

Maternity units close because they are the most expensive service line in a hospital: 24/7 coverage, specialized staff, high liability insurance costs, and equipment that sits idle between deliveries. Rural hospitals operating on thin margins cut the service that costs the most to maintain. The math is straightforward. The consequence is that an entire community loses not just delivery services but prenatal care, contraceptive counseling, STI screening, and cancer screening — because these services were typically co-located with the maternity unit. When the unit closes, the providers leave. Everything goes.

The pace of closures has been accelerating: 18 in 2020, 34 in 2023, 21 in 2024, and 27 in 2025 year-to-date — 116 or more rural labor and delivery unit closures since 2020 alone.^5 The scale of surviving infrastructure is equally alarming: of the nation's approximately 2,396 rural hospitals, only 950 — representing 41% — still offer labor and delivery services.^6 In twelve states, fewer than one-third of rural hospitals continue to provide maternity care. An additional 127 rural hospitals are currently designated "at risk"^7 due to negative total margins and sustained losses on patient services over the past two fiscal years. Rural facilities are uniquely vulnerable because they lack the alternative revenue streams available to large urban health systems, making 24/7 obstetric coverage financially untenable. Three forces are converging simultaneously on these hospitals: declining birth volumes, acute workforce shortages, and persistent financial insolvency — and there is no federal intervention on the horizon to address any of the three.

The geographic consequence for pregnant patients is direct and measurable. Approximately 70% of pregnant individuals in rural areas must now travel more than 30 minutes to reach a delivery facility^8; 20% must travel more than 50 minutes. These travel times are not inconveniences. In active labor, with a ruptured placenta, with a cord prolapse, with eclamptic seizures, transport time is the difference between life and death. Beyond the delivery crisis, these closures have produced a secondary catastrophe: the "deskilling" of rural emergency departments. When a hospital closes its maternity unit, the specialized staff leave. Over time, even the local emergency department loses the obstetric competency to stabilize a maternal emergency — meaning that when a crisis occurs, even the closest emergency room may no longer have the staff or equipment to keep a woman alive long enough to reach a facility that can treat her. The infrastructure collapse transforms manageable clinical emergencies into fatal ones.

The OB-GYN workforce shortage was already estimated at 8,000 physicians before the current crisis, projected to reach 22,000 by 2050.^9 Forty percent of practicing OB-GYNs are over 55 years old. Only 19% are under 40. Approximately 15,000 baby boomer obstetricians are approaching retirement. Training a replacement takes 12 years: four years of medical school, four years of residency, and the early-career years before full independent practice. There is no way to accelerate this. There is no shortcut. As early as 2004, active OB-GYN numbers were declining or failing to match population growth. By 2024, projections warned of a 5,000-physician deficit by 2030. The retirement wave was coming regardless of politics. Politics turned a slow-moving workforce crisis into an acute emergency. The nursing workforce is similarly strained: more than 193,000 registered nurse openings are projected annually[^10] through 2032.

Title X served 2.8 million patients at $102 per patient per year^11 — the most cost-efficient healthcare delivery in the federal government. Ten states still had not expanded Medicaid as of 2025: Alabama, Florida, Georgia, Kansas, Mississippi, South Carolina, Tennessee, Texas, Wisconsin, and Wyoming. This left 1.4 to 1.9 million adults in a coverage gap — earning too much for Medicaid, too little for ACA subsidies. The Hyde Amendment had banned federal Medicaid funds from covering abortion since 1976.

The Rural Geography of Death

The maternity care desert is not a metaphor. It is a map of who lives and who dies based on where they were born.

According to CDC National Center for Health Statistics data, rural counties have a maternal mortality rate of 25.9 per 100,000 live births — 42% higher than the 18.2 rate in large metropolitan counties.^12 The excess deaths are not mysterious. Hemorrhage and hypertensive emergencies account for approximately 60% of them^13, and both are conditions where minutes determine outcomes. Rural women are 65% more likely to die from postpartum hemorrhage^14 than their urban counterparts, driven by blood product access limitations — rural hospitals have limited blood bank capacity, smaller transfusion volumes that shorten shelf life, and no dedicated hemorrhage response teams — and by delayed recognition at low-volume facilities where staff see these emergencies too infrequently to respond with automaticity.

The workforce maldistribution is staggering. Only 4.1% of all OB-GYNs practice in rural areas, despite 10.2% of childbearing-age women living there.^15 Sixty-four percent of rural counties have no practicing OB-GYN at all. According to a KFF analysis of HRSA data, provider density in rural areas is one-third that of metropolitan counties — 13 OB-GYNs per 100,000 women compared to 41.^16 Over 10 million women live in counties with no OB-GYN whatsoever.^17 The HRSA Health Workforce Simulation Model projects rural workforce adequacy declining from 57.1% in 2025 to 51.4% by 2037^18, while metropolitan adequacy slides from 97.9% to 85.1%. The gap is widening, not closing.

Fifty-four percent of rural hospitals with obstetric units deliver fewer than 100 births per year^19 — below established quality-safety thresholds. Twenty-nine percent deliver fewer than 50. Low-volume units are associated with lower provider skill maintenance, higher cesarean rates, and worse outcomes when emergencies occur. This creates a lethal paradox: the rural hospitals that remain open are often too small to safely deliver babies, but closing them means no delivery facility at all for a hundred miles.

The measurable health consequences cascade beyond the delivery room. Research published in JAMA Network Open found that rural obstetric unit closure is associated with a 9% increase in preterm births and a 7% increase in low birthweight^20 in the surrounding county within two years — effects that persist for three years or more. Rural women are 2.3 times more likely to have an unplanned out-of-hospital birth[^21] than urban women. Unplanned out-of-hospital births carry four times the neonatal mortality of planned facility births. Rural infants have a 27% higher overall mortality rate — 6.45 per 1,000 live births compared to 5.06 in large metropolitan counties^22 — and the gap has been widening since 2010. Only 16.9% of rural birth hospitals offer higher-level neonatal care^23, compared to 74.4% of urban facilities. Fifty-five percent of rural counties have no NICU at any level. Rural premature infants face 25% longer transport times to reach intensive care.

The prenatal care pipeline is eroding in parallel. Rural women are 40% less likely to initiate prenatal care in the first trimester^24 compared to urban women, with the largest disparities concentrated in the rural South. Among rural Black women, first-trimester initiation drops to 52% — compared to 71% for rural white women. Among rural American Indian and Alaska Native women, it is 49%. Nationally, first-trimester prenatal care has declined from 78.3% in 2021 to 75.5% in 2024^25, while late or no prenatal care has risen from 6.3% to 7.3%. In maternity care deserts specifically, the inadequate prenatal care rate reaches 15.8%, and the rate of pre-pregnancy hypertension is 1.3 times higher than in full-access counties — meaning the communities with the least care also carry the highest risk.

The States Where Infrastructure Has Collapsed

The crisis is national, but its most severe expression is regional — concentrated in the South, the Plains, and Appalachia, where rural poverty, Medicaid non-expansion, and now abortion bans converge.

The March of Dimes identifies the states with the highest percentage of counties classified as maternity care deserts: North Dakota at 73.6%, South Dakota at 57.6%[^26], Oklahoma at 51.9%, Missouri at 51.6%, Nebraska at 51.3%, and Arkansas at 50.7%. In these states, half or more of all counties have no birthing facility and no obstetric clinician.

Alabama has undergone a four-decade collapse. In 1980, 45 of Alabama's 55 rural counties had obstetric services. By 2024, only 15 to 16 remain[^27] — a 64 to 67% decline. Lawrence Medical Center in Moulton ended emergency and inpatient services in February 2025. Thomasville Regional halted obstetric care in 2024. Twenty-eight additional rural hospitals remain at risk. The destruction is concentrated in the Black Belt counties, where the population is predominantly Black and the infrastructure was already threadbare.

Mississippi has a maternal mortality rate of 39.1 per 100,000 live births, with Black women dying at 4.5 times the rate of white women.^28 Nearly a quarter of Mississippi women live more than 30 minutes from an obstetric hospital. According to the Chartis Center for Rural Health, 47% of Mississippi's rural hospitals are vulnerable to closure.[^29] Georgia's pregnancy-related mortality rate reaches 37.9 per 100,000, with 87% of those deaths deemed preventable^30 — cardiovascular conditions, mental health crises, hemorrhage, embolism — all conditions treatable with timely access to care that does not exist.

Texas has more rural hospitals at highest closure risk than any state in the nation — 47 facilities. CapRock Hospital in Bryan closed in December 2024.[^31] Texas is a non-expansion state with the largest uninsured population in the country, and Hispanic and Black Texans are disproportionately served by the at-risk facilities. In Missouri, maternal mortality in maternity care desert counties is 36% higher^32 than in counties with full obstetric access.

The Chartis 2025 rural hospital vulnerability index identifies 432 rural hospitals at risk of closure^33 nationwide. Forty-six percent of all rural hospitals operate with negative margins.^34 In non-expansion states, the figure rises to 53%.^35 The states with the highest proportion of at-risk rural hospitals — Arkansas at 50%, Mississippi at 47%, Kansas at 47% — overlap almost perfectly with the states that have refused Medicaid expansion and, in most cases, banned abortion.

This is not coincidence. An analysis found that states with the combined burden of rural provider shortage, abortion restriction, and Medicaid non-expansion have 3.4 times higher preventable maternal mortality than states with none of these conditions.[^36] The effects are multiplicative. Each condition independently increases mortality; in combination, they create near-complete reproductive healthcare deserts where ectopic emergency care delays, untreated hypertension, and absent prenatal care drive preventable death. Rural Black and American Indian women bear the disproportionate burden — and the states with the highest minority populations are overrepresented in this triple-burden category.

The Emergency Surgery Gap

Thirty-two percent of rural counties lack a hospital with 24/7 emergency cesarean section capability^37 within 30 minutes of travel. This statistic is not about scheduling inconvenience. It is a clinical reality codified in obstetric guidelines: emergency cesarean section requires a decision-to-incision time of 30 minutes or less. When a placental abruption occurs, when a cord prolapse is diagnosed, when fetal heart tracings show acute distress — the clock starts. If there is no anesthesiologist in the building because the hospital cut staffing, if the nearest surgical team is 45 minutes away by ambulance, if the rural emergency department closed its obstetric unit and the remaining staff have not managed a crash cesarean in two years — women and babies die in that interval.

The CMS Rural Emergency Hospital designation, launched in January 2023, was designed to stabilize struggling rural hospitals by allowing them to convert to emergency-and-outpatient-only facilities with enhanced reimbursement — $285,626 per month in facility payments and 105% of outpatient payment rates. By early 2026, 42 to 44 hospitals across 18 to 20 states had converted.^38 But REH designation, by definition, eliminates inpatient services. It eliminates maternity care. Every REH conversion is another community where a pregnant woman in crisis has no local surgical option.

Trump 1.0

The Gag Rule stripped approximately 1,000 clinics from Title X.^39 Each closed clinic was a node in the safety net. When the node went dark, the women it served had nowhere to go — and the services they lost were not limited to contraception. Co-located prenatal care, cancer screening, and STI testing were gone with the clinic. From 2010 to 2018, Title X had already been cut by $31 million, resulting in 1.2 million fewer patients served. The Gag Rule finished the job. In rural areas, the damage was especially concentrated: 28% of rural Title X family planning sites closed or reduced services between 2019 and 2021, leaving 1.6 million rural patients without a primary family planning provider.[^40]

The Pandemic

Eighteen rural maternity units closed in 2020. The healthcare workforce hit a breaking point — burnout, moral injury, PTSD. Providers left the profession. The U.S. lost more than 100,000 registered nurses between 2020 and 2021^41 — the largest single-year decline on record. L&D units were particularly devastated because obstetric nursing requires specialized training that cannot be quickly backfilled. By 2022, 53% of OB-GYNs reported burnout[^42], a 13-percentage-point increase from pre-pandemic levels. Among OB-GYN residents, a national survey found 64.8% reporting burnout, 57.2% depression, and 70.9% anxiety. Rural hospitals, already operating on razor-thin margins, lost the financial reserves that might have sustained them through the next crisis. Nothing was replaced. No surge capacity. No rebuilding.

What Began to Recover

Biden restored Title X. Some clinics reopened. The overall pace of closures slowed temporarily. The policy infrastructure stabilized — but the structural forces driving closures (workforce aging, rural hospital financial fragility, malpractice costs) were not resolved.

Dobbs

Idaho lost 114 OB-GYNs[^43] — 43% of its workforce. Maternal-fetal medicine specialists went from 9 to fewer than 5. Complete labor and delivery unit closures followed. Idaho's Attorney General attempted to legally block doctors from even referring patients to out-of-state clinics.

Residency applications for OB-GYN programs dropped 10.5% across ban states^44 overall. Alabama saw a 21.2% plummet. Missouri: a 25% drop. Texas SB 8 triggered a 16% decline in applications to Texas programs alone, with the majority of OB-GYN residents considering leaving the state entirely. A Commonwealth Fund analysis found a 6.7% overall decrease in residency applicants to ban states^45 — trainees actively avoiding states where they cannot receive comprehensive clinical training in miscarriage management, ectopic pregnancy treatment, and emergency obstetric intervention. Sixty percent of physicians in total ban states considered leaving — 11% actually did.^46 Forty-two percent of abortion providers have relocated out of ban states. Practices that once had three or four doctors collapsed to one or zero. Retired physicians are being pulled out of retirement to staff hospitals that would otherwise close their maternity units entirely.

Sixty-one percent of OB-GYNs practicing in ban states report concerns about their legal risk.^47 The Health Resources and Services Administration projects the U.S. will meet only 82% of anticipated OB-GYN demand by 2037 — and that projection was made before accounting for the Dobbs exodus. Malpractice premiums increased 30-40% in ban states.^48 Twenty-eight percent of women of reproductive age with young children report difficulty accessing maternity care nationally; in states with abortion bans, the figure is 34%.

Closures accelerated: 34 in 2023, 21 in 2024, 27 in 2025 year-to-date — 116+ rural labor and delivery unit closures since 2020 alone. The following 2025 closures are documented and named:

Facility Location Primary Driver Cited
Greene County General Hospital Linton, IN Rising costs, underpayment
Centra Southside Community Farmville, VA Strategic transition
Faribault Birth Center (Allina) Faribault, MN Regional consolidation
Soin Medical Center (Kettering) Beavercreek, OH Decline in birth rates
UF Health Leesburg Hospital Leesburg, FL Broad alignment
Aspirus Ironwood Hospital Ironwood, MI Physician/nurse shortages
Mount Desert Island Hospital Bar Harbor, ME Dramatic decline in births
Waldo Hospital (MaineHealth) Belfast, ME Birth rate decline
Fairmont Unit (Mayo Clinic) Fairmont, MN Physician shortage

These are not abstractions. They are specific hospitals in specific communities where a woman can no longer deliver a baby without driving — in some cases for hours.

Physicians in ban states now write medical charts as legal defense briefs — documenting in statutory language why each intervention is required to prevent "substantial and irreversible impairment." If a patient presents bleeding after a self-managed abortion, clinical strategy dictates treating the symptoms as a spontaneous miscarriage, minimizing documented inquiry into the cause to prevent triggering mandatory reporting laws. This is not medicine. This is survival. The legal ambiguity — what counts as a "life-threatening" exception, when exactly does a physician have legal clearance to intervene — is not an abstract policy question. It is a real-time clinical calculation that physicians are making while a patient is hemorrhaging or septic on a gurney. In that ambiguity, women die.

The Travel Burden

Before Dobbs, the average driving distance to the nearest abortion facility was 46.8 miles. After Dobbs, in ban states, it rose to 279.7 miles — a six-fold increase.[^49] In 2024, 155,000 patients traveled across state lines[^50] for abortion care, nearly double the 81,000 pre-Dobbs baseline. Texas alone generated more than 28,000 interstate travelers. Illinois served as the top destination, absorbing 35,470 out-of-state patients from the South and Midwest.

The rural dimensions are worse. Post-Dobbs, rural women in ban states travel a median of 75 or more miles for abortion care.^51 One in five rural women lives more than 200 miles from the nearest provider. Rural Black women in ban states report inability to access abortion at nearly twice the rate of rural white women — 34% compared to 18%. For rural Latinas, the figure is 29%. These are women without public transportation, without paid leave, without childcare options — women for whom a 200-mile journey is not a road trip but an impossibility. Three-quarters of abortion patients are at or below 200% of the federal poverty level. The distance is the ban.

Meanwhile, a parallel geography of misinformation has emerged. Rural areas have 2.3 times more crisis pregnancy centers per reproductive-age woman[^52] than actual abortion providers. In state after state, CPCs cluster near the sites of closed abortion clinics — offering medically inaccurate information to women who have no alternative provider within reach. Thirty states fund these centers through "Alternatives to Abortion" programs.

The Midwifery Bottleneck

The midwifery model works. It produces equivalent or better outcomes for low-risk pregnancies at lower cost. Rural Federally Qualified Health Centers with integrated certified nurse-midwives show 40% higher prenatal care initiation rates and 28% better birth outcomes^53 than rural counties without CNM-integrated care. Midwives are the most scalable answer to the OB-GYN shortage in rural America.

But 23 states have policies restricting midwives from practicing to the full extent of their training — scope-of-practice restrictions requiring physician supervision or collaborative agreements that are impossible to establish in counties where there is no physician to collaborate with. The restriction creates a circular trap: the midwife cannot practice without a supervising physician, but the supervising physician has already left or retired, so the community gets no provider at all.

Progress is slow but measurable. Twenty-six states and the District of Columbia now allow independent CNM practice^54, up from 24 in 2023, with Wisconsin, Illinois, Florida, and North Carolina expanding scope between 2023 and 2025. But every year of delay in the remaining 23 states is another year in which rural communities that could have midwifery care go without any care.

Home births have increased from 29,592 in 2016 to 46,918 in 2023.^55 Black women's home births tripled: from 823 to 2,733. This is not a lifestyle choice. It is a response to a failing hospital system — hospitals closing, providers leaving, and a system where Black women are three and a half times more likely to die. Expansion of midwifery is undermined by scope-of-practice restrictions, insurance barriers, and Medicaid reimbursement at a punitive 50-85% of hospital rates.

The Medicaid Architecture

Medicaid finances 47% of all births in rural areas^56 — nearly half. In maternity care deserts, Medicaid covers close to 50% of all births compared to 40.1% in full-access counties. This means that any federal Medicaid spending cut hits rural maternity care first and hardest.

The data on Medicaid expansion and hospital survival is unambiguous. Approximately 69% of rural hospital closures between 2014 and 2024 occurred in states that had not expanded Medicaid^57 at the time of closure. An analysis from the Chartis Center for Rural Health found that critical access hospitals in non-expansion states operate on margins 40% thinner^58 than their peers in expansion states. ACA Medicaid expansion reduced rural uninsured rates by 41% in expansion states compared to just 15% in non-expansion states, with rural reproductive-age women experiencing the greatest coverage gain.

Federally Qualified Health Centers have become the critical safety net. FQHCs served 4.7 million rural patients for reproductive healthcare in 2022.^59 Thirty percent of rural Americans depend on FQHCs as their primary reproductive health access point. FQHCs provided prenatal care to 566,000 patients and care for one in six U.S. births. They accept Medicaid at roughly 90%, compared to 77% for private OB-GYN practices. They are the last line.

During the 2023 Medicaid unwinding, rural states had 22% higher disenrollment rates^60 than urban areas, with reproductive-age women accounting for 31% of all disenrollments — lost to paperwork failures at understaffed rural Medicaid offices, mail renewal failures at rural addresses, and a digital divide that limited online renewal. Four-point-six million children lost Medicaid coverage, 70% due to paperwork alone.^61

Trump 2.0

Title X frozen — $65.8 million, as of March 31, 2025.^62 The Office of Population Affairs went from approximately 50 staff to one officer; 30 received RIF notices on October 10, 2025. 879 clinics across 23 states have been affected. 834,000 women are projected to lose access.[^63] Planned Parenthood defunded from Medicaid in 39 states, effective July 2025 — $45 million per month in unreimbursed care. Twenty-three Planned Parenthood clinics have closed since July 2025; 32 more closures announced across 11 states. 16 of 86 Title X family planning grantees have had funding frozen. In many of these communities, there is no replacement program and no other provider.

HHS reduced from 82,000 to 62,000 employees. Over 4,800 federal health workers fired.^64 The Indian Health Service targeted for $900 million in cuts; 12 IHS facilities closed since March 2025 — removing healthcare infrastructure for Native American communities where maternal mortality is 4.9 times the white rate.^65 American Indian and Alaska Native women experience rural maternal mortality rates 3.2 times higher than rural white women and twice the rate of urban AI/AN women — driven by IHS chronic underfunding, geographic isolation on reservations, and historical trauma. In Alaska, the median distance to obstetric care is 2.5 hours. The only federal healthcare system serving these communities is being dismantled.

On July 4, 2025, the One Big Beautiful Bill Act was signed into law, projecting $900 billion in Medicaid and CHIP cuts over ten years.^66 The CBO projects 10.5 million coverage losses. New caps on state-directed payments will limit the supplemental funding that has kept many rural hospitals solvent. Medicaid work requirements of 80 hours per month take effect January 1, 2027 — imposing administrative barriers on the same rural, low-income populations that already face the most severe healthcare access gaps. For the 432 rural hospitals already vulnerable to closure, for the 46% of rural hospitals already operating with negative margins, this legislation does not merely fail to solve the crisis. It accelerates it.

Meanwhile, enhanced ACA premium tax credits expired on December 31, 2025. An estimated 3.8 million people are projected to become newly uninsured in 2026. Twenty-two million marketplace enrollees face an average 114% premium increase.^67 The national uninsured rate, currently 8%, will climb. The coverage losses compound every structural failure documented in this chapter.

Private equity consolidation of hospital systems produces its own casualties. When PE firms acquire and consolidate hospitals, they reduce staffing. In obstetric emergencies — emergency cesarean sections — the decision-to-incision time is a critical safety metric. When there is no anesthesiologist in the building because the hospital cut staffing to improve margins, that time increases. Women and babies die in that interval.

The Parallel Collapse: Behavioral Health Workforce

The healthcare infrastructure crisis extends beyond obstetric providers. The behavioral health workforce — the psychiatrists, psychologists, addiction counselors, and social workers who treat the conditions that are now the leading medical killers of pregnant women — is collapsing in parallel.

There are fewer than 500 reproductive psychiatrists in the United States^68 for approximately 3.6 million births per year. Perinatal mood and anxiety disorders affect 1 in 5 women, yet 75% receive no treatment. Maternal suicide is now the leading cause of death in the first year postpartum. Ninety-six percent of birthing-aged women live in maternal mental health professional shortage areas. Fifty-eight percent of rural counties have no licensed perinatal mental health specialist whatsoever, and rural women are 42% more likely to experience untreated perinatal depression than their urban counterparts. By 2025, approximately 150 "Dark Zone" counties have been identified^69 — counties with both high maternal mental health risk and severe provider shortage. The number of severe-risk counties has tripled, from 24 to 92. Ninety-three percent of behavioral health professionals reported burnout in a 2023 workforce survey, with 33% annual clinician turnover during the pandemic period.

The addiction treatment workforce faces a projected shortfall of 77,050 counselors by 2038.^70 Overdose is the leading medical cause of maternal death. Rural opioid-involved maternal mortality is 43% higher than urban^71, and rural pregnant women with opioid use disorder are 28% less likely to receive medication-assisted treatment — blocked by geographic barriers to addiction treatment, limited buprenorphine-waivered providers, transportation barriers to methadone clinics that require daily in-person dosing, and rural stigma that punishes help-seeking. SAMHSA grants — $2 billion in addiction treatment and mental health funding — were terminated. The programs that train and deploy the providers who treat pregnant women with substance use disorders are being defunded at the exact moment when fentanyl-xylazine is producing the worst overdose crisis in American history.

The cascading effect is compounding: when a rural hospital closes its maternity unit, the co-located behavioral health services disappear too. The woman who needs both prenatal care and addiction treatment now has nowhere to go for either. The OB-GYN shortage and the behavioral health shortage are not two separate crises. They are the same crisis — a systematic withdrawal of the workforce that keeps pregnant women alive — playing out across two interlinked clinical domains.
