Chapter 6: Perinatal Mental Health

The Crisis No One Built Infrastructure For

She had figured she miscarried over the weekend. Her stomach started looking different. She came in. She drove herself. Her family was in Charlotte. She was alone.

She was young. Second miscarriage — both early, both around seven weeks. She had been throwing up every thirty minutes for three or four weeks, couldn't eat, had been to the emergency room multiple times. They gave her fluids each time and sent her home. They prescribed an antiemetic she never picked up. Nobody followed up.

The physician walked in and saw a woman who looked, in her clinical assessment, "very sad." Not sad the way people are sad after a loss. Sad in a way that makes a physician concerned. The patient had been put in foster care as a child. Her siblings were separated. Her mother's home was not safe — her younger siblings were "too bad." Her partner of four or five years had left when he found out about the pregnancy. He didn't want it. She had been raped before. She had relocated to a new town where she knew almost no one. She was living alone in a place where the toilet would not flush. She was using trash bags. She drove for Amazon and had been out of work for weeks.

The ultrasound showed retained tissue. Her white count was elevated — 12,000. The differential diagnosis was infection versus the body's normal response to loss. But the physician's primary concern was not the uterus. It was the patient's mental state.

"Tell me about this feeling of being out of it," the physician said. Then: "Who is around you that you trust?"

The physician called the patient's mother on speaker. She called her "princess." Her mother offered what she could — "You're welcome to come here" — but everyone in the room understood it was not a real option.

"I can't stay here too long," the patient said. "I still have bills to pay."

"Are you able to work in this condition?" the physician asked.

Silence.

"My friend, that's the depression talking," the physician said. "We haven't even tried anything."

She stayed with her for over an hour. She explained the difference between sadness and depression — that sadness is the appropriate response to loss, but depression changes brain chemistry in ways that willpower cannot reverse. That it is no different from telling someone to concentrate really hard on curing their infection. "You would say, 'You are a bad doctor. Give me some antibiotics.' It's the same thing." She admitted the patient for stabilization. She coordinated a psychiatric evaluation. She tried to find resources for housing, for safety, for the basic scaffolding of a life that was collapsing in real time.

Seventy-eight minutes. One patient. One visit. And the system had nothing waiting for either of them on the other side of the hospital door.

Mental health conditions account for approximately 23 percent of all pregnancy-related deaths in the United States.[^1] That figure — nearly one in four — makes mental health the single largest clinical category of pregnancy-related mortality, exceeding hemorrhage, hypertension, and infection individually. According to CDC Maternal Mortality Review Committee data, more than 80 percent of these deaths are judged preventable.[^2] The women who die from this do not die in delivery rooms. They die in the months afterward, in a window the system was specifically designed not to cover.

Sixty-two percent of all pregnancy-related suicides occur between 43 and 365 days postpartum^3 — precisely the period that, until recently, fell outside the 60-day Medicaid postpartum coverage window that governed healthcare access for over 40 percent of all U.S. births. Suicide is now the leading single cause of death in the postpartum year, outpacing every obstetric complication. The insurance expired while the crisis peaked. That was not a policy oversight. It was a policy execution.

The geography of maternal suicide mirrors the geography of abandonment. Pregnancy-associated suicide rates vary four-fold across states: from 2.1 per 100,000 live births in Massachusetts to 8.7 per 100,000 in Wyoming.[^4] States without Medicaid expansion have maternal suicide rates 2.3 times higher than expansion states.[^5] The highest rates cluster in Wyoming, Alaska, and Montana — rural states with no perinatal psychiatry infrastructure and geographic isolation that makes the nearest mental health provider a three-hour drive. The lowest rates anchor in Massachusetts and Connecticut — states with integrated perinatal systems, universal Medicaid, and the PPAP model Massachusetts pioneered.

The Doubling

In 2010, 9.4 percent of women were diagnosed with postpartum depression at delivery. By 2021, that number had nearly doubled to 19 percent^6 — roughly one in five mothers leaving the hospital with a diagnosis. Among non-Hispanic Black women, the increase was 140 percent over the same period^7 — the steepest racial slope in the data. Today, approximately 20 percent of all new mothers nationally[^8] meet criteria for postpartum depression, with rates reaching 35 percent among Medicaid-enrolled women.

The COVID-19 pandemic turned a building crisis into a conflagration. Perinatal depression symptoms among pregnant and postpartum women surged from 15 percent to 41 percent[^9] — a 173 percent increase. Anxiety symptoms rose from 29 percent to 72 percent.[^10] Nearly three-quarters of pregnant and postpartum women were experiencing significant anxiety during the pandemic. Among Black women, pandemic-era perinatal mood disorder rates reached 49 percent.[^11] Among Hispanic women, 47 percent.[^12] Essential worker status meant higher exposure trauma combined with continued work obligation. NICU visitor bans created maternal isolation trauma that disproportionately impacted Black mothers, who already carry elevated preterm birth rates.

The pandemic peak was supposed to be transient. It was not. Perinatal depression and anxiety rates in 2023–2024 remained 30 to 40 percent above pre-pandemic baselines[^13], driven by the collapse of informal childcare networks — grandparent COVID deaths decimated Black and Latino multigenerational childcare systems — the permanent loss of employer-based childcare, and housing costs that made single-income parenting unviable. An estimated 1.2 million mothers reduced work hours for childcare in 2023 alone[^14], each instance an independent risk factor for perinatal mood disorder. The psychiatric infrastructure to treat them did not exist.

Before the pandemic, the United States had approximately two inpatient perinatal psychiatric units for the entire country[^15] — for 4 million annual births. The United Kingdom, with fewer than 700,000 annual births, had 22 mother-baby units. State psychiatric bed capacity declined 8 percent between 2016 and 2023, to 10.8 beds per 100,000 people.[^16] Fewer than 250 board-certified reproductive psychiatrists now serve 3.7 million annual births[^17] — down from an already inadequate 500. Psychiatric residency programs include an average of 2.4 hours of perinatal psychiatry content across four years of training.[^18] Twelve fellowship programs exist nationwide.[^19] The median wait for consultation without a PPAP is 47 days[^20] — in a clinical context where postpartum psychosis can become lethal in hours. Seventy-five percent of affected women went untreated[^21] — not because they didn't need care, but because the care did not exist.

Beyond Depression: The Anxiety and PTSD That No One Screens For

Postpartum depression dominates the public conversation, but the clinical reality is broader and more dangerous than the narrative suggests.

Perinatal anxiety disorders affect 18 percent of pregnant women and 17 percent of postpartum women.[^22] They co-occur with depression at rates of 50 to 60 percent.[^23] Yet perinatal anxiety screening rates run 30 to 40 percent lower than depression screening nationally.[^24] The Edinburgh Postnatal Depression Scale — the most widely used screening tool — contains an anxiety subscale in items 3, 4, and 5, but clinicians do not always score it separately. The result is that the most common perinatal mental health condition after depression is systematically under-detected. ACOG updated its clinical practice guidelines in 2024 to recommend screening for both perinatal depression and anxiety at every prenatal visit and at the 4-to-6-week postpartum visit^25, expanding the prior recommendation of a single postpartum screen. Twelve months post-publication, an estimated 35 percent of practices remain non-compliant with the new guideline.^26

Perinatal PTSD is the condition the system is least equipped to see. It affects 3 to 9 percent of postpartum women overall, but the rate reaches 18 percent among Black women with histories of intimate partner violence.[^27] Among all deliveries, 4 percent produce PTSD from the birth experience itself[^28] — emergency cesarean sections, perinatal loss, NICU admissions, and what the clinical literature now terms obstetric violence: unconsented procedures, dismissal of pain, provider disrespect during labor. Sixty-eight percent of pregnant women with opioid use disorder carry documented PTSD[^29], their addiction and their trauma sharing the same upstream pipeline. Only 22 percent of women with perinatal PTSD receive PTSD-specific treatment.[^30] The condition is frequently misdiagnosed as postpartum depression because OB settings do not routinely screen for PTSD, and the symptom overlap — insomnia, emotional numbing, difficulty bonding with the infant — makes misclassification easy.

This matters clinically because the treatments are different. A woman with PTSD who is prescribed an SSRI for presumed depression will receive partial symptom relief at best. She needs trauma-focused therapy — and the infrastructure for that in the perinatal period does not exist at scale.

The Screening-to-Treatment Chasm

Screening has improved dramatically. National perinatal depression screening rates reached 72 percent of prenatal visits and 58 percent of postpartum visits in 2024, up from 42 percent and 28 percent in 2019.[^31] Thirty-two states now mandate perinatal screening by law.[^32] The EPDS has been validated in 11 languages for U.S. clinical use and demonstrates 86 percent sensitivity at the standard cutoff of 10 or above.[^33]

But screening without treatment is a diagnostic exercise with no clinical endpoint. Treatment initiation among women who screen positive remains below 30 percent.[^34] The screen-to-care boundary is where the system fails — a positive result meets a referral void. Black women are screened at near-equivalent rates to White women (70 versus 73 percent)[^35], but they are three times more likely to screen positive and not receive follow-up.[^36] Hispanic women are screened at 64 percent.[^37] American Indian and Alaska Native women — the population with the highest pregnancy-associated suicide and overdose rates in the country — are screened at only 51 percent.[^38]

The EPDS carries a structural bias. Validated primarily in White, English-speaking populations, its sensitivity drops to 74 percent in Asian American women and 70 percent in recent African immigrant women.[^39] For Spanish-speaking Latina perinatal women, care access runs 38 percent lower than English-speaking peers[^40] — not because the EPDS lacks a validated Spanish version (it has one, with 85 percent sensitivity)[^41], but because 62 percent of perinatal mental health providers lack Spanish-speaking staff or certified interpretation.[^42] A promotora community health worker model reaches three times more Spanish-speaking women than clinic-based outreach[^43], but these programs remain exceptions, not infrastructure.

The treatment chasm is widened by insurance barriers that function as policy choices. Despite the Mental Health Parity and Addiction Equity Act, perinatal mental health services face prior authorization denial rates three times higher than obstetric services.[^44] Commercial insurers deny perinatal psychiatry claims at 18 percent versus 6 percent for other OB services.[^45] A 2024 Department of Labor audit found non-quantitative treatment limitation violations in 34 percent of commercial plans[^46] — meaning a third of insurers were breaking the law, and the treatment was still being denied.

The Racial Architecture

Black women experience postpartum depression at rates 38 percent higher than White women[^47], are 40 percent less likely to receive a formal diagnosis, and initiate pharmacotherapy at less than half the rate of their White peers.[^48] In rural settings, Black women's PPD rates run 80 percent higher than those of their White rural counterparts.[^49] Only 37 percent of Black and Hispanic women receive any mental health treatment for perinatal conditions, compared to 67 percent of White women.[^50] Indigenous women experience depression 87 percent more often than White women and carry a lifetime sexual violence prevalence of 56.1 percent^51 — a rate of traumatic exposure that directly feeds the perinatal mental health crisis through the ACEs pipeline documented below.

American Indian and Alaska Native women carry the most catastrophic outcomes in the data. Their pregnancy-associated suicide and overdose rates are 4.2 times higher than White women's.[^52] The Indian Health Service — a federal treaty obligation — operates at mental health staffing 40 percent below recommended, with per-capita mental health spending of $283 versus $1,491 in non-IHS systems.[^53] Sixty-eight percent of AIAN women report unmet perinatal mental health needs.[^54]

This is not a failure of individual women to seek help. It is a system that does not employ providers who look like the patients, does not fund treatment in the geographies where the need is greatest, and does not screen with culturally appropriate instruments. The treatment gap persists at every income level — racial disparities in perinatal mental health outcomes remain statistically significant even after adjusting for socioeconomic status, indicating racism as an independent driver beyond poverty.

The ACEs Pipeline: How Childhood Trauma Becomes Maternal Death

The pipeline from childhood trauma to maternal mortality has been quantified at every link.

At least 1 in 4 girls in the United States experiences sexual abuse before age 18.^55 Among American Indian and Alaska Native women, 56.1 percent report lifetime contact sexual violence. Across all women, 26.8 percent — more than 33 million — report completed or attempted rape.^56 This is the first input.

The biology of what follows is dosed and measurable. An ACE score of 4 or more produces a 7-times elevation in alcoholism risk, an 11-times elevation in intravenous drug use, and a nearly 3-times elevation in depression.^57 At an ACE score of 6, life expectancy shortens by 20 years.^58

In pregnancy, 4 or more ACEs produce an odds ratio of 3.06 for preeclampsia.^59 Every ACE beyond zero increases preterm delivery risk by 13 percent.^60 Any ACEs increase antenatal depression risk by an odds ratio of 1.59, low birth weight by 1.27, gestational diabetes by 1.39.^61 One in five American adults carries 4 or more ACEs. Among AIAN adults, 32.4 percent. Among Black adults, 24 percent. Among adults earning under $15,000, 24.1 percent.^62

The woman who arrives at her first prenatal appointment carrying 4+ ACEs is arriving with a pregnancy already marked by the accumulated biology of her history. When clinicians treat her hypertension or her gestational diabetes in isolation from that history, they treat the symptom while leaving the cause untouched.

The pipeline closes through three parallel mechanisms: ACEs produce chronic disease, which produces high-risk pregnancy and elevated maternal mortality. ACEs produce untreated mental health conditions, which produce maternal suicide and impaired bonding that accumulates new ACEs in the next generation. ACEs produce substance use disorder, which produces overdose death and the family separation pipeline documented in this report's chapters on substance use and incarceration. The loop is intergenerational.

Where Mental Health Meets Substance Use: The Dual Crisis

Seventy-three percent of women with opioid use disorder in pregnancy have co-occurring depression or anxiety.[^63] Sixty-eight percent carry documented PTSD. These conditions share neurobiological pathways and an upstream cause in the ACEs pipeline. Yet the treatment system operates as if they were different diseases treated by different providers in different buildings.

Integrated programs that co-locate medication-assisted treatment with mental health services, peer support, and social work show 45 percent better treatment retention and dramatically lower maternal mortality — 0.8 per 1,000 compared to 2.4 per 1,000 in siloed care.[^64] The mechanism is straightforward: siloed care creates a dropout cascade where mental health treatment dropout leads to MAT dropout leads to relapse leads to death. Integration removes the fragmented care navigation burden from the patients least equipped to carry it.

Yet pregnant women face reduced access to the treatment that exists. Buprenorphine appointment access for pregnant women stands at 61.4 percent, compared to 73.9 percent for non-pregnant women.^65 Ninety percent of specialty addiction care is concentrated in urban areas^66, leaving rural pregnant women structurally excluded. The substance abuse counselors who staff these programs earn an average of $34,000 per year[^67] — poverty wages for work that prevents downstream death.

The Poverty Gradient

Poverty is the single strongest social predictor of perinatal mood and anxiety disorder onset and severity. Women with household incomes below 200 percent of the federal poverty level experience postpartum depression at 28 percent — nearly double the 15 percent rate for women above 400 percent FPL.[^68] Among women who were poor before birth, 68 percent remain in poverty afterward.[^69]

The social determinants are additive. Housing instability elevates perinatal mood disorder risk by 45 percent. Food insecurity by 52 percent. Absence of childcare by 40 percent. Social isolation by 55 percent.[^70] When three or more co-occur — and one in three perinatal women reports two or more concurrent adversities — the cumulative risk is 3.2 times baseline.[^71] These adversities fall disproportionately on Black, Hispanic, and AIAN women, but racial disparities persist at equivalent income levels, confirming that racism operates as an independent driver beyond socioeconomic status.

What Was Built, What Is Being Destroyed

All fifty states plus the District of Columbia have now implemented 12-month postpartum Medicaid coverage — a historic expansion, created by the American Rescue Plan Act of 2021, made permanent by the Consolidated Appropriations Act of 2023, and fully operational across every state as of January 2026. An estimated 1.7 million additional postpartum women per year[^72] are now eligible for extended mental health coverage beyond the old 60-day cliff. For the first time, the coverage window matches the clinical risk window.

Perinatal Psychiatry Access Programs now operate in 31 states, providing real-time telephone consultation to OB providers who lack perinatal psychiatry expertise. Where PPAPs exist, median wait times for perinatal psychiatry consultation drop from 47 days to 2.4 days.[^73] These programs function as a workforce multiplier — enabling primary OB providers to manage mild-to-moderate perinatal mood disorders without a specialist referral that would take weeks. But 40 percent of high-need rural counties remain without PPAP coverage.[^74]

Telehealth now accounts for 47 percent of all perinatal mental health visits nationally, following permanent Medicare and Medicaid telehealth reauthorization. No-show rates dropped 31 percent compared to in-person visits. But broadband infrastructure gaps reproduce geographic inequity even when policy is equitable: rural telehealth utilization stands at 28 percent versus 54 percent urban[^75], and AIAN women on tribal lands have the lowest telehealth utilization of any group because the broadband does not exist.

The 988 Suicide and Crisis Lifeline has experienced an 80 percent surge in demand since its launch, with monthly contacts growing from 355,000 to more than 654,000. Text message volume has increased more than 11-fold^76, reflecting shifting communication preferences among younger populations — and accessibility of texting in unsafe or private situations. The National Maternal Mental Health Hotline (1-833-TLC-MAMA), launched in 2022 as the first dedicated federal line for perinatal mental health crises, is under threat from HHS staffing reductions.

The proposed Medicaid cuts now law through the One Big Beautiful Bill Act — $880 billion over 10 years — would undo the coverage that took a generation to build. The Medicaid work requirements taking effect in January 2027 will strip coverage from millions of women of reproductive age. The 12-month postpartum extension is meaningless if the woman loses her Medicaid eligibility before she becomes pregnant.

Dobbs is not merely a reproductive rights issue — it is a psychiatric event. Women in states with abortion restrictions show depression and anxiety rates 18 percent higher[^77] than women in non-restriction states. Thirty-nine percent of OB/GYN providers in ban states report inability to provide standard mental health referrals due to legal uncertainty.[^78] Twenty-five percent of maternal-fetal medicine specialists have left ban states, reducing perinatal psychiatry consultation availability in precisely the states adding the greatest psychiatric burden.[^79] Adolescent mothers — who experience postpartum depression at 2.5 times the rate of adult mothers, with only 19 percent initiating treatment[^80] — are increasing in high-restriction states where forced parenthood at an adolescent developmental stage has independent psychiatric consequences.

The FDA approved zuranolone (Zurzuvae) in August 2023 as the first oral treatment specifically for postpartum depression — producing 50 percent remission by day 15, compared to 30 percent for placebo.[^81] But two years after approval, fewer than 25,000 patients have received it. The $15,900 list price per 14-day course and Medicaid non-preferred formulary status in 32 states[^82] have created a two-tier system: commercially insured White women hold the majority of prescriptions, while Medicaid-enrolled Black, Hispanic, and low-income women face near-total exclusion.

The Bipartisan Safer Communities Act grants that funded mental health staff in K-12 schools — the front-line identification system for children accumulating the ACEs that will become the next generation's maternal deaths — were rescinded in April 2025. The school counselor-to-student ratio nationally sits between 385:1 and 408:1 — more than 50 percent above the recommended maximum of 250:1.[^83] HRSA projects a combined behavioral health workforce deficit exceeding 277,000 clinicians by 2038: 77,050 addiction counselors, 99,780 mental health counselors, and 99,840 psychologists short.[^84] The workforce that would deliver treatment is in structural free fall.

The administration's actions form a coherent pattern: expand the conditions that produce perinatal mental illness (ban abortion, cut safety net, reduce housing and nutrition programs, escalate immigration enforcement that suppresses care-seeking) while simultaneously reducing the capacity to treat it (cut behavioral health workforce funding, rescind school mental health grants, threaten the hotline, defund SAMHSA, maintain parity law violations without enforcement). The result is predictable, dosed, and measurable. It will be counted in maternal deaths — if anyone is left to count them.

Chapter 7: Pregnancy Behind Bars

Consider two possible endings for the patient in the preceding chapter — the pregnant woman whose partner loaded a gun on her, whose partner jumped her, whose partner burned their ultrasound pictures.

In the first ending, she follows the physician's safety plan. She hides her documents. She leaves the area. She delivers her baby somewhere else. She survives.

In the second ending, he escalates. She defends herself. She uses a weapon — the same weapon he loaded on her. He is injured or killed. She is arrested. She is charged with assault or homicide. She is pregnant at the time of her arrest. She enters the correctional system.

In the second ending, the system does not see a victim. It sees a perpetrator. She enters a facility where there is a 62.3 percent chance [^1] she will not be pregnancy-tested. Where she may labor in shackles. Where her baby will be separated from her within 72 hours. [^2] Where, if her sentence exceeds 15 months, [^3] the Adoption and Safe Families Act will begin the clock toward permanent termination of her parental rights — and she will lose the child she carried through the violence.

Ninety-four percent of incarcerated women have experienced domestic violence. [^4] Seventy-four percent of women imprisoned for murder or manslaughter had experienced intimate partner violence within a year of the offense that led to their conviction. [^4] The system that failed to protect them then punishes them for surviving.

The System Before Dobbs

Between 1980 and 2023, the number of incarcerated women in the United States grew by more than 600 percent — from 26,326 to 186,244 — a rate of growth twice that of men. [^5] The drivers were the War on Drugs, mandatory minimum sentencing, and the criminalization of poverty. Women in state prisons are incarcerated for drug or property offenses at rates substantially higher than men — the proportion convicted of drug offenses rose from 12 percent in 1986 to 25 percent by 2021. [^6] Sixty percent of incarcerated women lived below the federal poverty line before arrest. [^5] By 2023, over one million women were under some form of criminal justice supervision — prison, jail, probation, or parole — and the female incarcerated population rebounded 22 percent in a single post-pandemic year. [^7]

This growth was a racial project. Between 1986 and 1991 — a single five-year period — incarceration of Black women in state prisons for drug offenses surged 828 percent. [^6] Despite a 69 percent decline in Black women's imprisonment rate since 2000, Black women remain imprisoned at 68 per 100,000 — 1.7 times the white women's rate of 41 per 100,000. [^6] In the same period, white women's imprisonment rate actually rose 18 percent, [^6] narrowing the disparity ratio while disguising the underlying expansion of the carceral apparatus into all women's lives. Latina women are imprisoned at 51 per 100,000, 1.2 times the white rate. [^6] American Indian and Alaska Native women experience an incarceration rate 4.3 times higher than white women — the highest disparity of any group [^8] — driven by the intersection of federal jurisdiction on tribal lands, chronic underfunding of Indian Health Service, and historical trauma.

The geography matters as much as the demography. Idaho imprisons women at 152 per 100,000 — the highest rate in the nation. Rhode Island imprisons women at 6 per 100,000 — the lowest. [^9] That is a twenty-five-fold variation. [^9] A pregnant woman's exposure to every harm documented in this chapter — prenatal care gaps, shackling, postpartum separation, Medicaid disruption, and family severance — is determined not by clinical need or individual risk but by which side of a state line she was standing on when she was arrested.

The Abuse-to-Incarceration Pipeline

Over 80 percent of incarcerated women have histories of physical or sexual abuse [^9] — many are imprisoned for defending themselves against their abusers. The abuse routinely continues in custody. The December 2024 permanent closure of FCI Dublin—following a systemic sexual abuse scandal resulting in a $116 million settlement for over 100 survivors and a February 2025 consent decree mandating independent monitoring—demonstrates that facilities themselves function as sites of continuous victimization [^33]. The cases of Cyntoia Brown-Long and Marissa Alexander are the ones that became nationally visible, but the structural pattern they represent is routine: self-defense standards built around a male model of combat — the requirements of imminent threat and proportional force — systematically fail women who use a weapon against an unarmed attacker twice their size, or who strike preemptively after years of escalating violence. Black women are disproportionately arrested in domestic violence incidents even when they are the victims, and receive longer sentences than white women in comparable self-defense cases. Stand Your Ground laws, written to protect the person with the gun in the confrontation, have not functioned equivalently for Black women.

The pipeline begins even earlier. Black girls are 14 percent of the female youth population but 39 percent of girls in juvenile residential facilities. [^10] Native girls are confined at 39 per 100,000 — four times the rate of white girls. Black girls are confined at 29 per 100,000 — three times the rate. [^11] Between 40 and 81 percent of justice-involved girls report histories of sexual abuse, compared to 20 percent in the general population, [^10] and girls are nearly twice as likely as boys to report five or more distinct forms of abuse. [^10] Forty percent of youth placed for status offenses — truancy, curfew violations, running away — are girls, despite representing only 15 percent of the total incarcerated youth population. [^11] The pipeline is widening: girls' share of all youth arrests rose from 19 percent in 1985 to 31 percent by 2024 [^34]. These offenses are survival behaviors. Running away from a home where you are being raped is not a criminal act. But it is treated as one, and it is the first contact point in a system that will follow these girls into adult incarceration, adult pregnancy, and adult loss.

The Pregnancy Testing Gap and the Data Void

Only 37.7 percent of U.S. jails conduct pregnancy tests upon entry — a number that comes from the only national-scale jail dataset in the field, the 2016–2017 Johns Hopkins Pregnancy in Prison Statistics study. [^1] Most facilities rely on self-reporting, which misses pregnancies where women don't yet know they're pregnant, fear disclosure due to potential loss of autonomy, or don't trust correctional healthcare providers. Pregnancies are routinely discovered late, missing critical windows for prenatal care initiation and, where legal, abortion access.

The federal picture is marginally better but appallingly recent. The first comprehensive federal data on pregnancy in prisons — the BJS Maternal Health Supplement — was not collected until 2024 and was published in a landmark 2025 report covering calendar year 2023. In the 47 jurisdictions reporting, 88 percent of female admissions were tested for pregnancy, and 2 percent tested positive. [^12] On a single day — December 31, 2023 — 328 pregnant women were in state or federal prisons, representing 0.5 percent of the female prison population. [^13] Approximately 58,000 pregnant people enter U.S. jails and prisons annually. [^14] That the federal government did not begin counting them until 2024 is not an oversight. It is a design feature of a system that does not want to be measured.

The BJS data recorded 727 pregnancy outcomes across 49 jurisdictions in 2023: 665 live births (91.5 percent), 47 miscarriages (6.5 percent), and 15 abortions (2.1 percent). [^15] These numbers are a beginning, but they are not accountability. The BJS report excludes jails, private facilities, and the metrics that would actually permit evaluation of care quality — preterm birth rates, low birth weight, NICU admissions, and maternal mortality. The 2025 BJS report itself omitted jails, private facilities, and critical metrics [^35]. A 2024 Government Accountability Office review concluded that comprehensive national data on pregnancy outcomes and care quality in correctional facilities does not exist. [^16] Without it, tracking maternal or infant mortality in custody, identifying systemic failures in obstetric care, or comparing outcomes across jurisdictions is structurally impossible. The data gap itself prevents racial disaggregation — you cannot document a disparity you refuse to measure.

Among the pregnant women whose race was recorded in the 2023 BJS data, 60 percent were white, 20 percent were Black, 9 percent were Hispanic, and 4 percent were American Indian or Alaska Native. [^14] ACOG's Committee Opinion 830 states that incarcerated pregnant people must receive care equivalent to non-incarcerated patients [^17] — comprehensive prenatal care, high-risk screening, twenty-four-hour emergency access, breastfeeding support. These are professional recommendations. No enforcement mechanism exists. No facility is required to follow them.

Prison diets are designed for adult men. Pregnant women require 300+ additional calories per day, increased protein, and specific micronutrients. [^17] Many incarcerated pregnant women report being unable to modify their diet, having no access to prenatal vitamins without paying a copay, and supplementing from commissary with wages of $0.13 to $0.52 per hour. [^18]

Shackling: The Practice That Won't Die

As of 2025, approximately 40 states, D.C., and the federal government — through the 2018 First Step Act — prohibit the use of restraints on incarcerated individuals during labor and delivery. [^19] Only three states — New York, Illinois, and California — completely ban shackling of pregnant women. [^19] The remaining jurisdictions include exceptions for "extraordinary circumstances" or subjective "security risk" assessments — loopholes that are routinely exploited. Roughly ten states have no anti-shackling legislation at all, though 2026 legislative actions in Utah and Virginia signal ongoing state-level battles [^36]. Missouri's HB 495, signed in March 2025, extended protections to county jails, prohibiting restraints during the third trimester, labor, and for 48 hours postpartum [^20] — an expansion that underscores how recently even basic protections have been absent at the local level.

The laws do not work. A 2019 study of perinatal nurses found 83 percent reported that their patients were shackled "sometimes" to "all of the time." Twelve percent reported patients were always shackled. [^21] In one-third of cases, the only justification was adherence to facility protocol — not any individualized assessment of risk. Even in New York — which banned shackling in 2009 — a 2015 compliance audit found 85 percent of interviewed incarcerated women had been shackled while pregnant. [^22] The law had been in effect for six years. The audit relied on facility self-reporting. There is no independent monitoring mechanism, no penalty for violation, no mandatory data collection on restraint use, and no sanctions regime.

ACOG, AMA, the American Nurses Association, the American Public Health Association, and the National Commission on Correctional Health Care unanimously condemn shackling as medically dangerous. The clinical case is unambiguous: shackling interferes with emergency obstetric interventions including cesarean sections, increases fall and injury risk, impedes fetal monitoring, raises the risk of deep vein thrombosis, and causes severe pain and psychological trauma during labor. Jurisdictions that prohibit it report zero escapes and zero threats to staff from pregnant prisoners. [^23] The practice persists because enforcement is absent, correctional staff training is inconsistent, and facility-level protocols routinely contradict state law — particularly during transport to outside medical facilities.

Abortion Access at 2.1 Percent

Prior to Dobbs, incarcerated women retained a constitutional right to abortion under the Fourteenth Amendment. The access reality was a different matter. A 2021 study found only half of state prisons surveyed allowed abortion in both first and second trimesters. Fourteen percent prohibited it entirely. Thirty-two percent had no written policy at all. Two-thirds of prisons required women to self-pay for abortions. [^24] The Hyde Amendment bars federal Medicaid from covering abortion. Incarcerated women earn pennies per hour.

The 2023 BJS data captured 15 abortions across 49 jurisdictions — a prison abortion rate of 2.1 percent of all pregnancy outcomes, against a national abortion ratio of 18 percent. [^15] The barriers — financial, logistical, informational, and discretionary — made the legal right functionally inaccessible for most women.

Post-Dobbs, that legal right no longer exists in the states where the majority of women's prisons are located. A woman incarcerated in Alabama, Texas, Mississippi, or Georgia cannot access abortion at any price, for any reason, at any gestational age below the state's threshold — and prison transport does not extend across state lines for elective procedures. The constitutional framework that once provided at least a theoretical backstop is gone.

The Criminalization of Pregnancy Outcomes

Alabama's chemical endangerment statute — §26-15-3.2, originally a 2006 law targeting methamphetamine lab exposure — was expanded by the Alabama Supreme Court in 2013 and 2014 to include "viable unborn children." In 2018, Alabama enshrined fetal personhood in its state constitution. The result: hundreds of women faced pregnancy-related criminal charges in the two-year period between June 2022 and June 2024. [^25] A woman whose fetus is exposed to a controlled substance faces a Class C felony carrying one to ten years in prison. If the pregnancy ends in death — miscarriage, stillbirth — the charge escalates to a Class A felony. Prosecutors have applied the law aggressively, and its impact falls disproportionately on women of color in low-income communities. [^25]

The expansion of fetal personhood frameworks — the same legal architecture that underlies post-Dobbs abortion criminalization — is being applied to substance use cases in states with aggressive prosecutors. As this report's chapter on substance use documents, the 1,396 arrests, 79 percent in five Southern states, 46.5 percent in Alabama alone, [^25] are not abstract policy. They are the intake pipeline for every harm documented in this chapter. A pregnant woman arrested on a felony chemical endangerment charge enters a correctional system with no mandatory prenatal care standards, a 62.3 percent chance [^1] of not being pregnancy-tested, an 83 percent chance of being shackled during labor, and near-zero access to medication-assisted treatment. [^1] The same administration that pardoned 23 individuals convicted of violating the FACE Act is presiding over an expansion of the carceral apparatus targeting pregnant women.

Mother-Child Separation and the Destruction of Bonds

In the vast majority of U.S. correctional facilities, newborns are separated from their incarcerated mothers within 24 to 72 hours of birth. [^2] There is no national policy governing this separation. Only 11 state prison systems and the federal Bureau of Prisons operate nursery programs, and their scale is negligible: at the end of 2023, just 86 women were participating — against 665 live births that year. [^18] Eligibility is typically restricted to nonviolent offenses with near-term release dates, meaning the women with the longest separations are systematically excluded. No federal mandate requires states to offer nursery programs. No national standards exist for their operation.

Sixty-two percent of women in state prisons have a child under 18. Incarcerated mothers are five times more likely than incarcerated fathers to have children enter foster care. [^3] The Adoption and Safe Families Act of 1997 requires states to file termination-of-parental-rights petitions when a child has been in foster care for 15 of the most recent 22 months. [^3] ASFA includes no exception for incarceration. For mothers serving sentences longer than 15 months — the majority — this law all but guarantees permanent severance of the parent-child relationship, regardless of the mother's capacity to parent upon release. Incarcerated parents face structural barriers to completing court-ordered reunification services — parenting classes, substance abuse treatment — while behind bars. Maintaining "significant contact" is nearly impossible when phone calls cost a dollar a minute, prisons are geographically remote from courts and communities, and visitation policies are restrictive. The law treats incarceration as evidence of abandonment. Approximately one in eight incarcerated parents with children in foster care faces termination of parental rights. [^3]

The psychological toll is measured. Incarcerated postpartum women experience postpartum depression at rates of 35 to 80 percent — compared to 12 to 20 percent in the general population. [^26] The primary driver is not pre-existing mental illness. It is forced separation during the critical attachment window. Separation within hours of birth causes an acute grief reaction, compounding pre-existing trauma histories and the near-total absence of mental health services in correctional facilities. There is no federal requirement for postpartum mental health screening in correctional settings. The children, in turn, face cascading consequences: sons are 25 percent more likely to drop out of school, and across the population, children of incarcerated mothers experience higher rates of migraines, asthma, anxiety, aggressive behavior, and involvement in the juvenile justice system. They are more likely to be adopted and less likely to be reunified with their parents. [^7]

Reentry: The Coverage Gap as a Kill Zone

A structural win occurred effective January 1, 2026: the Consolidated Appropriations Act of 2024 mandated that all states must suspend — not terminate — Medicaid for incarcerated adults, eliminating the re-enrollment gap that previously left formerly incarcerated women without coverage for weeks to months after release. [^27] Separately, 19 states have been approved for Section 1115 Medicaid reentry waivers allowing pre-release services — including case management, medication-assisted treatment, and a 30-day medication supply — for up to 90 days before discharge. ^28 For incarcerated youth, mandatory Medicaid screening and case management 30 days pre-release took effect a year earlier, on January 1, 2025. [^29]

This matters. But it is being enacted in the same administrative period that is proposing $400 to $880 billion in cuts to the Medicaid program itself, [^27] and in the same states where the coverage a woman returns to may no longer include the behavioral health services, MAT access, and postpartum care that would allow her to survive reentry. Nineteen states have waivers. Thirty-one do not. For a pregnant woman released from a facility in a non-waiver state, the coverage gap remains a period of maximum medical vulnerability — the weeks after release when prenatal care lapses, prescriptions go unfilled, and the stressors of reentry converge with the physiological demands of pregnancy or postpartum recovery. Without a coverage bridge, women face delayed prenatal care, missed postpartum visits, interrupted MAT, and medication nonadherence — conditions that directly increase maternal mortality risk.

The Black Maternal Health Momnibus Act, introduced multiple sessions by the Congressional Black Caucus but not yet enacted, would begin to address this intersection. Among its provisions: federal funding for doula programs in correctional facilities, a federal ban on shackling at all stages of pregnancy, and commissioned studies on incarcerated maternal mortality with an explicit racial disparity focus. [^30] It remains proposed legislation. In the meantime, programs that exist at the margins have shown what is possible. The Minnesota Prison Doula Project has demonstrated high rates of vaginal deliveries and healthy birthweight. New York City's Growing HOPE program received a $2 million expansion grant in 2024. Mount Sinai's Arnhold Institute published its "Born Inside" report in 2026, documenting birth experiences during incarceration. [^31] These programs provide emotional, physical, and educational support through pregnancy, labor, and postpartum — increasing parenting confidence and partially addressing separation trauma. They are recognized as key interventions for maternal mortality disparities among incarcerated people of color. They remain exceptions, not systems.

The Pipeline, Measured

The data documented in this report's chapter on substance use — 1,396 arrests, 79 percent in five Southern states, 46.5 percent in Alabama alone [^25] — connects directly to these incarceration outcomes. A pregnant woman arrested on a felony chemical endangerment charge enters a correctional system with no mandatory prenatal care standards, a 62.3 percent chance [^1] of not being pregnancy-tested, an 83 percent chance of being shackled during labor, and near-zero access to MAT. [^1] If she delivers in custody, her infant is separated within 72 hours. If her sentence exceeds 15 months, [^3] ASFA timelines begin running toward the permanent termination of her parental rights. Her child enters the foster care system and begins accumulating the Adverse Childhood Experiences that, as this report's chapter on perinatal mental health documents, triple the risk of preeclampsia in that child's own future pregnancy.

The pipeline is not theoretical. Every link has been measured. And every link is being strengthened, not broken, by the current policy trajectory.

Chapter 7: Pregnancy Behind Bars

Consider two possible endings for the patient in the preceding chapter — the pregnant woman whose partner loaded a gun on her, whose partner jumped her, whose partner burned their ultrasound pictures.

In the first ending, she follows the physician's safety plan. She hides her documents. She leaves the area. She delivers her baby somewhere else. She survives.

In the second ending, he escalates. She defends herself. She uses a weapon — the same weapon he loaded on her. He is injured or killed. She is arrested. She is charged with assault or homicide. She is pregnant at the time of her arrest. She enters the correctional system.

In the second ending, the system does not see a victim. It sees a perpetrator. She enters a facility where there is a 62.3 percent chance [^1] she will not be pregnancy-tested. Where she may labor in shackles. Where her baby will be separated from her within 72 hours. [^2] Where, if her sentence exceeds 15 months, [^3] the Adoption and Safe Families Act will begin the clock toward permanent termination of her parental rights — and she will lose the child she carried through the violence.

Ninety-four percent of incarcerated women have experienced domestic violence. [^4] Seventy-four percent of women imprisoned for murder or manslaughter had experienced intimate partner violence within a year of the offense that led to their conviction. [^4] The system that failed to protect them then punishes them for surviving.

The System Before Dobbs

Between 1980 and 2023, the number of incarcerated women in the United States grew by more than 600 percent — from 26,326 to 186,244 — a rate of growth twice that of men. [^5] The drivers were the War on Drugs, mandatory minimum sentencing, and the criminalization of poverty. Women in state prisons are incarcerated for drug or property offenses at rates substantially higher than men — the proportion convicted of drug offenses rose from 12 percent in 1986 to 25 percent by 2021. [^6] Sixty percent of incarcerated women lived below the federal poverty line before arrest. [^5] By 2023, over one million women were under some form of criminal justice supervision — prison, jail, probation, or parole — and the female incarcerated population rebounded 22 percent in a single post-pandemic year. [^7]

This growth was a racial project. Between 1986 and 1991 — a single five-year period — incarceration of Black women in state prisons for drug offenses surged 828 percent. [^6] Despite a 69 percent decline in Black women's imprisonment rate since 2000, Black women remain imprisoned at 68 per 100,000 — 1.7 times the white women's rate of 41 per 100,000. [^6] In the same period, white women's imprisonment rate actually rose 18 percent, [^6] narrowing the disparity ratio while disguising the underlying expansion of the carceral apparatus into all women's lives. Latina women are imprisoned at 51 per 100,000, 1.2 times the white rate. [^6] American Indian and Alaska Native women experience an incarceration rate 4.3 times higher than white women — the highest disparity of any group [^8] — driven by the intersection of federal jurisdiction on tribal lands, chronic underfunding of Indian Health Service, and historical trauma.

The geography matters as much as the demography. Idaho imprisons women at 152 per 100,000 — the highest rate in the nation. Rhode Island imprisons women at 6 per 100,000 — the lowest. [^9] That is a twenty-five-fold variation. [^9] A pregnant woman's exposure to every harm documented in this chapter — prenatal care gaps, shackling, postpartum separation, Medicaid disruption, and family severance — is determined not by clinical need or individual risk but by which side of a state line she was standing on when she was arrested.

The Abuse-to-Incarceration Pipeline

Over 80 percent of incarcerated women have histories of physical or sexual abuse — many are imprisoned for defending themselves against their abusers. The cases of Cyntoia Brown-Long and Marissa Alexander are the ones that became nationally visible, but the structural pattern they represent is routine: self-defense standards built around a male model of combat — the requirements of imminent threat and proportional force — systematically fail women who use a weapon against an unarmed attacker twice their size, or who strike preemptively after years of escalating violence. Black women are disproportionately arrested in domestic violence incidents even when they are the victims, and receive longer sentences than white women in comparable self-defense cases. Stand Your Ground laws, written to protect the person with the gun in the confrontation, have not functioned equivalently for Black women.

The pipeline begins even earlier. Black girls are 14 percent of the female youth population but 39 percent of girls in juvenile residential facilities. [^10] Native girls are confined at 39 per 100,000 — four times the rate of white girls. Black girls are confined at 29 per 100,000 — three times the rate. [^11] Between 40 and 81 percent of justice-involved girls report histories of sexual abuse, compared to 20 percent in the general population, [^10] and girls are nearly twice as likely as boys to report five or more distinct forms of abuse. [^10] Forty percent of youth placed for status offenses — truancy, curfew violations, running away — are girls, despite representing only 15 percent of the total incarcerated youth population. [^11] These offenses are survival behaviors. Running away from a home where you are being raped is not a criminal act. But it is treated as one, and it is the first contact point in a system that will follow these girls into adult incarceration, adult pregnancy, and adult loss.

The Pregnancy Testing Gap and the Data Void

Only 37.7 percent of U.S. jails conduct pregnancy tests upon entry — a number that comes from the only national-scale jail dataset in the field, the 2016–2017 Johns Hopkins Pregnancy in Prison Statistics study. [^1] Most facilities rely on self-reporting, which misses pregnancies where women don't yet know they're pregnant, fear disclosure due to potential loss of autonomy, or don't trust correctional healthcare providers. Pregnancies are routinely discovered late, missing critical windows for prenatal care initiation and, where legal, abortion access.

The federal picture is marginally better but appallingly recent. The first comprehensive federal data on pregnancy in prisons — the BJS Maternal Health Supplement — was not administered until 2024, covering calendar year 2023. In the 47 jurisdictions reporting, 88 percent of female admissions were tested for pregnancy, and 2 percent tested positive. [^12] On a single day — December 31, 2023 — 328 pregnant women were in state or federal prisons, representing 0.5 percent of the female prison population. [^13] Approximately 58,000 pregnant people enter U.S. jails and prisons annually. [^14] That the federal government did not begin counting them until 2024 is not an oversight. It is a design feature of a system that does not want to be measured.

The BJS data recorded 727 pregnancy outcomes across 49 jurisdictions in 2023: 665 live births (91.5 percent), 47 miscarriages (6.5 percent), and 15 abortions (2.1 percent). [^15] These numbers are a beginning, but they are not accountability. The BJS report excludes jails, private facilities, and the metrics that would actually permit evaluation of care quality — preterm birth rates, low birth weight, NICU admissions, and maternal mortality. A 2024 Government Accountability Office review concluded that comprehensive national data on pregnancy outcomes and care quality in correctional facilities does not exist. [^16] Without it, tracking maternal or infant mortality in custody, identifying systemic failures in obstetric care, or comparing outcomes across jurisdictions is structurally impossible. The data gap itself prevents racial disaggregation — you cannot document a disparity you refuse to measure.

Among the pregnant women whose race was recorded in the 2023 BJS data, 60 percent were white, 20 percent were Black, 9 percent were Hispanic, and 4 percent were American Indian or Alaska Native. [^14] ACOG's Committee Opinion 830 states that incarcerated pregnant people must receive care equivalent to non-incarcerated patients [^17] — comprehensive prenatal care, high-risk screening, twenty-four-hour emergency access, breastfeeding support. These are professional recommendations. No enforcement mechanism exists. No facility is required to follow them.

Prison diets are designed for adult men. Pregnant women require 300+ additional calories per day, increased protein, and specific micronutrients. [^17] Many incarcerated pregnant women report being unable to modify their diet, having no access to prenatal vitamins without paying a copay, and supplementing from commissary with wages of $0.13 to $0.52 per hour. [^18]

Shackling: The Practice That Won't Die

As of 2025, approximately 40 states, D.C., and the federal government — through the 2018 First Step Act — prohibit the use of restraints on incarcerated individuals during labor and delivery. [^19] Only three states — New York, Illinois, and California — completely ban shackling of pregnant women. [^19] The remaining jurisdictions include exceptions for "extraordinary circumstances" or subjective "security risk" assessments — loopholes that are routinely exploited. Roughly ten states have no anti-shackling legislation at all. Missouri's HB 495, signed in March 2025, extended protections to county jails, prohibiting restraints during the third trimester, labor, and for 48 hours postpartum [^20] — an expansion that underscores how recently even basic protections have been absent at the local level.

Even in New York — which banned shackling in 2009 — a 2015 compliance audit found 85 percent of interviewed incarcerated women had been shackled while pregnant. [^22] The law had been in effect for six years. The audit relied on facility self-reporting. There is no independent monitoring mechanism, no penalty for violation, no mandatory data collection on restraint use, and no sanctions regime.

ACOG, AMA, the American Nurses Association, the American Public Health Association, and the National Commission on Correctional Health Care unanimously condemn shackling as medically dangerous. Jurisdictions that prohibit it report zero escapes and zero threats to staff from pregnant prisoners. [^23] The practice persists because enforcement is absent, correctional staff training is inconsistent, and facility-level protocols routinely contradict state law — particularly during transport to outside medical facilities.

Her child enters the foster care system and begins accumulating the Adverse Childhood Experiences that, as this report's chapter on perinatal mental health documents, triple the risk of preeclampsia in that child's own future pregnancy.

The pipeline is not theoretical. Every link has been measured. And every link is being strengthened, not broken, by the current policy trajectory.

Chapter 8: The Children

Every policy restriction of the last decade was justified in the name of children. Here is what happened to them.

She arrived at triage dilated to eight centimeters. The team moved her urgently to a labor room. On re-exam, there was an anterior lip — a rim of cervix still present. The physician reduced it gently. The fetal heart rate was decelerating: 60s to 90s. Pushing began.

Then the head delivered and the body did not.

Shoulder dystocia. The baby's anterior shoulder was trapped behind the pubic bone. The clock started. McRoberts maneuver — the mother's knees driven toward her chest to widen the pelvic outlet. Suprapubic pressure — an assistant pressing down above the pubic bone to dislodge the shoulder. Neither worked. The physician reached behind the baby and delivered the posterior arm, rotating the body free.

Total time: approximately one minute. The baby was born in direct occiput anterior position. APGAR score at delivery: zero. Not breathing. No heart rate. No muscle tone. No reflex. No color. Zero.

After resuscitation, the APGAR rose to seven. The baby improved significantly. There was a possible fracture of the clavicle — the collarbone may have broken during delivery, a known complication of the maneuvers required to save a life. The mother had a second-degree perineal laceration and two deep labial lacerations tracking toward her urethra, repaired with absorbable suture. The left labia continued to bleed after repair and was managed with gentle pressure.

The placenta was sent to pathology. The delivery was reviewed with the team and the parents. Questions were answered. Surgical counts were correct. The family went home with a baby who, for approximately one minute of its life, had been dead.

That minute is the gap this chapter is about. The space between a system that works and a system that does not — measured in seconds, in staffing levels, in whether the hospital has a neonatologist in the building, in whether the NICU can handle what happens when a delivery goes wrong.

The Poverty Multiplier

When the safety net was dismantled, children absorbed the impact first. The Medicaid unwinding stripped coverage from 5.7 million children — 72% of whom lost their insurance not because they were no longer eligible, but because of administrative paperwork errors. Children comprised 53% of total Medicaid disenrollments. According to KFF and the Georgetown Center for Children and Families, Black and Latino children were disenrolled at 1.8 times the rate of white children for procedural reasons alone. These were not policy decisions about eligibility. They were bureaucratic failures visited upon the most vulnerable enrollees in the system.

The racial architecture of child uninsurance tells its own story. American Indian and Alaska Native children carry the highest uninsured rate at 8.4%, followed by Hispanic children at 8.1%. White non-Hispanic children sit at 3.5%. The gap is not narrowing. Immigration status of parents creates Medicaid and CHIP eligibility barriers for Hispanic children. Geographic isolation reduces coverage enrollment for AI/AN children. And now, the 2026 budget reconciliation proposes a $625 billion reduction in federal Medicaid for children over ten years through per-capita caps and work requirements. According to the Congressional Budget Office, work requirements alone would disenroll an estimated 1.7 million additional children. Thirty-seven million children are currently enrolled. The scale of the proposed cut is civilizational.

Simultaneously, the administration targeted the foundational supports for early childhood. The Special Supplemental Nutrition Program for Women, Infants, and Children (WIC) faces a proposed $1.1 billion cut in the 2026 budget, threatening participation for 1.4 million women, infants, and children. The WIC Cash Value Benefit for fruits and vegetables — the program's most effective nutritional tool — faces a proposed reduction from $26 to $10 for children and from $52 to $13 for breastfeeding mothers. WIC's current enrollment of 6.7 million is already declining: participation among eligible infants fell to 49% in 2025, down from 56% in 2020. Among AI/AN families — who face the highest food insecurity rates in the nation at 27% or higher — WIC enrollment sits at just 38%. The cuts disproportionately harm the 53% Hispanic, 22% Black, and 2% AI/AN families who make up WIC's enrollment base.

Head Start — the early childhood education program that also functions as an essential detection and intervention system for abuse and neglect — faces outright elimination. The 2026 federal budget proposes cutting Head Start's entire $11.7 billion budget, threatening services for 750,000 children ages zero to five. Head Start disproportionately serves children of color: 36% Hispanic, 30% Black, 8% Native American — populations with the fewest alternative early childhood education options. Decades of research, including landmark work by Nobel laureate James Heckman at NBER, show Head Start improves school readiness, health outcomes, and lifetime earnings. No replacement mechanism has been proposed.

The childcare system, already a market failure, collapsed further. With the $24 billion ARPA childcare stabilization fund expiring in September 2024 — a fund that had preserved an estimated 3.3 million childcare slots and stabilized wages for 1.5 million childcare workers — the sector fell off a fiscal cliff. Federal block grants were reduced. Prices surged 22% between 2020 and late 2024, leaving working families spending up to 16% of their income on care for a single child. Family childcare homes, predominantly owned by women of color, received proportionally less stabilization funding than center-based providers due to application complexity. The childcare workforce — 46% workers of color, median wage $13.65 per hour — has no economic incentive to remain.

And then Dobbs forced hundreds of thousands of pregnancies that women did not want and could not afford. The UCSF Turnaway Study provides the definitive data on children born from denied terminations. Their mothers were four times more likely to fall below the federal poverty line. The children themselves faced worse developmental outcomes: lower scores on cognitive assessments, higher rates of behavioral and emotional problems, and a lower likelihood of meeting educational benchmarks. In Texas, infant mortality due to congenital anomalies increased 22.9% following the implementation of SB8, compared to a 3.1% decrease nationally — a finding published in JAMA Pediatrics that quantifies the lethal cost of forcing families to carry pregnancies with fatal fetal diagnoses to term.

The mechanism here is not complex. Poverty is the single most powerful predictor of adverse childhood outcomes. Forced birth drives poverty. The policies of the administration guaranteed both.

Before the First Breath: Infant Mortality and NICU Disparities

The infant mortality rate in the United States is 5.4 per 1,000 live births. For Black infants, it is 9.8. For AI/AN infants, 8.1. For white infants, 4.3. The Black-to-white ratio — 2.28 to 1 — has not materially changed in a generation.

The disparities begin in the NICU. According to the Vermont Oxford Network, Black very preterm infants have 30% higher in-NICU mortality than white very preterm infants after controlling for gestational age — 10.2% versus 7.8%. Severe intraventricular hemorrhage rates are more than double: 9.3% for Black infants versus 4.4% for white. A critical driver is the antenatal corticosteroid gap: only 62% of Black mothers delivering very preterm receive antenatal steroids, compared to 79% of white mothers. This is not a knowledge gap. It is a care-delivery failure.

Geography compounds race. Black and AI/AN infants are disproportionately born at hospitals without Level III NICUs — 28% and 41% respectively, compared to 19% for white infants. Rural NICUs transport very low birth weight infants a median of 47 miles to reach Level III care. Transport-associated morbidity — hypothermia, oxygen desaturation, glucose derangement — occurs in 18% of these transfers. Every mile is a risk multiplier.

Sudden Unexpected Infant Death tells the same story in different registers. The overall SUID rate is 0.87 per 1,000 live births. For AI/AN infants, it is 1.91. For Black infants, 1.72. For white infants, 0.72. Unsafe sleep environments — bed-sharing on adult mattresses, soft bedding, prone positioning — are more prevalent in low-income households, where the cost of a crib is not trivial and where safe sleep campaigns have failed to reach.

Breastfeeding: The First Disparity

Breastfeeding initiation has reached 84.1% nationally. But the racial gap persists with mechanical precision. Black infants have a 73.6% initiation rate compared to 88.9% for white infants — a 15-point gap. By six months, exclusive breastfeeding rates diverge further: 18.8% for Black infants versus 28.5% for white infants, according to the CDC.

The causes are structural, not cultural. Black mothers face shorter maternity leave, less workplace lactation support despite the PUMP Act, higher rates of in-hospital formula supplementation, and dramatically lower access to IBCLC-certified lactation consultants. NICU separation — which Black infants experience at higher rates due to higher preterm birth rates — reduces initiation. In NICUs, kangaroo mother care hours reveal the gap: Black families average 2.1 hours per day of skin-to-skin contact versus 3.4 hours for white families. The barriers are language, transportation, work schedules, and implicit provider bias.

The downstream costs are measurable. Lower breastfeeding rates are associated with higher gastrointestinal infection, otitis media, SIDS, and childhood obesity. Black infants bear a disproportionate disease burden from this single disparity.

Feeding the Children: The Food Insecurity Crisis

In 2024, 14.1 million children lived in food-insecure households — up from 13.8 million the year before. The racial breakdown is stark: 42.4% of Black households with children and 42.7% of Hispanic households are food-insecure, compared to 25.0% of white households. The numbers are climbing.

The SNAP emergency allotments that had cushioned millions of families ended in March 2023, cutting benefits for 30 million households by an average of $90 per month. Food pantry demand surged 30-50% immediately. Feeding America projects national food bank demand to increase 13% in 2026 versus the 2023 baseline. And now, the 2025 reconciliation package proposes $230 billion in ten-year SNAP cuts, expanding work requirements to parents of children ages seven to seventeen and ending the longstanding caregiver exemption. The Center on Budget and Policy Priorities projects 5 to 10 million individuals at risk of losing benefits. Families with children bear approximately 40% of the cuts.

School meals — the last standing universal nutrition program for children — are under direct threat. Thirty million children rely on school meals. The 2026 budget reconciliation proposes requiring states to fund 25% of school meal costs, up from zero, jeopardizing universal free meal programs in eighteen states. Separately, the House Child Nutrition Reauthorization proposal would raise the Community Eligibility Provision poverty threshold from 40% to 60%, eliminating universal free lunch for approximately 7,000 schools serving 18 million students. Nine states have enacted universal school meals laws since the pandemic, reporting 15-30% increases in participation, elimination of meal debt, and improved attendance. The federal government is now moving to dismantle what the states built.

For Native American communities, the crisis is existential. AI/AN households face food insecurity rates of 27% or higher nationally, reaching 44% in some reservation communities. Thirty percent of tribal households lack a SNAP-authorized store within ten miles. The Food Distribution Program on Indian Reservations provides processed staples that bear no relationship to traditional Indigenous diets. Traditional foods represent less than 1% of SNAP-eligible items.

The Poisoned Environment

Children living in poverty are more than twice as likely to have elevated blood lead levels. Five hundred thousand children ages one to five currently have blood lead levels at or above 3.5 micrograms per deciliter. AI/AN infants carry a mean blood lead level of 2.8 micrograms per deciliter versus 1.4 for white infants — double the exposure, in a context where no safe level exists. Each 10-microgram increase costs 1 to 5 IQ points, permanently. The EPA reports that 37% of K-12 schools have detectable lead in drinking water, with Black and Hispanic students disproportionately attending the most contaminated schools.

PFAS contamination has reached breast milk. According to the EPA and the Silent Spring Institute, 45% of U.S. breast milk samples tested in 2024-2025 contained detectable PFAS. Children exposed to PFAS show 40% reduced vaccine response and elevated thyroid dysfunction risk. AI/AN communities near military bases and manufacturing sites face PFAS exposure four to ten times higher than the general population. Two hundred million Americans drink PFAS-contaminated water, and the children among them are absorbing these chemicals through every route — water, food, dust, and the breast milk that is supposed to protect them.

Wildfire smoke adds another layer. Children exposed to wildfire PM2.5 have 39% higher respiratory ER visits.[^13] The western United States saw a 22% increase in pediatric respiratory hospitalizations during the 2024 fire season.[^13] Black children already carry an asthma prevalence of 13.4%, nearly double the 7.7% rate for white children[^14], and asthma accounts for 13.8 million missed school days annually.[^14] Environmental deregulation under the 2025 EPA rollbacks compounds every one of these exposures.

The Return of Child Labor

As families were pushed deeper into poverty, the regulatory guardrails protecting children were simultaneously dismantled. The Department of Labor documented 17,203 children employed in violation of child labor laws in fiscal year 2023[^2] — the highest number in fifteen years. Violations increased 88% between 2019 and 2023[^3]. Six hundred eighty-eight employers were cited. Total penalties amounted to $6.6 million, an average of $9,600 per violation — less than the cost of a month's childcare.

This was not accidental. Twenty-eight states introduced or passed legislation weakening child labor protections[^30], lowering the minimum age for hazardous work, extending allowable working hours, and removing work permit requirements. The children filling these slots are predominantly Hispanic and Latino immigrant youth, including unaccompanied minors released from HHS custody to sponsors who exploit them for labor in meatpacking, food processing, construction, and cleaning. The safety net was cut, forcing teenagers into the labor market. Then the labor market's protections were removed to receive them.

The Vaccination Collapse

National kindergarten MMR vaccination coverage fell from 95.2% in the 2019-2020 school year to 92.5% in 2025[^7] — below the 95% herd immunity threshold. That decline left 280,000 unprotected kindergartners[^7]. By 2025, the United States recorded 1,333 confirmed measles cases[^8], the highest since the disease was declared eliminated in 2000.

The collapse was engineered from the top. All seventeen members of the Advisory Committee on Immunization Practices were dismissed in February 2025 by HHS Secretary RFK Jr.[^9] The replacement committee was not seated for four months. A fifty-year institution of independent scientific vaccine review was decapitated. Infant vaccine series completion declined to 69.5% by age 24 months[^10], with stark racial gaps: 61.2% for Black infants versus 74.3% for white. Among undocumented and mixed-status immigrant children, vaccination rates are 12 to 18 percentage points lower than citizen children[^11] — the 2025 immigration enforcement surge significantly increased healthcare avoidance in immigrant communities, with 55% of parents in mixed-status families reporting they avoided healthcare for their children entirely[^12].

The Pediatric Mental Health Catastrophe

Among high school girls, persistent sadness or hopelessness increased from 36% in 2011 to 57% in 2021.[^15] For Black girls, the rate reached 69%[^31]. For Native and Indigenous girls, 74%[^31]. Suicidal ideation stands at 30% for girls nationally[^15]. The CDC Youth Risk Behavior Surveillance System data is unambiguous: this is the worst adolescent mental health crisis in the history of the survey.

Black youth suicide rates increased 78% from 2018 to 2022.[^16] Black boys ages five to twelve now have the highest suicide rate of any pediatric racial group[^16]. AI/AN youth have suicide rates 2.5 to 3 times the national average[^17], with mental health services on reservations available at only 40% of recommended levels. The pediatric mental health system's response has been structural failure: 48% of children presenting to emergency departments with psychiatric crises board for 24 hours or more awaiting admission.[^18] Pediatric psychiatric bed capacity contracted 8% between 2016 and 2023, with 90% of remaining beds concentrated in urban areas.

The school system — theoretically the frontline of detection — cannot absorb the demand. The national school counselor ratio stands at 1:408 versus the recommended 1:250.^19 Ninety-six percent of counselors report inability to meet student needs.^19 Counselors devote only 25% of their time to mental health support^19, diverted to administrative tasks like scheduling and test coordination.

Transgender youth face a compounded crisis. Twenty-six states have enacted bans on gender-affirming care for minors, affecting approximately 150,000 young people.[^20] According to the Williams Institute at UCLA, transgender youth in ban states report 72% higher rates of suicidal ideation and twice the attempted suicide rate compared to youth in non-ban states.[^21] The Trevor Project's 2024 survey found that 41% of LGBTQ youth nationally seriously considered suicide in the past year[^22] — a figure that rose to 56% in states without legal protections.[^22]

The Foster Care Pipeline

Black children represent 14% of the U.S. child population but 23% of all children in foster care[^23], according to the Administration for Children and Families. The overrepresentation is driven by systemic bias in mandated reporting, structural poverty, and disproportionate scrutiny of marginalized families by child protective services. Girls in foster care are 2.5 times more likely to experience teen pregnancy than their non-foster peers.[^24] Fifty percent of former foster youth have a child before age 21.[^24] Sixty percent report inconsistent access to contraception.[^24]

Meanwhile, 5.1 million U.S.-citizen children have at least one undocumented parent.[^5] Concurrently, DACA recipients briefly gained ACA marketplace access in late 2024, only to have eligibility abruptly reversed by a mid-2025 federal rule change, severing access to subsidized care for a predominantly reproductive-age population.[^33] The 2025-2026 immigration enforcement surge has subjected 185,000 or more of these citizen children to parental deportation or detention.[^5] Parental absence is itself an ACE — one that produces PTSD, anxiety, behavioral regression, educational disruption, and, for many, entry into the very foster care system that will compound their trauma.

The ACEs Pipeline: Building Tomorrow's Mortality Crisis

The CDC's Adverse Childhood Experiences study established the dose-response relationship between childhood adversity and adult health outcomes. The math is not theoretical. It is a biological certainty.

Currently, 1 in 5 American adults carries an ACE score of 4 or higher^25 — a figure the CDC's BRFSS data places at 20.4%.^25 Among American Indian and Alaska Native adults, that figure is 32.4%, the highest of any racial group. Each additional ACE increases the risk of preterm birth — a BMJ pooled meta-analysis found an odds ratio of 1.41 for any ACE exposure.^26 An ACE score of 4 or higher triples a woman's risk of developing preeclampsia.[^27] An ACE score of 6 reduces life expectancy by 20 years.[^28] California's Medi-Cal ACE Aware program, active since 2020, remains the only state-level initiative with reimbursable screening linked to trained referral infrastructure — a model no other state has replicated.

The crises documented in this chapter — the 5.7 million children who lost Medicaid[^1], the 17,203 children in illegal labor, the 14.1 million children in food-insecure households[^4] (a crisis compounded by a $1.1 billion cut to WIC funding in the 2026 budget proposal, threatening 1.4 million participants[^32]), the 185,000 citizen children experiencing parental deportation, the half-million children with elevated blood lead[^6] — are all accumulating ACEs right now.

This is the pipeline. Childhood adversity feeds into the adolescent mental health crisis. That distress feeds into substance use, which feeds into chronic disease, which feeds into pregnancy complications, which feeds into maternal death. Firearms are now the leading cause of death for children ages one to nineteen — 4,752 pediatric firearm deaths in 2022[^29], with Black children facing a firearm homicide rate 20 times higher than white children.

The maternal mortality crisis of 2035 and 2040 is being built today. We know precisely how many children are entering the pipeline, and we know exactly what will happen to them when they reach the end of it.

Chapter 8: The Children
