In Texas, infant mortality due to congenital anomalies increased 22.9% following the implementation of SB8, compared to a 3.1% decrease nationally — a finding published in JAMA Pediatrics that quantifies the lethal cost of forcing families to carry pregnancies with fatal fetal diagnoses to term.

The mechanism here is not complex. Poverty is the single most powerful predictor of adverse childhood outcomes. Forced birth drives poverty. The policies of the administration guaranteed both.

Before the First Breath: Infant Mortality and NICU Disparities

The infant mortality rate in the United States is 5.4 per 1,000 live births. For Black infants, it is 9.8. For AI/AN infants, 8.1. For white infants, 4.3. The Black-to-white ratio — 2.28 to 1 — has not materially changed in a generation.

The disparities begin in the NICU. According to the Vermont Oxford Network, Black very preterm infants have 30% higher in-NICU mortality than white very preterm infants after controlling for gestational age — 10.2% versus 7.8%. Severe intraventricular hemorrhage rates are more than double: 9.3% for Black infants versus 4.4% for white. A critical driver is the antenatal corticosteroid gap: only 62% of Black mothers delivering very preterm receive antenatal steroids, compared to 79% of white mothers. This is not a knowledge gap. It is a care-delivery failure.

Geography compounds race. Black and AI/AN infants are disproportionately born at hospitals without Level III NICUs — 28% and 41% respectively, compared to 19% for white infants. Rural NICUs transport very low birth weight infants a median of 47 miles to reach Level III care. Transport-associated morbidity — hypothermia, oxygen desaturation, glucose derangement — occurs in 18% of these transfers. Every mile is a risk multiplier.

Sudden Unexpected Infant Death tells the same story in different registers. The overall SUID rate is 0.87 per 1,000 live births. For AI/AN infants, it is 1.91. For Black infants, 1.72. For white infants, 0.72. Unsafe sleep environments — bed-sharing on adult mattresses, soft bedding, prone positioning — are more prevalent in low-income households, where the cost of a crib is not trivial and where safe sleep campaigns have failed to reach.

Breastfeeding: The First Disparity

Breastfeeding initiation has reached 84.1% nationally. But the racial gap persists with mechanical precision. Black infants have a 73.6% initiation rate compared to 88.9% for white infants — a 15-point gap. By six months, exclusive breastfeeding rates diverge further: 18.8% for Black infants versus 28.5% for white infants, according to the CDC.

The causes are structural, not cultural. Black mothers face shorter maternity leave, less workplace lactation support despite the PUMP Act, higher rates of in-hospital formula supplementation, and dramatically lower access to IBCLC-certified lactation consultants. NICU separation — which Black infants experience at higher rates due to higher preterm birth rates — reduces initiation. In NICUs, kangaroo mother care hours reveal the gap: Black families average 2.1 hours per day of skin-to-skin contact versus 3.4 hours for white families. The barriers are language, transportation, work schedules, and implicit provider bias.

The downstream costs are measurable. Lower breastfeeding rates are associated with higher gastrointestinal infection, otitis media, SIDS, and childhood obesity. Black infants bear a disproportionate disease burden from this single disparity.

Feeding the Children: The Food Insecurity Crisis

In 2024, 14.1 million children lived in food-insecure households — up from 13.8 million the year before. The racial breakdown is stark: 42.4% of Black households with children and 42.7% of Hispanic households are food-insecure, compared to 25.0% of white households. The numbers are climbing.

The SNAP emergency allotments that had cushioned millions of families ended in March 2023, cutting benefits for 30 million households by an average of $90 per month. Food pantry demand surged 30-50% immediately. Feeding America projects national food bank demand to increase 13% in 2026 versus the 2023 baseline. And now, the 2025 reconciliation package proposes $230 billion in ten-year SNAP cuts, expanding work requirements to parents of children ages seven to seventeen and ending the longstanding caregiver exemption. The Center on Budget and Policy Priorities projects 5 to 10 million individuals at risk of losing benefits. Families with children bear approximately 40% of the cuts.

School meals — the last standing universal nutrition program for children — are under direct threat. Thirty million children rely on school meals. The 2026 budget reconciliation proposes requiring states to fund 25% of school meal costs, up from zero, jeopardizing universal free meal programs in eighteen states. Separately, the House Child Nutrition Reauthorization proposal would raise the Community Eligibility Provision poverty threshold from 40% to 60%, eliminating universal free lunch for approximately 7,000 schools serving 18 million students. Nine states have enacted universal school meals laws since the pandemic, reporting 15-30% increases in participation, elimination of meal debt, and improved attendance. The federal government is now moving to dismantle what the states built.

For Native American communities, the crisis is existential. AI/AN households face food insecurity rates of 27% or higher nationally, reaching 44% in some reservation communities. Thirty percent of tribal households lack a SNAP-authorized store within ten miles. The Food Distribution Program on Indian Reservations provides processed staples that bear no relationship to traditional Indigenous diets. Traditional foods represent less than 1% of SNAP-eligible items.

The Poisoned Environment

Children living in poverty are more than twice as likely to have elevated blood lead levels. Five hundred thousand children ages one to five currently have blood lead levels at or above 3.5 micrograms per deciliter. AI/AN infants carry a mean blood lead level of 2.8 micrograms per deciliter versus 1.4 for white infants — double the exposure, in a context where no safe level exists. Each 10-microgram increase costs 1 to 5 IQ points, permanently. The EPA reports that 37% of K-12 schools have detectable lead in drinking water, with Black and Hispanic students disproportionately attending the most contaminated schools.

PFAS contamination has reached breast milk. According to the EPA and the Silent Spring Institute, 45% of U.S. breast milk samples tested in 2024-2025 contained detectable PFAS. Children exposed to PFAS show 40% reduced vaccine response and elevated thyroid dysfunction risk. AI/AN communities near military bases and manufacturing sites face PFAS exposure four to ten times higher than the general population. Two hundred million Americans drink PFAS-contaminated water, and the children among them are absorbing these chemicals through every route — water, food, dust, and the breast milk that is supposed to protect them.

Wildfire smoke adds another layer. Children exposed to wildfire PM2.5 have 39% higher respiratory ER visits.[^13] The western United States saw a 22% increase in pediatric respiratory hospitalizations during the 2024 fire season.[^13] Black children already carry an asthma prevalence of 13.4%, nearly double the 7.7% rate for white children[^14], and asthma accounts for 13.8 million missed school days annually.[^14] Environmental deregulation under the 2025 EPA rollbacks compounds every one of these exposures.

The Return of Child Labor

As families were pushed deeper into poverty, the regulatory guardrails protecting children were simultaneously dismantled. The Department of Labor documented 17,203 children employed in violation of child labor laws in fiscal year 2023[^2] — the highest number in fifteen years. Violations increased 88% between 2019 and 2023[^3]. Six hundred eighty-eight employers were cited. Total penalties amounted to $6.6 million, an average of $9,600 per violation — less than the cost of a month's childcare.

This was not accidental. Twenty-eight states introduced or passed legislation weakening child labor protections[^30], lowering the minimum age for hazardous work, extending allowable working hours, and removing work permit requirements. The children filling these slots are predominantly Hispanic and Latino immigrant youth, including unaccompanied minors released from HHS custody to sponsors who exploit them for labor in meatpacking, food processing, construction, and cleaning. The safety net was cut, forcing teenagers into the labor market. Then the labor market's protections were removed to receive them.

The Vaccination Collapse

National kindergarten MMR vaccination coverage fell from 95.2% in the 2019-2020 school year to 92.7% in 2023-2024[^7] — below the 95% herd immunity threshold. That decline left 280,000 unprotected kindergartners[^7]. By 2025, the United States recorded 1,333 confirmed measles cases[^8], the highest since the disease was declared eliminated in 2000.

The collapse was engineered from the top. All seventeen members of the Advisory Committee on Immunization Practices were dismissed in February 2025 by HHS Secretary RFK Jr.[^9] The replacement committee was not seated for four months. A fifty-year institution of independent scientific vaccine review was decapitated. Infant vaccine series completion declined to 69.5% by age 24 months[^10], with stark racial gaps: 61.2% for Black infants versus 74.3% for white. Among undocumented and mixed-status immigrant children, vaccination rates are 12 to 18 percentage points lower than citizen children[^11] — the 2025 immigration enforcement surge significantly increased healthcare avoidance in immigrant communities, with 55% of parents in mixed-status families reporting they avoided healthcare for their children entirely[^12].

The Pediatric Mental Health Catastrophe

Among high school girls, persistent sadness or hopelessness increased from 36% in 2011 to 57% in 2021.[^15] For Black girls, the rate reached 69%[^31]. For Native and Indigenous girls, 74%[^31]. Suicidal ideation stands at 30% for girls nationally[^15]. The CDC Youth Risk Behavior Surveillance System data is unambiguous: this is the worst adolescent mental health crisis in the history of the survey.

Black youth suicide rates increased 78% from 2018 to 2022.[^16] Black boys ages five to twelve now have the highest suicide rate of any pediatric racial group[^16]. AI/AN youth have suicide rates 2.5 to 3 times the national average[^17], with mental health services on reservations available at only 40% of recommended levels. The pediatric mental health system's response has been structural failure: 48% of children presenting to emergency departments with psychiatric crises board for 24 hours or more awaiting admission.[^18] Pediatric psychiatric bed capacity contracted 8% between 2016 and 2023, with 90% of remaining beds concentrated in urban areas.

The school system — theoretically the frontline of detection — cannot absorb the demand. The national school counselor ratio stands at 1:408 versus the recommended 1:250.^19 Ninety-six percent of counselors report inability to meet student needs.^19 Counselors devote only 25% of their time to mental health support^19, diverted to administrative tasks like scheduling and test coordination.

Transgender youth face a compounded crisis. Twenty-six states have enacted bans on gender-affirming care for minors, affecting approximately 150,000 young people.[^20] According to the Williams Institute at UCLA, transgender youth in ban states report 72% higher rates of suicidal ideation and twice the attempted suicide rate compared to youth in non-ban states.[^21] The Trevor Project's 2024 survey found that 41% of LGBTQ youth nationally seriously considered suicide in the past year[^22] — a figure that rose to 56% in states without legal protections.[^22]

The Foster Care Pipeline

Black children represent 14% of the U.S. child population but 23% of all children in foster care[^23], according to the Administration for Children and Families. The overrepresentation is driven by systemic bias in mandated reporting, structural poverty, and disproportionate scrutiny of marginalized families by child protective services. Girls in foster care are 2.5 times more likely to experience teen pregnancy than their non-foster peers.[^24] Fifty percent of former foster youth have a child before age 21.[^24] Sixty percent report inconsistent access to contraception.[^24]

Meanwhile, 5.1 million U.S.-citizen children have at least one undocumented parent.[^5] The 2025-2026 immigration enforcement surge has subjected 185,000 or more of these citizen children to parental deportation or detention.[^5] Parental absence is itself an ACE — one that produces PTSD, anxiety, behavioral regression, educational disruption, and, for many, entry into the very foster care system that will compound their trauma.

The ACEs Pipeline: Building Tomorrow's Mortality Crisis

The CDC's Adverse Childhood Experiences study established the dose-response relationship between childhood adversity and adult health outcomes. The math is not theoretical. It is a biological certainty.

Currently, 1 in 5 American adults carries an ACE score of 4 or higher^25 — a figure the CDC's BRFSS data places at 20.4%.^25 Among American Indian and Alaska Native adults, that figure is 32.4%, the highest of any racial group. Each additional ACE increases the risk of preterm birth — a BMJ pooled meta-analysis found an odds ratio of 1.41 for any ACE exposure.^26 An ACE score of 4 or higher triples a woman's risk of developing preeclampsia.[^27] An ACE score of 6 reduces life expectancy by 20 years.[^28] California's Medi-Cal ACE Aware program, active since 2020, remains the only state-level initiative with reimbursable screening linked to trained referral infrastructure — a model no other state has replicated.

The crises documented in this chapter — the 5.7 million children who lost Medicaid[^1], the 17,203 children in illegal labor, the 14.1 million children in food-insecure households[^4], the 185,000 citizen children experiencing parental deportation, the half-million children with elevated blood lead[^6] — are all accumulating ACEs right now.

This is the pipeline. Childhood adversity feeds into the adolescent mental health crisis. That distress feeds into substance use, which feeds into chronic disease, which feeds into pregnancy complications, which feeds into maternal death. Firearms are now the leading cause of death for children ages one to nineteen — 4,752 pediatric firearm deaths in 2022[^29], with Black children facing a firearm homicide rate 20 times higher than white children.

The maternal mortality crisis of 2035 and 2040 is being built today. We know precisely how many children are entering the pipeline, and we know exactly what will happen to them when they reach the end of it.

Chapter 9: Sexual Violence — The Scale Nobody Will Say Out Loud

The patient came in for flank pain and a fever. She was pregnant. She had recurrent kidney infections that no one had ever worked up — septic twice during her first pregnancy, UTIs since she was thirteen. That was the medical reason for the visit.

But the reason the physician was still in the room thirty minutes later was not the kidney. It was the gun.

Her partner of ten years had started sleeping with someone else when he found out about the pregnancy. Things got bad fast. He jumped her while she was pregnant. Three days later, he sent his girlfriend and three of her friends to jump her again. The night of the first assault, he loaded a gun on her. He was wishing death on their unborn son. He burned all of their ultrasound pictures. He was using drugs and drinking.

This was not her first partner to do this. A previous partner had loaded a gun on her and her older son three separate times. She had gotten a DVO — a domestic violence order — against that one.

She lived alone with her older child. She was a bartender and a stripper. She had one trusted friend. Her mother lived an hour away. Her rent was about to double. She had no family support system, no safe place to go, and a baby coming.

The physician told her: "The number one cause of death for pregnant women is murder." Not hemorrhage. Not preeclampsia. Murder.

Then the physician did what the system does not provide anyone else to do. She became the social worker, the legal advisor, the safety planner, and the domestic violence counselor — all in a prenatal visit, between ultrasounds. She told the patient to hide her documents at a friend's house — passport, ID, birth certificates, money. She told her to set aside ten dollars a week. She told her to tell someone. She texted her own nonprofit contacts. She printed out local domestic violence numbers. She acknowledged, to her team afterward, that "you just don't have great success with our social workers."

The clinical note became a safety plan. The prescription pad became a resource list. The twenty-minute prenatal visit became a thirty-minute crisis intervention. And the next patient was waiting.

There is no billing code for this. There is no department for this. There is no referral pathway that works reliably for this. The physician does it because if she does not, no one will. And the patient knows it. She came in for her kidneys, and she told her physician about the gun, because there was no one else to tell.

The Prevalence Numbers

54.3% of women in the United States have experienced contact sexual violence in their lifetime. That is the CDC's National Intimate Partner and Sexual Violence Survey. More than half of all women. 26.8% have experienced completed or attempted rape. 443,000 or more people aged 12 and older are sexually assaulted or raped each year. 75% of those assaults go unreported. At least 225,000 rape kits sit untested in evidence rooms across the country. And the forensic infrastructure to respond is collapsing — as documented later in this chapter.

79% of female rape survivors were first raped before age 25. 40% before age 18. The perpetrator is known to the victim in over 80% of cases. Less than one-third report to police. The CDC estimates the lifetime economic cost at $122,461 per survivor — totaling over $3.1 trillion annually.

Nearly 20 people per minute are physically abused by an intimate partner — more than 10 million women per year. The CDC estimates over 2,300 intimate partner homicides of women annually; 3,991 female IPV homicide victims were documented between 2018 and 2021 alone. 66.6% involved firearms. 98.5% had a male suspect. 68% occurred at the victim's residence. Sexual violence precedes 70% of femicides. There is no federal femicide tracking database.

The Racial Disparity in Victimization and Justice

The numbers are not race-neutral. Black women experience sexual violence at 35% higher rates than white women, according to the CDC's NISVS. Black women aged 25 to 44 are six times more likely to be murdered than white women of the same age. American Indian and Alaska Native women report the highest lifetime rape prevalence of any racial group — 34.1%, compared to 22% for Black women, 18.8% for white women, and 14.6% for Latina women.

But the disparity does not end at victimization. It compounds at every stage of the response. Black survivors of sexual violence are 40% less likely to have their cases prosecuted than white survivors — a disparity that persists after controlling for assault type, location, and relationship to perpetrator. Researchers have documented a "Black woman believability gap" across criminal legal systems: police are less likely to classify reports from Black women as founded, district attorneys are less likely to bring charges, and juries are less likely to convict. Cases with Black suspects, by contrast, are prosecuted at higher rates regardless of the survivor's race.

Latina survivors face compounding barriers of a different architecture. Familismo norms, deportation fear, language access gaps, and police mistrust drive formal service utilization 50% lower than for non-Latina white survivors. Seventy percent of Latina survivors report family pressure not to seek services. Undocumented Latina women face the most extreme version of this trap — seeking justice means risking deportation. The U-visa program, designed to protect immigrant crime victims, has a backlog of over 350,000 applications against a statutory cap of 10,000 approvals per year. Survivors wait five or more years in unauthorized status, vulnerable to re-victimization the entire time.

Homicide Is the Leading Cause of Maternal Death

Pregnancy-associated homicide exceeds all leading clinical causes of maternal death — hemorrhage, hypertension, infection, cardiomyopathy — by more than two times. The rate is 3.62 per 100,000 live births. Black women aged 18 to 24 die from pregnancy-associated homicide at 8 per 100,000 — four times the national average. According to a JAMA Network Open analysis, 76% of pregnancy-associated homicides involve firearms.

These are not clinical failures. These are violence failures. Most maternal health policy focuses on clinical causes of death. The thing that actually kills more pregnant women than any single clinical cause — their partners — is absent from the policy response. States that prohibit divorce finalization during pregnancy exhibit significantly higher rates of pregnancy-associated intimate partner homicide. The law traps women in the marriage; the marriage traps women in the violence.

Non-fatal strangulation — the signature of sexual domination within intimate partner violence — is a direct predictor of lethality. A survivor of non-fatal strangulation by an intimate partner is 7.5 times more likely to be killed by that partner. Strangulation precedes 25% of femicide cases, often leaves no visible marks, and causes traumatic brain injury in an estimated 50% of cases. The TBI itself can impair the survivor's ability to testify coherently, which then undermines the prosecution. Thirty-five states have enacted strangulation-specific felony statutes. But statutes without enforcement are words on paper.

The Dobbs-Violence Connection

NBER and Tulane researchers documented a 7-10% increase in intimate partner violence in ban counties compared to non-ban counties, producing an estimated 9,000 to nearly 14,000 additional domestic violence incidents nationally — with an estimated $1.24 billion in social costs. Each additional state abortion restriction correlates with a 3.4% increase in the intimate partner violence homicide rate. When a woman cannot leave a pregnancy, she cannot leave the relationship. The pregnancy becomes a tool of control. The violence escalates. This is measured, published, peer-reviewed research. The UCSF Turnaway Study confirmed the mechanism longitudinally: women denied the abortion they sought stayed in contact with violent partners at significantly higher rates and experienced more subsequent intimate partner violence than women who received the care they needed.

Fourteen states with near-total abortion bans include no rape or incest exceptions. Guttmacher estimates 64,000 rape-related pregnancies occur annually in ban states, with 32,000 additional rape-related pregnancies forced to term in the first year after Dobbs alone. Survivors in ban states travel an average of 700 miles to reach an abortion provider. In six states, a police report is required to access even a nominal rape exception — but nationally, 75% of sexual assaults go unreported. The exception is structurally inaccessible to three-quarters of the women it claims to protect.

The geographic concentration of Black women in ban states compounds every disparity. Black women in Florida, Texas, and Georgia account for 44% of Black women in ban states and one-quarter of all Black women of reproductive age nationally. All three states have above-average maternal mortality rates, the strictest bans, and the most aggressive safety-net dismantlement.

Reproductive Coercion and the Control Architecture

Reproductive coercion is not an edge case within intimate partner violence — it is one of its primary mechanisms. Traffickers use forced pregnancy as a control tactic in 60% of sex trafficking cases reviewed by the Polaris Project. In 15 states without rape or incest exceptions, trafficking survivors are denied abortion access entirely. The documentation requirements that exist in other states — police reports, identity verification, immigration status — are precisely the instruments a trafficker controls. The survivor cannot access the exception because the exception was designed for someone who is not being trafficked.

Healthcare providers identify fewer than 1% of trafficking victims, despite the fact that 88% of trafficking survivors have contact with a healthcare facility during their exploitation — an average of 4.5 encounters before anyone asks the right question. The training gap is not incidental. It is the difference between a clinical encounter and a rescue.

Emergency contraception, which prevents 89% of pregnancies when taken within 24 hours and 52% within 72 hours, could prevent an estimated 25,000 rape-related pregnancies annually. But conscience clause expansions in 2025 allow hospitals to refuse EC to assault survivors without a transfer mandate. Advocacy surveys report that 15% of ERs in abortion-ban states are declining to provide emergency contraception to sexual assault survivors. In rural areas where the ER is the only acute care option, refusal is tantamount to forced pregnancy.

Child Marriage: The Legalized Pipeline

Child marriage remains legal in 34 U.S. states as of mid-2025. Between 2000 and 2021, at least 315,000 minors were legally married in the United States. 86% were girls, disproportionately married to adult men under parental and judicial consent statutes. Four states have no statutory minimum marriage age when all exemptions — parental, judicial, pregnancy, religious — are applied. A child of any age can be legally married.

The reform trajectory is real but slow. Complete bans on child marriage increased from two states in 2018 (Delaware and New Jersey) to 16 by mid-2025. The Federal Child Marriage Prevention Act of 2024 has not advanced through Congress. There is no federal marriage-age floor. And the geography of reform maps almost perfectly onto the geography of reproductive rights: 15 of the 16 states that have banned child marriage also preserve abortion access. Missouri is the sole exception. In the states where a minor rape victim can be forced to carry a pregnancy to term, she can also be legally married to her rapist.

The Justice Gap: IPV Impunity and the Criminalization of Survival

The criminal justice system's response to intimate partner violence fails women at every stage. Only approximately 14% of reported IPV cases result in criminal conviction. 53% of IPV incidents are never reported to police. Nine states still retain marital rape exemptions or lesser penalties for spousal rape. The men who kill pregnant women are almost never held accountable before the homicide occurs.

When women act to protect themselves, the system reverses. 94% of incarcerated women have experienced domestic violence. 74% of women imprisoned for murder or manslaughter had experienced intimate partner violence within a year of the offense that led to their conviction. At least 30% of women serving time for murder or manslaughter — including those with life sentences — were acting in self-defense or protecting a child during the incident. Most state laws do not provide meaningful opportunity to assert self-defense during prosecution or to present abuse history as a mitigating factor in sentencing.

The incarceration rate for women is growing twice as fast as that for men. Black, Latina, American Indian, and Alaska Native women are disproportionately represented. The system does not prevent the violence, does not prosecute the men, and imprisons the women who survive.

Campus and Military Sexual Violence

25.9% of female undergraduate students experienced nonconsensual sexual contact according to the Association of American Universities campus climate survey. The CDC's 2025 Youth Risk Behavior Survey found that 12% of female high school students reported forced sex in the past year — rising to 22% for LGBTQ+ youth. Title IX protections were expanded in 2022 to include LGBTQ+ protections and updated investigation standards. In January 2025, those expanded protections were fully rolled back, restoring the 2020 DeVos-era rule that narrowed the definition of sexual harassment and reinstated live hearing cross-examination of survivors — a procedural mechanism that research consistently shows re-traumatizes victims and reduces reporting. Students of color face compounded barriers, with documented racial disparities in campus adjudication outcomes.

Military sexual assault affects an estimated 134,000 service members per year when unreported cases are included, according to the DoD's own SAPRO data showing a 73% non-reporting rate. Female service members are 3.8 times more likely to be sexually assaulted than male peers. The VA reports that 42% of women veterans screen positive for military sexual trauma, with MST-related mental health treatment utilization increasing 28% in a single year. The VA abortion ban, enacted December 2025, contains no exception for rape or incest — affecting 460,000 women veterans of reproductive age, including survivors of military sexual trauma. Over 2 million women total receive VA healthcare.

Missing and Murdered Indigenous Women

In 2024, 10,248 reports of missing Indigenous persons were filed with the FBI's NCIC. Murder is the third leading cause of death for Indigenous women and girls, at rates 10 times the national average. 84% of Indigenous women have experienced violence in their lifetime; 56.1% sexual violence specifically. They are 2.5 times more likely to experience sexual violence than any other racial group, with 96% of perpetrators being non-Native — and until 2013, tribal courts lacked jurisdiction over non-Native offenders.

VAWA 2022 expanded tribal jurisdiction, and Savanna's Act improved NCIC reporting of AIAN missing persons by 23%.[^1] But implementation has been incomplete. The Indian Health Service — the only federal healthcare system serving these communities — has been targeted for $900 million in cuts; twelve IHS facilities have closed since March 2025.[^2] The Urban Indian Health Institute maintains its own independent data because federal databases cannot be trusted. And the children who witness this violence carry it forward: Native children have PTSD rates three times the general population, shaped by intergenerational trauma from the boarding school system and ongoing exposure to domestic violence.

Disability, Deafness, and Sexual Violence

The populations least visible in sexual violence data are often the most victimized. People with disabilities experience sexual violence at two to ten times the rate of non-disabled populations.[^3] Among women with intellectual disabilities, the lifetime prevalence reaches 83%.[^4] Dependency on caregivers, communication barriers, and power differentials create compounding vulnerability. Compulsory sterilization persists: 31 states still permit court-ordered sterilization of adults with intellectual disabilities, with approximately 600 sterilizations ordered annually.[^5]

Deaf and hard-of-hearing survivors experience sexual violence at 1.5 to 2 times general population rates[^6], yet only 5% of SANE programs offer ASL-fluent providers for forensic exams.[^7] Sixty-two percent of SANE programs lack non-Spanish interpreter services entirely, and only 18% offer real-time interpretation for forensic interviews.[^8] Language access is the difference between evidence that holds up in court and evidence that does not.

The FACE Act Pardons and the New Enforcement Reality

In January 2025, 23 individuals convicted under the FACE Act for violence against reproductive health clinics were pardoned.[^9] In June 2025, HIPAA reproductive privacy protections were vacated in Purl v. HHS. EMTALA emergency abortion guidance was rescinded. A pregnant woman walking into a hospital now faces risks that did not exist three years ago: her medical records can be subpoenaed, her physician may be legally obligated to report her, the clinic has no federal protection from violence, and the DOJ has dismissed pending FACE Act cases protecting abortion clinics while simultaneously prosecuting church intrusions under the same statute. Violence against reproductive healthcare facilities is no longer a federal enforcement priority.

Immigration Enforcement as a Maternal Health Threat

Since the January 2025 executive orders expanding immigration enforcement, prenatal visits dropped 40-60% in border regions after ICE removed the sensitive-locations buffer. Physicians at Hennepin Healthcare in Minneapolis reported patients with high-risk pregnancies going into labor at home rather than coming to the hospital. The chilling effect extends to crime reporting: undocumented crime reporting dropped 40% in high-ICE-enforcement jurisdictions in 2025.[^10] An undocumented sexual assault survivor who shows up to testify may be detained at the courthouse. DHS OIG documented 14,340 sexual abuse complaints at immigration facilities over a five-year period, with 90% not properly investigated[^11] and complainants held 16% longer than non-complainants.

Trump 1.0 ended presumptive release for pregnant detainees in 2017 — resulting in over 4,600 pregnant women detained by 2018, documented miscarriages, and reported shackling. Biden reversed the policy. Trump 2.0 reinstated it. The 2025 detention death toll reached 33 — the highest since ICE began publishing records — with 95% documented as preventable.[^12]

The SANE Shortage: No One to Collect the Evidence

The forensic nursing infrastructure is failing sexual assault survivors at scale. Fewer than 25% of U.S. hospitals have a trained Sexual Assault Nurse Examiner available — roughly 970 SANE programs across 6,146 hospitals.[^13] Only 39% of hospitals with any SANE capacity have 24/7 availability.[^14] In rural areas, certified SANEs are present in only 16.7% of counties[^15], according to Penn State University research. An RTI International analysis found rural jurisdictions take three times longer to submit rape kits for testing, and 45% of rural law enforcement agencies lack written protocols for evidence collection.[^16] Rural sexual assault cases are 50% less likely to result in arrest.[^17] There are an estimated 3,000 SANE vacancies nationally.[^18]

A rape survivor in much of the country faces a choice: drive hours to a hospital with SANE capability, or go to the nearest ER where no one is trained to conduct a forensic exam — meaning the evidence that could lead to prosecution is never collected.

The shortage is compounded by funding. SANE programs rely on a patchwork of federal VAWA and state grants. The Violence Against Women Act reauthorization in 2022 expanded funding, but the Trump FY2026 budget proposes a 40% cut to VAWA grant programs — \$258 million slashed from the \$644 million FY2025 Office on Violence Against Women allocation.[^19] That funding supports approximately 1,600 rape crisis centers and SANE training programs. The proposed cut would force closures and eliminate forensic nursing positions in the communities that need them most. Meanwhile, sexual assault kit backlogs remain massive — at least 225,000 untested kits sit in evidence rooms[^20], with Black and Latina survivors overrepresented in backlogged jurisdictions concentrated in under-resourced urban labs. The evidence exists. No one is processing it.

The Sextortion-to-Pregnancy Pipeline

Sextortion — the online blackmail of minors using sexual images — has exploded to 26,718 reports to NCMEC by 2023.^21 CSAM reports to the CyberTipline surged from 16.9 million in 2019 to 36.2 million in 2024 — a 114% increase^22, with AI-generated content now accounting for over 20% of reports.[^23] Financial sextortion has been linked to at least 20 documented youth suicides. The pipeline extends into reproductive health: sextortion victims are disproportionately adolescent girls simultaneously losing access to sex education, contraception, and abortion care. An adolescent coerced into sexual activity through digital exploitation, in a ban state with abstinence-only education and no contraceptive access, faces a forced pregnancy originating from sexual violence — with no legal recourse and potential prosecution. The sextortion crisis is the entry point of a pipeline that feeds directly into the maternal mortality statistics documented in Chapter 1.

The Service Gap: Demand Without Capacity

National domestic violence and sexual assault programs turned away 11,105 service requests in a single 24-hour census count in 2023.[^24] Seventy-three percent were for emergency shelter.[^25] Sexual assault hotline calls have increased 23% since 2020.^26 Housing instability and sexual violence form a bidirectional spiral: 38% of homeless women report sexual assault as the direct cause of their homelessness, while one in three homeless youth experiences sexual assault within 48 hours of their first street episode.[^27]

Sexual violence survivors have three times the PTSD rate, four times the suicide attempt rate, and three times the depression rate of non-survivors.[^28] They are 13 times more likely to abuse alcohol.[^29] These are chronic conditions requiring sustained treatment — treatment the system is simultaneously defunding. Forty-two percent of low-income survivors cite cost as the primary barrier to services.[^30] Crime victim compensation covers medical costs but not lost wages in 30 states.[^31] The system demands that survivors absorb the economic consequences of someone else's crime.

Chapter 10: The Immigration Crisis

A physician who treats pregnant women learns, eventually, what it looks like when patients start disappearing.

It does not happen all at once. It happens appointment by appointment. A woman misses her twenty-eight-week visit. She had been coming reliably — every four weeks, then every two. Her glucose screen was abnormal; she needed follow-up. She does not reschedule. When the front desk calls, the phone rings and no one picks up. The chart stays open. The glucose stays unmanaged. The physician finds out what happened, eventually, from another patient who knows her — she went into labor at home because she was afraid to come to the hospital.

Physicians at Hennepin Healthcare in Minneapolis reported exactly this: patients with high-risk pregnancies laboring at home rather than coming in. The reason was not poverty. It was not distance. It was not cultural preference. It was ICE. On January 20, 2025, the Department of Homeland Security rescinded the sensitive locations policy that had, for decades, protected hospitals and clinics from immigration enforcement. The message was received instantly, and it was received by every undocumented pregnant woman in the country: the place you go to have your baby is now the place where they can come to take you.

In border regions, prenatal visits dropped 40 to 60 percent. Women called their clinics before appointments — not to reschedule, but to ask whether agents had been seen in the area. The clinical question was no longer "How far along are you?" It was "Is it safe to leave the house?"

The physician cannot treat a patient who does not come. Every missed visit is a missed diagnosis — gestational diabetes unmanaged, preeclampsia undetected, fetal growth restriction uncaught. Every missed diagnosis becomes a complication. Every complication becomes, in a system designed to deny these women coverage, an Emergency Medicaid presentation — the most expensive and most dangerous way to give birth.

The Invisible Patients

There is no reliable count of how many undocumented pregnant women live in the United States. What is documented is this: undocumented women are categorically excluded from Medicaid in most states, cannot safely access clinics in the current enforcement climate, and cannot appear in the courthouses where protective orders are obtained without risking their own deportation. They exist in a healthcare system that simultaneously counts on their labor and refuses to count their deaths.

The reproductive health crisis for immigrant women does not begin at the border. It begins at the internal CBP checkpoints that create de facto internal borders, making it impossible for undocumented women in border-adjacent communities to travel to the nearest hospital, OB-GYN, or domestic violence shelter without passing through federal enforcement infrastructure. A pregnant woman in the Rio Grande Valley who needs specialty care in San Antonio must pass through a checkpoint. Many don't go. The resulting avoidance of prenatal care is not a personal choice — it is a rational survival calculation in a system that has made healthcare access and deportation risk inseparable. According to the Guttmacher Institute, an estimated 72 percent of undocumented women in abortion-restrictive states report an inability to travel to abortion-access states because of these interior checkpoints — a barrier that traps roughly 40,000 additional women per year inside states that have banned or severely restricted abortion care.[^2]

The chilling effect is now quantified. A 2026 analysis by the Center for American Progress found that 41 percent of federally qualified health centers in high-enforcement ZIP codes reported immigrant patient no-shows directly attributed to ICE fear.[^1] First-trimester prenatal care initiation among Medicaid-enrolled immigrants dropped 23 percent following the rescission of ICE's sensitive locations policy.[^1] Women are calling clinics to ask whether ICE has been spotted in the area before deciding whether to come in for a prenatal visit. This is the triage system the enforcement state has created — one in which the first clinical question is not "How far along are you?" but "Is it safe to leave the house?"

The Erosion of the Immigrant Advantage

Epidemiologists have long documented an "immigrant advantage" — the paradox that newly arrived immigrants often have better birth outcomes than native-born Americans of the same demographic profile, despite lower income and reduced healthcare access. The advantage is attributed to strong social networks, healthier dietary patterns, and lower rates of substance use. The numbers are substantial: according to ACOG and data published in the American Journal of Obstetrics and Gynecology, the foreign-born overall maternal mortality ratio stands at 14.3 per 100,000 live births, compared to 22.7 for US-born women.[^3]

The advantage disappears with time in the United States — and it disappears fastest for the women who need it most. The longer an immigrant woman lives in this country, the worse her birth outcomes become, her risk profile converging with and eventually mirroring the disparities experienced by native-born women of her racial and ethnic group. Black immigrant women lose the healthy immigrant effect most rapidly, with a maternal mortality ratio of 31.2 per 100,000.[^3] The current enforcement environment is accelerating this erosion. In high-enforcement states, the advantage has not merely eroded — it has reversed. Undocumented Latina women in these states now have an estimated maternal mortality ratio of 26.4 per 100,000, exceeding that of US-born Latinas.[^3] The stress of immigration enforcement, social isolation produced by fear of public activity, and avoidance of institutions that could provide support are the mechanisms through which the advantage collapses. In the current climate, the advantage may not survive first contact with the American healthcare system at all.

The biological pathways are becoming clearer. A study published in JAMA Psychiatry found that 71 percent of pregnant undocumented women in enforcement-heavy communities screen positive for anxiety, 54 percent for depression, and 38 percent for prenatal PTSD — compared to 11 percent in the general perinatal population.[^4] Chronic immigration enforcement stress activates the HPA axis, elevating cortisol throughout pregnancy, creating measurable pathways to preterm birth and disrupted fetal neurodevelopment. Infants born to enforcement-exposed mothers show elevated cortisol at six months of age — the stress response dysregulated before the child can speak. A University of Michigan analysis found that preterm birth rates among undocumented Latina mothers have risen to 11.4 percent, up from 8.9 percent in 2022[^5], with county-level ICE enforcement intensity used as a natural experiment for causal identification. The healthy immigrant effect in birth outcomes is now reversed for undocumented Latinas as a population.

Mixed-Status Families and the Arithmetic of Fear

The numbers begin with children. According to the Migration Policy Institute, 4.4 million US-citizen children live with at least one undocumented parent.[^6] These children are American citizens whose healthcare is held hostage by their parents' immigration status. When an undocumented mother avoids a clinic, her citizen child also misses well-child visits, vaccinations, and developmental screenings.

Mixed-status family structures reshape reproductive decision-making itself. Nearly half — 48 percent — of mixed-status couples report that immigration anxiety directly affects their reproductive decisions.[^6] A third have delayed planned additional children to reduce household vulnerability.[^6] Nineteen percent report using contraception more consistently specifically because of enforcement fear[^6] — not as reproductive autonomy, but as threat mitigation. Some families move in the opposite direction: 22 percent of DACA recipients surveyed by the National Immigration Law Center chose earlier childbearing than planned, hastening pregnancy before anticipated deportation.[^7] Fifty-eight percent of DACA recipients — now aged 26 to 38, in their peak reproductive years — changed or delayed reproductive health decisions because of program uncertainty.[^7] Thirty-one percent discontinued long-term birth control to "prepare for pregnancy before deportation."[^7] This is reproductive planning conducted under duress, family formation distorted by the state.

The birthright citizenship executive order issued in January 2025 intensified the crisis. Thirty-seven percent of pregnant undocumented women surveyed reported changing their plans about prenatal care enrollment in response[^8], and Medicaid prenatal enrollment for undocumented women dropped 19 percent following the announcement[^8] — despite the order being immediately challenged in court. Twenty-four percent of women surveyed considered delivering outside the United States entirely.[^8] The chilling effect does not require a policy to take effect. The announcement is sufficient.

Detention Conditions

In May 2025, CBP eliminated protections for pregnant detainees — stripping away requirements for water, food, formula, and diapers that had been established under prior administrations. The consequences are now documented in government data obtained through FOIA. As of February 16, 2026, 86 pregnant women were being held in ICE detention, including 9 in their third trimester.[^9] Between January and September 2025 alone, 16 miscarriages occurred in ICE custody.[^10] In the fourteen months from January 2025 through February 2026, 363 pregnant, postpartum, or nursing immigrants were deported.[^10] Women reported being shackled and restrained while actively miscarrying. One woman was deported 10 days after being diagnosed with a missed miscarriage — without receiving treatment in the United States — and required emergency care in Honduras.[^9]

Eighty-nine percent of pregnant asylum seekers report receiving no prenatal care during CBP detention, where the median custody time for pregnant women reached 18 days in early 2026.[^11] The maternal complication rate in custody is estimated at three to five times the general population rate.[^11] Sworn testimonies document pregnant women experiencing miscarriage in detention cells without medical attention. ICE detention facilities are not bound by the same healthcare standards as state or federal prisons — and those standards, as this report documents in its chapter on incarceration, are already inadequate.

The intersection of immigration detention and reproductive health produces a unique category of harm: women who are simultaneously subject to carceral health standards and immigration administrative processes, with no guaranteed access to prenatal care, no clear pathway to abortion where it remains legal, and no advocate in the system whose mandate is the health of the pregnant detainee rather than the enforcement of immigration law. ACLU documentation found 18 cases of detained immigrant women denied timely abortion access in 2023-2024, with an average delay from request to access of 22 days.[^12] Six women exceeded gestational limits due to the delay.[^12] Four were transferred to more restrictive states while their abortion request was pending.[^12]

The Irwin County Detention Center case has continued to expand. The 2020 nurse whistleblower who reported non-consensual gynecological procedures on detained immigrant women exposed a pattern that, by 2026, has grown to more than 40 identified women who received procedures — including hysterectomies and oophorectomies — without adequate informed consent.[^13] Non-English-speaking women signed consent documents they could not read. Fourteen active lawsuits are now pending.[^13] The contractor physician lost his medical license. This is not a rogue actor. It is the predictable outcome of a system that concentrates power over reproductive bodies in facilities with no independent medical ethics oversight.

The Coverage Architecture

The 5-year bar on Medicaid eligibility for legal immigrants — even green card holders — means that women who have followed every legal process available to them are still denied the coverage that funds prenatal care, delivery, and postpartum mental health treatment. Emergency Medicaid covers the delivery itself in most states, creating a system in which the government will pay for the most expensive moment of a pregnancy (the birth) but refuses to fund the prenatal care that would have prevented complications making that birth more expensive. The data confirms the absurdity: according to the Urban Institute and Health Affairs, 44 percent of undocumented births are now Emergency Medicaid-only.[^14] Emergency C-section rates for these births run at 31 percent, compared to 22 percent for births with full Medicaid prenatal coverage.[^14] Hemorrhage rates are double — 18 percent versus 9 percent.[^14] ICU admissions run four times higher.[^14] The average hospital cost for an Emergency Medicaid birth is $14,200, compared to $8,400[^14] for a birth preceded by full prenatal care. The system pays more to produce worse outcomes.

States vary in their coverage of immigrant women. Some have extended state-funded coverage to undocumented pregnant women; others have not. The result is a postcode lottery layered on top of an immigration status lottery — a pregnant woman's access to care depends on which state she lives in, which documents she holds, and whether she can reach a clinic without passing through an enforcement checkpoint.

The public charge rule has weaponized even the coverage that exists. After the Trump administration expanded the rule in 2025 to include Medicaid, SNAP, and other public benefits as factors weighing against green card applications, the Kaiser Family Foundation found that 34 percent of low-income immigrant women withdrew from or declined Medicaid.^15 Twenty-nine percent stopped using Title X-funded family planning clinics.^15 Eighteen percent skipped prenatal visits entirely.^15 The original 2019 public charge rule announcement — before the rule even took effect — drove 13.8 percent of immigrant adults in mixed-status families to disenroll from Medicaid and prenatal care. Hispanic and Latino families were hit hardest, with 15.2 percent avoiding benefits. The rule was eventually blocked and withdrawn, but the chilling effects persisted for years. Fear, once introduced into a community's relationship with its healthcare system, does not retract on the same timeline as the policy that created it.

DACA, TPS, and the Contraception Collapse

Two hundred fifty thousand women hold Temporary Protected Status[^16] for countries including El Salvador, Honduras, Haiti, and Venezuela. As the Trump administration terminates or fails to renew TPS designations, these women — many of whom have lived legally in the United States for 15 to 25 years — lose work authorization, employer-sponsored insurance, and with it, access to contraception and prenatal care. The National TPS Alliance found that 29 percent of TPS-holding women of reproductive age discontinued hormonal contraception following the loss of employer insurance tied to work-authorization lapse.[^16] Thirty-eight percent changed their reproductive plans entirely.[^16]

The contraception collapse extends beyond TPS holders. According to the Guttmacher Institute and the National Latina Institute for Reproductive Justice, the unintended pregnancy rate among undocumented immigrant women rose 34 percent between 2022 and 2025 — from 47 to 63 per 1,000 women.[^17] LARC uptake dropped 41 percent[^17], specifically because LARCs require a provider visit for insertion, making them the most avoidance-sensitive method. Multiple mechanisms converge: clinic avoidance from ICE fear, Title X clinic closures, public charge deterrence, and community isolation. Planned Parenthood closed 32 clinics in 2026 across ten states.[^18] Eight hundred seventy-nine Title X clinics — 24 percent of the national total — had their funding withheld pending a so-called civil rights review by HHS, even after Congress kept Title X funding steady in the February bipartisan budget.[^18] The infrastructure that provided contraception to immigrant women is being systematically dismantled.

The "Abortion Trafficking" Framework

Multiple states have introduced or enacted "abortion trafficking" laws that criminalize assisting minors — and in some proposed versions, any person — in traveling across state lines for abortion care. For immigrant women in border states with complete abortion bans, this legal architecture creates a double bind: the nearest legal abortion may be across a state line that new legislation aims to make uncrossable, while the nearest international border leads to a country whose healthcare system may be more accessible but whose crossing triggers re-entry risks. Before Dobbs, a 2018 border community study found that 22 percent of immigrant women were already crossing into Mexico to access reproductive healthcare services including abortion and contraception[^19] — a shadow cross-border system driven by US insurance exclusions and cost barriers. Dobbs made the shadow system the only system.

Immigrant women in abortion-ban states are 3.1 times less likely to access out-of-state abortion compared to US-born women in those same states.[^19] Sixty-eight percent of undocumented women in ban states who sought abortion in 2023-2024 could not access it.[^19] Sixty-one percent of immigrant women in ban states who had previously accessed abortion did so via medication abortion — a pathway now eliminated or severely restricted in 21 states.[^20]

The chilling effect extends beyond abortion. Community health centers that serve immigrant populations report declining utilization across all services — not just reproductive health — as fear of data sharing between health systems and immigration enforcement agencies suppresses healthcare-seeking behavior broadly. Cervical cancer screening rates among undocumented immigrant women stand at 54 percent, compared to 83 percent among US-born women.[^21] Late-stage diagnosis runs at 48 percent versus 29 percent.[^21] These are women dying of a cancer that is almost entirely preventable through routine Pap smears — screenings they are too afraid to seek. Women who avoid clinics for fear of deportation are not only forgoing prenatal care. They are forgoing cancer screening, STI treatment, contraception, and well-child visits for their U.S.-citizen children.

Separated Families, Invisible Children

Mass deportation operations do not pause for postpartum status. In the first quarter of 2026, 47 documented cases emerged of postpartum women deported within eight weeks of delivery.[^22] Twelve US-citizen newborns were left with relatives after maternal deportation.[^22] Breastfeeding cessation occurred in 100 percent of deportation-separated mother-infant pairs.[^22] Two deported mothers died from unmanaged postpartum complications in their countries of origin. Forced separation disrupts neonatal bonding, postpartum mental health, and the basic biological relationship between a mother and her newborn.

The children who remain face their own crisis. Over 37,000 unaccompanied minors were in ORR custody in fiscal year 2023.[^23] Thirty-one percent of girls in ORR custody reported sexual assault during their migration journey.[^23] Access to Plan B and emergency contraception was blocked in 43 percent of ORR facilities — many of which are operated by faith-based contractors that refuse reproductive healthcare on religious grounds. Abortion access was denied or severely delayed in all documented cases. These are girls who were raped during the most dangerous journey of their lives, housed by a government that will not provide them emergency contraception.

Meanwhile, 130,000 unaccompanied minors entered the workforce, with 66 percent working full-time as of 2022.[^24] Child labor violations surged 88 percent since 2019, reaching 5,792 children in violation in fiscal year 2023.[^24] The pipeline is seamless: children arrive unaccompanied, are placed in ORR custody where their reproductive health needs are ignored, are released to sponsors where oversight collapses, and enter exploitative labor where their bodies are consumed by an economy that refuses to acknowledge they exist.

The Sanctuary Evidence

The data makes the case for sanctuary. A Harvard T.H. Chan School of Public Health analysis published in Health Affairs found that immigrant women in sanctuary jurisdictions are 2.3 times more likely to initiate prenatal care in the first trimester compared to women in non-sanctuary areas.[^25] Emergency-only obstetric presentations are 47 percent lower.[^25] Late-stage cervical cancer diagnoses are 31 percent lower.[^25] The effect persists after controlling for socioeconomic status and insurance coverage. Sanctuary policies do not create a parallel healthcare system. They create the conditions under which immigrant women can use the system that already exists.

What the Administration Is Doing

The Trump administration's immigration enforcement posture and its reproductive health policy agenda are producing compounding effects that no prior administration has generated at this scale. The same government that eliminated pregnant detainee protections is the government that reinstated the Global Gag Rule as a "Mega-Gag" applying to $30 billion in foreign assistance — affecting the reproductive healthcare infrastructure of the countries these women fled. The same government that operates internal checkpoints preventing access to care is the government that banned undocumented children from Head Start, producing a 20 percent enrollment decline in some counties and removing the early childhood infrastructure that serves the U.S.-citizen children of undocumented parents.

The policy is coherent: restrict reproductive access for immigrant women at the border, in detention, within the country's interior, and in the countries of origin that would have provided care had these women been able to stay. There is no point in this system where an undocumented pregnant woman encounters a policy designed to help her. Every policy she encounters is designed to exclude her — from coverage, from care, from data, and from the count that would make her visible.

The Other Invisible Patients: Women Veterans

Over 900,000 women veterans are now enrolled in VA healthcare — the fastest-growing segment of the veteran population, with 50,000 new enrollees in a single year (2023-2024).[^26] In fiscal year 2024, a record 741,259 women veterans received VA compensation payments totaling $20.4 billion in benefits.[^26]

On December 22, 2025, the Trump administration implemented a near-total ban on VA-provided abortion services.[^26] The Biden-era policy had allowed VA abortion care in cases of rape, incest, or threat to the life or health of the veteran. The new policy, based on a DOJ memo, now permits abortion only in narrow life-saving circumstances — effectively eliminating coverage for abortions resulting from rape or incest. The ban also prohibits VA providers from offering abortion counseling.

The cruelty is specific: the women most directly affected are those who experienced military sexual trauma — the same population the VA was designed to serve. A woman veteran who was raped during active duty, who is enrolled in VA healthcare, cannot access abortion care through the healthcare system that exists because of her service. She cannot even receive counseling about her options from her VA provider. The ban does not merely restrict a procedure. It silences the providers who might help her navigate a situation that the military itself created.
