Chapter 11: The Corruption — Where the Money Actually Went

The money did not disappear. It was redirected.

The physician knew what she was seeing.

A colleague was performing non-indicated cesarean sections. Not emergencies. Not cases where the clinical trajectory had deteriorated and the decision to operate was the only remaining option. Cases where the operation was chosen because it was faster, or because it billed better, or because no one was going to question it.

She did not report it. There was no mechanism that would make reporting effective and no protection for the person who tried. What she did was dictate her observations into a device she carried in her pocket — building a record of what medicine looked like from inside, for the day when someone might care.

"Some colleagues perform non-indicated C-sections," she said. She contrasted this with her own training, which had emphasized preventing primary cesareans — keeping the scalpel in the drawer when it wasn't needed, even when the schedule and the billing system both incentivized taking it out. "There is a disparity in care quality for marginalized groups who cannot hold institutions accountable."

That sentence is the thesis of this chapter. The corruption in American maternal health is not a scandal. It is a business model. The patients who cannot hold the system accountable — the patients on Medicaid, the patients without advocates, the patients who do not know they have alternatives — receive care calibrated to the system's convenience, not to their survival. The physician who tries to provide equitable care is penalized for it. The physician who does not is rewarded.

"I strive to provide the same quality of care to all patients, regardless of insurance status," she said. Then, with the sadness of someone who has watched from inside: "The system often does not support ethical and safe practice."

Crisis Pregnancy Centers: Not Medical Facilities

Texas diverted $140 million from medical research to fund crisis pregnancy centers.[^1] Crisis pregnancy centers are not medical facilities. They do not have licensed providers. They have been documented providing inaccurate medical information to patients — including inaccurate information about abortion risk, fetal development, and treatment options. Dr. Connor has personally treated patients with ectopic pregnancies — a life-threatening emergency requiring immediate surgical or pharmaceutical intervention — whose treatment was delayed because they went to a crisis pregnancy center first, thinking it was a medical facility. The $140 million that went to these entities came from the same budget that should have funded the medical research infrastructure that is now gone.

The scale of this deception is now quantifiable. Approximately 2,700 CPCs operate nationally — three times the number of actual abortion clinics — receiving an estimated $700 million annually in combined federal, state, and private anti-abortion funding.[^2] The FTC issued warning letters to 15 CPCs for deceptive digital advertising^3, purchasing Google ads under keywords like "abortion clinic" to intercept patients seeking actual medical care. CPC clients are 34% Black and 26% Hispanic.[^4] These are not counseling centers. They are delay mechanisms. In states with gestational bans, delay equals denial.

The Consolidation of Power

Consider who is now running the American healthcare apparatus.

RFK Jr. — a man with no medical degree, no public health training, and a documented history of promoting anti-vaccine misinformation — is the Secretary of Health and Human Services, overseeing the health of 330 million Americans. He dismissed 17 standing members of the CDC's Advisory Committee on Immunization Practices. He oversaw the reduction of HHS from 82,000 to 62,000 employees.[^5] Under his authority, ACOG — the nation's leading obstetric professional organization — withdrew from the CDC's vaccine advisory process because it could no longer trust the scientific integrity of the committee.

Dr. Mehmet Oz — a television personality who built his career promoting unproven health products — is the CMS Administrator, overseeing Medicare and Medicaid. His first major action was deferring $259.5 million in Medicaid funding to Minnesota[^6] under a "fraud prevention" initiative that advocates warn could be weaponized to restrict reproductive healthcare reimbursements.

Jay Bhattacharya — a Stanford health economist who co-authored the Great Barrington Declaration opposing COVID lockdowns — has been appointed to oversee both the NIH and the CDC simultaneously (February 18, 2026).[^7] The world's two premier public health research institutions, under a single director, in the context of sweeping budget cuts and workforce reductions.

The "CRUSH" initiative — Comprehensive Regulations to Uncover Suspicious Healthcare — signals a major administrative overhaul of how Medicaid and Medicare claims are scrutinized, with potential to increase administrative burdens specifically on safety-net and reproductive care providers.

The pattern is consistent: public health infrastructure is being reconsolidated under political appointees whose qualifications are ideological alignment, not scientific expertise.

The $1.7 Trillion Operation and the Rational Blockholder Problem

HHS controls approximately $1.7 trillion annually[^8] — the single largest discretionary and mandatory spending complex in the federal government, exceeding the defense budget when Medicaid, Medicare, NIH, CDC, FDA, HRSA, and SAMHSA are aggregated. That is the scale of what is being restructured. To put it in terms that financial analysts understand: this is not a policy disagreement. This is a hostile restructuring of a $1.7 trillion operation.

The corporate governance literature has a concept directly applicable here: the rational blockholder. A blockholder is an entity with a concentrated ownership stake large enough to influence management decisions — and uses that influence to maximize its own return, not the return of all stakeholders. Applied to healthcare: private equity's concentrated ownership of hospital systems, physician practices, and insurance intermediaries creates a structural incentive to extract value rather than generate health outcomes. The blockholder maximizes return by reducing the cost of delivering care — through workforce cuts, service-line elimination, billing optimization — regardless of patient outcomes. The rational blockholder does not care that rural obstetric units are unprofitable. It closes them. It does not care that congenital syphilis is preventable. It does not profit from prevention. It profits from treatment — specifically the commercially insured portion. Medicaid patients generate the lowest margins. They are the first to lose access.

The current administration's restructuring of HHS follows rational blockholder logic: eliminate oversight, reduce regulatory burden on high-margin operators, defund safety-net infrastructure, and consolidate decision-making under leadership aligned with the extractive model. What looks like ideology from the outside looks like portfolio optimization from the inside. The people making the decisions are not confused about the consequences. The consequences are the feature.

AG Weaponization and the Preemption War

The state attorney general has become the single most consequential elected official for reproductive healthcare access — more determinative than any governor, more immediately dangerous than any legislator.

Anti-abortion PACs spent an estimated $43 million in 2024 state AG races[^9], according to the National Institute on Money in Politics, outpacing pro-access spending three to one. Dark money in AG races grew 340% from 2018 to 2024.[^10] The investment is rational: the AG controls enforcement of criminal penalties, determines which providers face prosecution, and decides whether to join multi-state litigation coalitions. Twelve AG races in 2024 were identified as abortion enforcement-determinative.

The weaponization is operational. At least five state AGs in ban states have asserted cross-border subpoena authority — demanding health records from providers in protection states when their residents travel for care. In 2026, Louisiana and Idaho AGs attempted interstate subpoenas on telemedicine providers in Illinois and Massachusetts.^11 Shield-law states refused. No Supreme Court ruling exists on interstate subpoena authority for reproductive health records, and with the HIPAA reproductive privacy rule vacated, there is no federal floor.

Preemption doctrine has become a two-directional weapon. In restrictive states, legislatures use "offensive preemption" to block local governments from regulating CPCs or expanding reproductive health protections. In protective states, the New Mexico Supreme Court ruled in January 2025[^12] that local abortion-restrictive ordinances were preempted by the state's Reproductive and Gender-Affirming Health Care Freedom Act. The effect is a hardening of the legal landscape in both directions, with AGs as the primary enforcement arms. Your reproductive rights are now a function of your state capital, enforced by your state attorney general, funded by donors you will never see.

The SCOTUS Pipeline and Judicial Capture

The judiciary is not a neutral arbiter in this system. It is the system.

The Supreme Court's demolition of voting rights protections followed a thirteen-year trajectory. Shelby County v. Holder in 2013 gutted preclearance. Brnovich v. DNC in 2021 narrowed Section 2 protections for vote denial. Louisiana v. Callais in April 2026 — a 6-3 ruling — struck down a second majority-Black congressional district[^13], requiring proof of intentional discrimination and allowing states to use partisan motives as a defense. Justice Kagan's dissent declared the ruling renders VRA Section 2 "all but a dead letter." No effective federal Voting Rights Act enforcement tool remains.

Below the Supreme Court, the pipeline is accelerating. The Trump administration confirmed 60 Article III federal judges in the first 120 days[^14] of his second term — lifetime appointments, none screened on reproductive rights. Sixth Circuit nominees are critical for abortion access in Michigan, Ohio, Kentucky, and Tennessee. These judges will adjudicate reproductive rights cases for thirty to forty years.

At the state level, judicial elections have become the primary battleground. The 2023 Wisconsin Supreme Court race drew a record $51 million^15 in spending — five times the previous state record. The liberal victory flipped the court for the first time in fifteen years, with direct implications for Wisconsin's 1849 abortion ban. According to the Brennan Center, at least six states with determinative supreme court elections in 2026 have active abortion litigation pending, and outside spending is tracking toward $100 million.^16 The people who decide whether your rights exist are selected by the people who profit from their elimination.

The Gerrymandering–Reproductive Health Feedback Loop

The connection between gerrymandered maps and dead women is not metaphorical. It is statistical.

A Voting Rights Lab correlation analysis found that states with the most restrictive voting access have maternal mortality rates 2.4 times higher[^17] than states with the most expansive voting access — a relationship that persists after controlling for income and urbanicity. The states that suppress votes are the same states that restrict abortion, defund maternal health infrastructure, and refuse Medicaid expansion. It is the same policy ecology, governed by the same actors, funded by the same donors.

Callais activates this feedback loop in real time. Within days of the ruling, Tennessee's governor called a special session. The legislature passed a new map on May 7, 2026, splitting Memphis — 64% Black — across three Republican-leaning districts.[^18] Representative Steve Cohen ended his reelection campaign on May 15, citing the impossibility of winning. Tennessee's delegation is projected to shift from 8-1 Republican to 9-0.[^19] Louisiana is restructuring Representative Cleo Fields' majority-Black district around predominantly white Baton Rouge communities. Mississippi's governor plans to dismantle the state's lone majority-Black district before 2027. All four post-Callais redistricting states have near-total abortion bans. Eliminating majority-Black districts removes the representatives most likely to advocate for reproductive healthcare.

The Senate filibuster completes the trap. The Women's Health Protection Act has failed in four consecutive Congresses. The John Lewis Voting Rights Advancement Act has failed on the same procedural barrier four times. Federal voting rights preclearance has been absent since 2013. Over 1,000 discriminatory voting law changes have been implemented[^20] in formerly covered states since Shelby County, according to the Brennan Center. The communities most harmed — Black women in Southern states — cannot fix either problem through the mechanisms designed to protect them.

The Global Dimension

The Mexico City Policy was expanded from $8 billion to over $30 billion in affected global aid, enacted January 27, 2026. Approximately 2,600 organizations worldwide are affected.[^21] The U.S. completed its withdrawal from the World Health Organization on January 26, 2026, severing $280 million in funding.[^22] The Guttmacher Institute has documented the mathematical relationship: every $10 million decrease in family planning funding produces approximately 500 additional maternal deaths globally.[^23] The U.S. is exporting its domestic reproductive policy to the developing world, where consequences are measured in deaths.

The downstream specificity of the Mexico City Policy damage is now documentable. An estimated 1,400 clinics globally have closed or suspended operations — 1,175 of those in Africa. Approximately $87 million in direct funding has been lost. An estimated 9 million people have been cut off from reproductive healthcare services.[^24] These are documented facility-by-facility closure records. The countries most affected are those with the highest baseline maternal mortality rates — the women most at risk of dying in childbirth are the ones being denied the care that would prevent it.

The administration simultaneously signed bilateral health cooperation MOUs with Panama ($22.5 million) and Burkina Faso ($147 million)[^25], establishing U.S.-led global health capabilities outside of the WHO — shifting resources away from established global reproductive health and maternal mortality frameworks into bilateral arrangements controlled by the administration.

The Two-Tier System, Formalized

There is a data point missing from every economic analysis of Dobbs, and its absence is itself a finding: nobody is tracking the shadow economy of forced-birth adoption. As forced pregnancies increase in ban states, there is no federal tracking of the financial dynamics of the private adoption industry — the transfer of wealth from low-income birth mothers to high-income adoptive parents and the fees extracted by private agencies. The babies most likely to be adopted are white. The mothers most likely to be forced into pregnancies they cannot sustain are poor and disproportionately of color. The economic beneficiaries of this system are not the birth mothers. This is an untracked wealth transfer operating inside the Dobbs economy, and no one is counting it.

Meanwhile, WIC has been weaponized through budget politics. A lapse in WIC benefits forces families to suddenly pay the full, unsubsidized cost of specialized infant formula — redirecting limited household funds from rent and utilities into hunger, driving spikes in deep poverty. The proposed cut to WIC's Cash Value Benefit from $26/$52 to $10/$13[^26] would deepen this deliberately engineered food insecurity.

The simultaneous withdrawal of federal infrastructure and variable state response has formalized a two-tier system. In California, state investment of $140 million or more in reproductive healthcare, mandatory nurse staffing ratios, sustained MMRC operations, and decades of quality improvement have produced a maternal mortality rate of 4.0 per 100,000. In Louisiana, the rate is 58.1 per 100,000 — fourteen times California's rate.[^27] Cash-pay concierge models thrive for wealthy patients while the safety net collapses for everyone else. Startups offering $5,000 "menopause optimization" packages[^28] are flush with venture capital, while the local federally qualified health center cannot afford generic estrogen cream because their 340B drug pricing program was restricted. If you work for a Fortune 500 company, your employer covers travel costs for out-of-state reproductive care. If you work retail, you get nothing.

Simultaneously, the physical infrastructure of care has vanished for those without means. Eighty-one clinics closed post-Dobbs, leaving 22 million reproductive-age women—and disproportionately Native American women—living in abortion deserts more than 100 miles from care.[^29] In 2025 alone, full-year data showed abortion incidence reached 1,126,000, but 24% were delivered via telehealth, including 91,000 to patients in total-ban states via shield laws.[^30]

The Economic Devastation — The $133 Billion Tax

$133 billion: the annual economic cost of abortion restrictions across 16 states (IWPR). 556,042 women suppressed from the workforce. $68 billion in annual economic drag from the 16 states with the most severe bans. $32.3 billion: the annual cost of maternal morbidity from conception through 5 years postpartum. $6.6 billion: lost productivity from maternal morbidity annually.^31 8 of the 10 lowest-GDP-per-capita states have abortion bans[^32]: Mississippi, West Virginia, Arkansas, Alabama, Kentucky, South Carolina, Idaho, and Louisiana. 129,000 residents fled ban states post-Dobbs.[^33] Furthermore, Black and Hispanic women now account for 49% of interstate abortion travelers despite comprising only 32% of the reproductive-age population—a structural racial disparity in post-Dobbs access.[^34] Brain drain accelerating.

The compounding nature of costs across a woman's lifetime represents a structural "woman tax": expensive out-of-pocket contraception in her twenties → prohibitive maternal care co-pays in her thirties ($18,865 average; $26,200 for a cesarean section)[^35] → high-cost menopausal symptom management in her fifties. Women aged 45-54 experiencing menopause incur healthcare costs 45-47% higher than their male or pre-menopausal peers, accompanied by an estimated $1.8 billion in annual lost workplace productivity.[^36] This is not a series of unrelated expenses. It is a lifelong economic extraction that inhibits intergenerational wealth accumulation — and it is getting worse at every stage simultaneously.

The $900 billion in total proposed Medicaid cuts[^37], the proposed 65% cut to Title X funding ($286 million annually at risk)[^38], the $223.5 million in terminated NIMHD grants, the $22 million in terminated fetal tissue research, the $280 million withdrawn from the WHO, the $45 million per month in unreimbursed Planned Parenthood care — this money is not being saved. It is being redirected. The Medicaid work requirements are designed to strip coverage; the administrative burden is the point. The system that is being built is not one that eliminates healthcare. It is one that eliminates healthcare for poor women. The wealthy will always have access. The question this administration has answered — through fifty specific, documented actions — is whether everyone else will.

The IVF and Contraceptive Contradiction

The administration publicly embraces IVF as a "pro-family" technology while systematically undermining the conditions that make it viable. The Alabama Supreme Court's February 2024 ruling in LePage v. Mobile Infirmary^39 — declaring frozen embryos to be "children" under state wrongful death law — temporarily shut down IVF services at three of Alabama's largest fertility clinics. The ruling exposed the legal fragility of IVF in states with fetal personhood frameworks. If a frozen embryo is a legal person, then the routine IVF process — which creates, evaluates, and discards embryos that are non-viable or carry genetic disease — becomes legally indistinguishable from homicide.

Alabama passed emergency legislation to restore IVF, but the underlying personhood logic remains embedded in the legal architecture of 11 states with active fetal personhood language. Four additional states now face active litigation threatening IVF access, with an estimated 15,000 IVF cycles annually at risk.^40 Congressional Republicans blocked the Right to IVF Act in June 2024. The racial dimension compounds the contradiction: Black patients face 1.5 times higher infertility rates[^41] but significantly lower IVF utilization. IVF costs $15,000-$30,000 per cycle, is not covered by most insurance plans, and the FTC has opened investigations into fertility clinic pricing. The intersection of legal risk, financial inaccessibility, and racial disparity means IVF is becoming a technology available only to the wealthy, in blue states, with the right employer.

The contradiction extends beyond IVF. Following the Dobbs concurrence, four state bills have been introduced to restrict IUD access citing fetal personhood, threatening the primary long-acting contraceptive for 4.3 million users nationally.^42 Twelve states have also eliminated or severely restricted judicial bypass for minors seeking abortion, affecting an estimated 45,000 pregnant minors annually.[^43]

The Comstock Act: A Backdoor National Ban

The Comstock Act of 1873 — an anti-obscenity statute that has been effectively dormant for a century — is being resurrected as a mechanism for a de facto national abortion ban without requiring new legislation. The statute prohibits mailing "obscene" materials, and legal scholars aligned with the current administration argue that abortion medications (mifepristone and misoprostol) qualify as "articles for producing abortion" under the statute's original text.

The significance cannot be overstated: Comstock enforcement would ban the mailing of medication abortion nationwide — including in states where abortion remains legal — without requiring a single new law, a single Congressional vote, or a single court ruling. This would affect the 60%+ of abortions now conducted via medication. A 2026 DOJ internal memo suggests Comstock bars mailing of mifepristone and misoprostol — a reinterpretation that could terminate approximately 500,000 annual mail-order medication abortions.^44 The 2022 OLC memorandum concluding Comstock does not apply to lawful mailings has been withdrawn. State attorneys general in several ban states have signaled intent to prosecute. The Trump DOJ has already withdrawn from 11 pending FACE Act prosecutions, signaling a non-enforcement era for clinic protections — and clinic violence has risen 346% in states where enforcement dropped.[^45] The Comstock threat exists inside this enforcement vacuum.

Meanwhile, the legal chaos surrounding EMTALA continues to cost lives. HHS documented 22 hospitals denying stabilizing abortion care in EMTALA-covered emergencies in 2025 alone, resulting in three maternal deaths and four hysterectomies while the Moyle v. US conflict remained unresolved.[^46]

Digital Surveillance of Pregnancy

The vacating of HIPAA reproductive privacy protections in Purl v. HHS (June 2025)[^47] removed the last federal barrier to the digital surveillance of pregnancy. The implications map onto everyday digital behavior:

  • Period-tracking apps: used by an estimated 50 million American women. This data can now be subpoenaed. The FTC found that Flo Health — with over 100 million users — had shared menstrual cycle data^42 and pregnancy intentions with Facebook and Google without consent.
  • Search history: queries for abortion services, clinics, and medication abortion can be obtained through standard law enforcement data requests.
  • Location data: commercially available from data brokers without a warrant. Data broker Near Intelligence sold location data from nearly 600 Planned Parenthood facilities[^48] to the anti-abortion Veritas Society, enabling targeted anti-abortion ads to clinic visitors' phones. State AGs in three ban states have issued geofence warrants capturing all individuals within 100 meters of clinics.^49
  • Pharmacy records: purchases of emergency contraception, pregnancy tests, and misoprostol can be tracked through retail pharmacy databases.
  • License plate surveillance: a Texas sheriff's office used the Flock Safety ALPR network — 83,000 cameras across 6,809 networks^50 — to track a woman suspected of self-administered abortion across state lines. The search was logged as "had an abortion, search for female."

Multiple states with abortion bans have already attempted to subpoena digital health data. The chilling effect is documented: an estimated 30% of period-tracking app users in ban states deleted their apps within six months of Dobbs.[^43] Women are avoiding digital health tools — which are the primary interface for prenatal care scheduling, medication reminders, and symptom tracking — because they fear their own health data will be used against them.

The convergence of Comstock, digital surveillance, and the HIPAA vacatur creates a legal environment in which a woman's pregnancy can be detected, tracked, and prosecuted through her own digital footprint — without her knowledge, without her consent, and without a warrant.

This surveillance feeds a growing criminalization apparatus: 73% of US pregnancy criminalization cases involve Black women, and the rate of prosecution is accelerating post-Dobbs, with 12 new cases filed in 2026 alone.[^51]

The Democratic Erosion Closes the Exits

The final dimension of the corruption is the systematic closure of every democratic mechanism that could reverse it.

Voters have tried. In every state where abortion has appeared on a ballot since Dobbs — ten of ten between 2022 and 2024 — voters chose to protect reproductive rights. Kansas shocked observers in August 2022 when 59% of voters in a deep-red state rejected an anti-abortion amendment.[^52] In 2024, seven of ten states approved protections.[^53] The direct democracy strategy works. Which is precisely why it is being dismantled.

Eleven states have enacted new restrictions on initiative and referendum processes since 2022 — raising signature thresholds and imposing supermajority ratification requirements of 60% to 67%. Florida's abortion measure received 57% support in 2024 but failed the 60% threshold.[^54] The strategy is anti-majoritarian by design: abortion measures consistently win at 55-63%, so the threshold is raised above the margin of victory.[^55] Nineteen states enacted restrictions on absentee and mail voting since 2020, producing a 23% drop in absentee voting — steepest among Black and Latino voters.[^56]

Voter suppression, gerrymandered legislatures, captured courts, weaponized AGs, and procedural sabotage of direct democracy operate as a system. The same states that ban abortion suppress votes, stack courts, fund CPCs, criminalize providers, surveil patients, and raise the threshold for the ballot measures that could undo it all. Fourteen states enacted both anti-trans legislation and abortion restrictions in the same sessions between 2022 and 2026[^57] — the same sponsors authoring both. The target is not abortion alone. It is bodily autonomy as a governing principle.

The corruption is not that the system is broken. The corruption is that the system is working exactly as redesigned — extracting wealth, consolidating power, eliminating oversight, and sealing the exits so the people harmed by it cannot vote their way out.

Chapter 12: Research and Surveillance — Destroying the Instruments

When you eliminate the capacity to measure a problem, you eliminate the political pressure to fix it. When you eliminate the capacity to protect the data that remains, you turn measurement into a weapon.

Before

The United States had built the most advanced public health surveillance infrastructure in the world. It was imperfect. It was underfunded. It was slow. But it existed, and it worked.

The Pregnancy Risk Assessment Monitoring System — PRAMS — coordinated by the CDC since 1987, was the only system of its kind: the only mechanism that systematically tracked what happens to women before, during, and after pregnancy, in every participating state, by race, geography, and risk factor. It had been running for nearly 40 years.

Maternal Mortality Review Committees in most states investigated every pregnancy-related death to determine whether it was preventable, what the contributing factors were, and what system changes could prevent the next one. By 2024, all 50 states and the District of Columbia had established MMRCs — the first nationwide infrastructure for systematic surveillance of preventable maternal deaths. Over 80 percent of maternal deaths reviewed by these committees were determined to have been preventable. The CDC's STI surveillance infrastructure tracked syphilis, gonorrhea, chlamydia, and HIV outbreak geography. The National Birth Defects Prevention Network tracked congenital anomalies. The ART surveillance system tracked assisted reproductive technology outcomes. The Safe to Sleep program educated parents about infant sleep safety. The NIMHD — the National Institute on Minority Health and Health Disparities — funded $534 million in research producing the evidence for every racial health disparity published in a medical journal.

This infrastructure did not merely observe outcomes. It produced the clinical protocols that save lives: magnesium sulfate for preeclampsia, antenatal corticosteroids for preterm birth, evidence-based hemorrhage bundles, standardized hypertension treatment algorithms. Every one of those protocols exists because someone funded the research, enrolled the patients, collected the data, and published the findings. That infrastructure took decades to build. California used it to reduce maternal mortality by 55% between 2006 and 2013 — from 16.9 to 7.3 per 100,000[^4] — through the CMQCC: evidence-based hemorrhage and preeclampsia toolkits, quality collaboratives, data transparency. That is what this infrastructure made possible.

NIH was funding 27 institutes with active clinical trials for preeclampsia, preterm birth, and placental development. Women's health research received 8.8 percent of total NIH grant spending[^5] over the prior decade — already a fraction of what the disease burden warranted. The National Academies had recommended an additional $15.7 billion over five years.[^4] And even within that underfunded infrastructure, the pipeline was structurally inequitable: a landmark 2011 study in Science documented that Black principal investigators were funded at 55 to 65 percent the rate of white investigators[^4] after controlling for education, training, and productivity — a gap that NIH's own UNITE initiative, launched in 2021, had not closed.

Trump 1.0

Fetal tissue research restricted in 2019, disrupting placental and preeclampsia research. NIH independence challenged politically. Research investment politicized.

What Began to Recover

Biden reversed fetal tissue restrictions in 2021. ARPA-H created ($2.5 billion). "Sprint for Women's Health" launched — $113 million to 24 projects.[^6] NIMHD supported. The research infrastructure began to stabilize.

More than stabilize. The White House Initiative on Women's Health Research (November 2023) catalyzed $200 million in new FY2025 NIH women's health funding[^7], bringing total projected spending to $5 billion — still only 10 percent of the $48 billion NIH budget,[^7] but the largest dedicated increase in decades. The Department of Defense committed $500 million.[^8] Combined with the ARPA-H Sprint's $100 million, total federal innovation commitment exceeded $800 million.[^9] ARPA-H launched the MOCS program — Making Obstetrics Care Smart — the first federal program explicitly integrating AI into obstetric care. The PCORI reauthorization in 2019 had secured $4.4 billion through 2029 in a Trust Fund structure insulated from annual appropriations volatility — one of the few pieces of infrastructure that would survive what came next.

Trump 2.0: The Dismantlement

PRAMS destroyed. The entire PRAMS team was fired April 1, 2025, before releasing the 2023 data.[^1] Federal funding expires April 2026[^3], and multiple states have already reported disrupted data collection. 2023 is the last year for which we will have reliable, nationally comparable data on pregnancy outcomes. We have no idea what happened in 2024. We have no idea what is happening right now.

Surveillance infrastructure systematically eliminated: - Women's Health and Fertility Branch — eliminated - Contraceptive Best Practices Team — eliminated - CDC STI surveillance labs — closed - 8-person STI committee — dissolved - ART surveillance — terminated - Birth defects tracking — cut - Safe to Sleep program — eliminated (April 2025) - Over 200 CDC datasets removed or altered between January and February 2025 - Pages on gender-based violence, HIV, abortion access, LGBTQ+ health, and teenage depression — removed or redacted - Nearly half of CDC surveillance databases: updates paused - Terminology altered: "gender" replaced with "sex" across federal health documents - OMB executive directive removed sexual orientation and gender identity data collection from all federally funded research — a permanent research blind spot on LGBTQ+ health - HHS reduced from 82,000 to 62,000 staff; over 4,800 federal health workers fired

DOGE-directed CDC workforce reductions eliminated approximately 2,400 staff,[^10] with the Division of Reproductive Health and Division of Vital Statistics disproportionately affected. These were the researchers who tracked maternal mortality, infant mortality, and reproductive health outcomes. The capacity to monitor preventable deaths in real time — to detect a spike in sepsis from delayed miscarriage management, to track the geographic spread of congenital syphilis — was not automated. It depended on epidemiologists, data analysts, and public health officers who received buyout offers and termination letters.

The scale of grant termination. As of November 2025, HHS had terminated 977 NIH grant awards representing approximately $1.7 billion in obligated funding.[^2] NIH flagged more than 1,700 grants as "not consistent with Trump's directives." Terminated grants included intimate partner violence research — a leading cause of pregnancy-associated death — HIV prevention research, and adolescent health research. The NSF separately terminated over 400 grants for DEI non-compliance.[^10] AAU member institutions reported over $3 billion in total federal grant terminations^11 by mid-2025. Clinical trials were interrupted. Minority-serving institutions had no bridge funding.

NIMHD gutted — and the anti-DEI framing. 77 grants terminated ($223.5 million), 29.6% cut, 61% fewer competitive grants[^12], targeted for elimination. The framing of this elimination as "anti-DEI" is politically useful. The functional outcome is defunding the only research infrastructure specifically designed to understand the patients who are dying the most. NIMHD is the primary federal funding source for research on Black maternal mortality, on why fibroids disproportionately affect Black women, on what social determinants are actually driving the maternal mortality crisis. Black women die at 3-4 times the white rate.[^6] The one institute whose entire purpose was to understand why and fix it was targeted for destruction — not because it was failing, but because its work was reclassified as ideology. Title X funding was simultaneously frozen under the guise of reviewing grantee compliance with anti-DEI executive orders.

Fetal tissue research grants terminated. 17 active grants worth $22 million were ended.^13 These were not abstract bench science — they were clinical trials studying preeclampsia, preterm birth, and placental development. Preeclampsia is a disease of the placenta. You cannot do preeclampsia research without studying placental tissue. When you terminate those grants, you need years of re-enrollment, tissue collection, and rebuilt teams. This is generational damage. And no FDA-approved drug has ever been developed specifically for preeclampsia — despite over 70,000 maternal deaths globally per year.[^14] The drugs we use — aspirin and magnesium sulfate — are generics from the 1920s.[^14] A century-long R&D failure driven by short treatment windows, exclusion of pregnant women from trials, and a patient population that is disproportionately lower-income and non-white. BARDA has never prioritized preeclampsia. The fetal tissue terminations guaranteed this pharmaceutical void will persist.

The political history of fetal tissue research is itself a measure of the damage: Reagan moratorium (1988), Clinton lifted it (1993), Trump 1.0 severely restricted it (2019), Biden reversed it (2021), Trump 2.0 formally terminated it (January 2026). This 10-to-20-year structural instability ultimately drives researchers permanently out of the field.

NIH consolidation — and the funding illusion. The administration proposed an $18 billion NIH cut — 40%.[^15] Congress rejected it. The Consolidated Appropriations Act (H.R. 7148) appropriated $47.2 billion — a $400 million increase.[^10] On paper, protected. In practice, OMB imposed spending restrictions requiring every institute to submit detailed spend plans before releasing funds. Combined with the 43-day federal shutdown, grant awards are running at a fraction of normal pace. FY 2025 success rates dropped to approximately 17%.[^16] The money exists. It cannot be spent. Jay Bhattacharya was appointed single director of NIH and CDC simultaneously. A proposed consolidation would reduce 27 institutes to 5.[^17] The NIH Office of Research on Women's Health — which had just released its 2025–2029 Strategic Plan — faces consolidation, rendering its $55 million budget aspirational.[^4] ACOG withdrew from the CDC's Advisory Committee on Immunization Practices. Fifteen states sued.

What the Women's Health Initiative produced — before it was terminated. WHI was terminated in September 2025. Here is what it produced over its three decades — just the findings from 2024-2025 alone: a 2024 JAMA comprehensive review establishing that low-fat dietary patterns reduce breast cancer mortality; a June 2025 NIH finding that estrogen-only hormone therapy reduces breast cancer incidence by 14% in women under 55;[^4] a December 2025 finding that hormone therapy is NOT associated with increased breast cancer risk in women with BRCA1/BRCA2 mutations — the largest prospective analysis ever conducted; and a March 2026 study identifying a blood biomarker (phosphorylated tau 217) that predicts dementia risk 25 years before symptoms appear.[^16] Every one of those findings came from WHI data. Every one of them has the potential to save thousands of lives. The initiative that produced them has been eliminated.

ARPA-H — the question mark. ARPA-H — the Advanced Research Projects Agency for Health, established in 2022 within NIH with a venture-capital model — invested $113 million in women's health across 24 awardees[^6] in six months under the Biden administration. Its non-dilutive funding model allowed startups to retain equity — a structural innovation in federal health investment. As of March 2026, ARPA-H's operational status under the new HHS leadership is uncertain. Its budget is technically included in the NIH appropriation, but its independence is at risk in the 27-to-5 institute consolidation plan. The only federal agency specifically designed to fund breakthrough women's health innovation may be eliminated before it completes its first investment cycle.

CDC bifurcation. A proposal would split the CDC into an infectious disease entity and a "lifestyle-related" chronic disease entity. The diseases reclassified as "lifestyle" include reproductive health, maternal mortality, obesity, diabetes, and mental health. These are not lifestyle choices. Separating infectious disease from reproductive health surveillance means the next congenital syphilis epidemic gets tracked by an agency with no mandate to integrate pregnancy data. The next COVID maternal mortality surge becomes invisible by institutional design. The fragmentation is the point.

The MMRC paradox. Congress funded the Maternal Mortality Review Committees ($113.5 million through 2030).[^6] Idaho tried to disband its own MMRC to stop publishing data showing its abortion ban is killing women. The paradox: they can count how women die. They cannot fund the systems to prevent it. Surveillance without intervention is not a healthcare system. It is an autopsy.

The Post-Dobbs Research Chill

The destruction of federal research infrastructure is happening alongside a second crisis: the legal environment created by state abortion bans is chilling reproductive health research. Researchers at Johns Hopkins and the Multi-Regional Clinical Trials Center have documented a measurable deterrence effect — IRB denials for abortion outcomes research, researcher departures from ban states, deterred clinical trial enrollment for reproductive-age participants. The ambiguity extends to documentation of pregnancy during trials, management of miscarriage complications, and criminal prosecution risk for trial staff.

This compounds a gender data gap predating the 1993 NIH Revitalization Act. Thirty years after that mandate, cardiovascular trials remain approximately 25 to 30 percent female.[^6] More than 67 percent of drugs prescribed to pregnant women lack adequate safety or dosing data.[^18] The research infrastructure is being dismantled from both ends — politically from above, legally from below.

Health Data as a Weapon: Surveillance in the Post-Dobbs Era

RestoredCDC.org exists because researchers lost access to federal data — an independent mirror maintained by librarians and public health advocates, archiving federal data before it disappears. This is not normal. This is emergency data preservation. But the crisis in research surveillance is mirrored by a second crisis: the weaponization of health data against the patients it was designed to protect.

Period apps, data brokers, and digital subpoenas. The FTC settled with Flo Health after the app — used by over 100 million people globally[^8] — shared menstrual cycle data, pregnancy status, and health symptoms with Facebook and Google, even for users paying for premium privacy. Most period tracking apps are classified as "wellness" devices outside HIPAA. According to FTC estimates, 88 percent of popular health apps are not HIPAA-covered.[^1] After Dobbs, app deletions spiked more than 30 percent^11 — women understood the risk before regulators acted. They were right: law enforcement in ban states has issued subpoenas and search warrants for menstrual app data, health search histories, and location data. At least three abortion prosecutions between 2022 and 2024 used digital health data as evidence.[^8] The Digital Defense Fund reported a fourfold increase in digital security assistance requests post-Dobbs.[^4] SafeGraph sold smartphone location data of abortion clinic visitors to anti-abortion groups for as little as $160 per dataset.^19 Google responded by auto-deleting location history for visits to abortion clinics and fertility centers. A technology company implemented stronger reproductive data protections than the federal government.

The Change Healthcare breach. In February 2024, Russian-linked ransomware group ALPHV/BlackCat accessed Change Healthcare — which processes roughly one in three U.S. patient claims[^4] — through compromised credentials with no multi-factor authentication. The breach exposed protected health information of approximately 100 million Americans.[^8] UnitedHealth Group paid a $22 million ransom.^13 It was the largest health data breach in U.S. history. The MOVEit vulnerability simultaneously compromised Medicaid data across at least ten states — Missouri alone, 3.2 million beneficiaries.[^20] These breaches disproportionately exposed BIPOC and low-income populations dependent on safety-net systems where 40 percent of critical access hospitals lack dedicated cybersecurity staff.[^15]

The enforcement vacuum. HHS's Office for Civil Rights — sole federal HIPAA enforcer — operates on $38 million annually to protect data for 330 million Americans,[^21] handling 60,000-plus complaints but issuing fewer than five civil penalties per year.[^21] Average breach-to-resolution: 2.8 years.[^21] Under the current administration, OCR staffing was cut 30 percent through DOGE.^11 The FY2026 budget proposes cutting the FTC by over $100 million.[^8] The 2024 HIPAA rule prohibiting disclosure of reproductive health records for law enforcement has been delayed. Meanwhile, the health data broker industry generates $11 billion annually.[^4] IQVIA and Symphony Health aggregate over 90 percent of U.S. pharmacy transactions[^12], selling medication histories enabling inference-based surveillance — mifepristone identifies abortions, antiretrovirals identify HIV, naloxone identifies opioid use — without diagnosis codes. Retail loyalty programs predict pregnancy with 85 to 90 percent accuracy.[^22] One-third of top U.S. hospitals transmitted patient data to Facebook via embedded Meta Pixel tracking. Congress failed to pass comprehensive health data privacy legislation through 2025, leaving 33 states with no health-specific privacy law.[^1] Washington State's My Health My Data Act — enacted 2024 with a private right of action — remains an outlier.

The patients most surveilled are the patients least protected: incarcerated people whose medical records are accessible to corrections officers; adolescents whose EHR billing discloses reproductive care to parents; LGBTQ+ individuals whose care records exist in states that have criminalized them. And Black and Latina women, disproportionately prosecuted in pregnancy cases, whose health data exists in systems designed without their safety in mind.

Innovation Without Equity

Innovation continues despite the destruction — femtech VC surged 55 percent to $2.6 billion in 2024[^17], shifting from healthtech apps toward biopharma. The FDA cleared the first AI-powered cervical cancer screening system (February 2024) and the first predictive AI for five-year breast cancer risk from standard mammograms (June 2025). Non-hormonal male contraceptives entered human trials. State innovation funding is emerging as a hedge — Massachusetts invested $3.7 million in women's health grants in 2025[^23], California committed $6 million[^4] through its Initiative for Precision Medicine.

But venture capital funds products for commercially insured patients in cities. All-female founding teams received approximately 2 percent of global VC in 2024[^6], with deal sizes of $5.2 million versus $11.7 million for male teams.[^24] Black women founders received less than 2 percent of femtech VC.[^4] Fertility prediction algorithms built on white-centric datasets show reduced accuracy for Black and Hispanic women. AI diagnostic tools trained on datasets where fewer than 5 percent of dermatology images show dark skin,[^4] where EHR records for Black patients contain twice as many missing fields, where VBAC prediction calculators systematically assign lower success rates to women of color — these tools do not correct disparities. They automate them.

Post-Dobbs philanthropic investment in reproductive health grew 50 to 100 percent, but remains below $500 million annually[^8] versus the multi-billion-dollar federal baseline. The Society of Family Planning launched a $10 million emergency fund.[^4] MacKenzie Scott contributed over $50 million.[^16] Private philanthropy cannot replicate the scale, continuity, or geographic reach of federal research. Rural women — 15 to 19 percent of the population — represent less than 5 percent of clinical trial participants.[^15] Only one in ten rural counties has an OB/GYN. The research desert maps onto the mortality crisis.

We will have better drugs and more sophisticated technology. We will have less understanding of why Black women die in childbirth at three times the rate of white women. Innovation without equity research is not progress. It is the widening of a gap.

Chapter 13: The Demographic Collapse

The Proof That It Backfired

The stated rationale for overturning Roe v. Wade, for the proliferation of state-level abortion bans, and for the broader pro-natalist agenda of the conservative movement is that restricting abortion will produce more births, strengthen the nuclear family, and reverse the demographic decline of the United States. The data says the opposite is happening. Every measured indicator of reproductive behavior since the Dobbs decision shows Americans — particularly young Americans — choosing fewer children, not more, and choosing permanent measures to ensure it.

If the goal was more babies, the policy has failed on its own terms. If the goal was something else — the enforcement of patriarchal control over female bodies, the punishment of sexual autonomy, the consolidation of a political coalition organized around the subordination of women — then it is succeeding. But it is not producing the demographic outcome its architects claim to want.

The Fertility Rate Is at an All-Time Low

The U.S. total fertility rate has plummeted to 1.6 children per woman[^1] — 24 percent below the 2.1 replacement rate required to sustain population without immigration. Provisional 2025 data from the CDC's National Center for Health Statistics shows a general fertility rate of just 53.1 per 1,000 women aged 15 to 44[^2], continuing the free fall. More than 52 percent[^20] of women aged 20 to 39 are childless[^3]. The marriage rate has fallen to 46.4 percent — the lowest since the Census Bureau began tracking it in 1940.

These are not new trends. The fertility rate has been declining for decades, driven by expanded educational and economic opportunity for women, the rising cost of childcare, and the structural impossibility of raising children on wages that have been stagnant in real terms since the 1970s. The cost of raising a child through age 17 now exceeds $310,000[^4]. What Dobbs has done is not reverse these trends — it has accelerated them, by adding fear to the list of reasons women choose not to become mothers.

The acceleration is measurable in intention, not just outcome. According to Pew Research Center, 47 percent[^96] of childless adults under 50[^5] now say they are unlikely to ever have children — a 10-percentage-point surge from 37 percent in 2018[^6]. When asked why, 57 percent say the same thing[^7]: "I just don't want to." This is not ambivalence. It is a generation that has watched the political system weaponize pregnancy, surveill miscarriage, and prosecute medical decisions, and concluded that the safest reproductive plan is no reproduction at all.

The Economics of Not Having Children

The decision not to have children is not purely ideological. It is, for millions of women, arithmetic. Childcare costs have surged past pre-pandemic levels, reaching a national average of $16,000 per year per child in 2025[^8] — a figure that exceeds in-state college tuition in many states and consumes 35 percent of single-parent median income[^9]. The federal government's own affordability benchmark — that families should spend no more than 7 percent of household income on childcare[^10] — is unmet in every single state for infant care. 46 percent of children under six live in licensed childcare deserts—a crisis that worsened in 2025 following the expiration of pandemic stabilization funds, costing the economy $172 billion annually.[^11]

And childcare is only the first bill. Insured patients pay $2,563 to $3,071 out of pocket for delivery alone[^12]; uninsured women face $7,000 to $30,000 or more[^13]. New mothers are twice as likely as their non-birthing peers to carry medical debt[^14] exceeding $250. According to the Peterson-KFF Health System Tracker, 46 percent of Black adults with medical debt report credit score damage[^15] — compared to 32 percent[^75] of White adults[^16] — meaning that the act of giving birth can lock a Black woman out of housing, employment, and mainstream credit for years afterward.

The downstream economics are equally punishing. Women denied abortion experience a 40 percent income decline over five years[^17] compared to women who obtained one, according to the landmark UCSF Turnaway Study. The denied women face three times higher odds of poverty[^18] and a $9,260 annual income gap[^19] that compounds across a lifetime. For Black women who are denied, the income decline is 52 percent[^20]. These are not projections. These are observed outcomes, rigorously controlled, from one of the most important longitudinal studies in reproductive health economics. And Dobbs has scaled the Turnaway penalty from a clinical research finding to a population-level economic event affecting millions of women in 21 ban states[^21].

The cascading costs are staggering. Unintended pregnancies cost federal and state governments $21 billion annually[^22], primarily through Medicaid-funded births and infant care. Post-Dobbs birth increases in ban states are projected to cost Medicaid an additional $4.6 billion from 2023 to 2026[^23] alone — 42,000 additional Medicaid-funded births[^24] per year in states that stripped women of the ability to choose. Meanwhile, the Institute for Women's Policy Research calculates that state abortion restrictions cost the U.S. economy $133 billion annually[^25] and suppress 556,000 women from the labor force entirely[^26]. Goldman Sachs projects that ban states will lose $105 billion in cumulative GDP over the next decade through reduced female labor force participation, healthcare cost increases, and talent relocation. The pro-natalist policy is not just failing to produce babies. It is producing poverty.

The Motherhood Penalty and the Fatherhood Bonus

The economic architecture punishing motherhood operates at every stage. Mothers face a 4 to 7 percent wage penalty[^27] per child, while fathers receive a measurable earnings boost — a dynamic so well-documented in labor economics it has its own name: the fatherhood bonus[^28]. The Pregnant Workers Fairness Act, which took effect in June 2023, was the first federal law to require employers to provide reasonable accommodations for pregnancy — meaning that for the entirety of American history before that date, a woman could be fired for asking for a bathroom break during a high-risk pregnancy.

The United States remains the only wealthy nation without federally mandated paid parental leave. Seventy-five percent of private-sector workers lack access to any paid leave[^29]. One in four American mothers returns to work within two weeks^30 of giving birth — not because she has recovered, but because she cannot afford to stay home. One in three fathers takes no leave at all[^31]. The maternity leave gap alone costs women $37 billion in lifetime earnings[^32]. The absence of paid leave does not merely reflect a policy gap — it reflects a political choice to make motherhood a financial catastrophe for everyone except the wealthy.

Women perform 2.6 hours per day of unpaid domestic work[^33] compared to 1.9 hours for men[^34], according to the Bureau of Labor Statistics. In dual-earner households, women still perform 73 percent of childcare[^35]. The caregiving crisis has forced more than 455,000 women out of the workforce between January and August 2025 alone, with 42 percent citing caregiving costs[^36] as the reason. Unpaid care work performed by women is valued at $1.1 trillion annually[^37] — an economic contribution that appears in no GDP calculation and earns no Social Security credit. The lifetime earnings loss for a mother averages $300,000[^38[^95]]. The system does not merely fail to support mothers. It extracts from them.

The Sterilization Surge

Prior to Dobbs, surgical sterilization rates had been declining since the mid-1970s, steadily replaced by Long-Acting Reversible Contraception like IUDs. The 2022 decision violently reversed a 40-year clinical trend.

In August 2022 alone[^39] — the first full month after the Dobbs decision — tubal sterilizations among young adults aged 19 to 26 leapt 70 percent[^40] compared to pre-decision baselines. Vasectomies among the same age group surged 95 percent[^41]. By September 2023, tubal ligations had approximately doubled and vasectomies tripled[^42] compared to pre-Dobbs levels. These are not fluctuations. They are young people — many of them nulliparous, meaning they have never had a child — choosing permanent, irreversible solutions because they do not trust the legal environment to allow them to control their fertility through any reversible means.

The sustained data is equally stark. Tubal sterilizations in states with abortion bans rose approximately 3 percent every month[^43] following Dobbs — a compounding trajectory that shows no sign of leveling off. Vasectomies among privately insured men rose 26 percent overall[^44]. In Texas, urologists report a massive, sustained surge in vasectomy requests, with cash-pay clinics heavily marketing the procedure at $500 to $900[^45] as a direct response to the state's political climate.

This is the clinical signature of a population that has concluded its government cannot be trusted with its reproductive future.

The Texas Paradox

Texas provides the clearest illustration of the backfire. Despite enacting one of the most restrictive abortion bans in the country — a total ban with no rape or incest exception — Texas's fertility rate dropped 2.07 percent in 2023[^46] to a new state low of 60.6 births per 1,000[^47] women of reproductive age. The law designed to force birth is producing fewer births, because the women subject to it are choosing sterilization, leaving the state, or avoiding pregnancy entirely.

The initial post-Dobbs data showed a brief birth spike — approximately 32,000 excess births annually[^48] in ban states in the first year (documented in this report's chapter on children). But that spike represents women who were already pregnant at the time of the decision and could not access abortion. The sustained trajectory is the opposite: a population actively suppressing its own fertility in response to state coercion.

The economic consequences in Texas are visible in the clinical data. Texas hospitals saw a 50 percent increase in sepsis[^49] from second-trimester pregnancy loss management after the ban — the direct clinical cost of physicians delaying standard-of-care intervention out of fear of prosecution. Pregnancy-related hospitalization costs in ban states rose 22 percent from 2022 to 2025[^50], driven by increased complexity and delayed care presentation. Emergency room visits for reproductive health complaints surged 18 percent[^51] in ban states, generating $2.3 billion in additional emergency care[^52] spending. The policy is not merely failing to produce more births. It is producing more complications, more near-deaths, and more bills — paid by the same taxpayers whose elected officials promised this would strengthen families.

The Intimacy Crisis

The demographic collapse extends beyond contraception to the fundamental architecture of human connection. Seventy percent of couples[^53] in states with abortion bans changed their contraceptive practices after Dobbs. One in five women[^54] in ban states reports avoiding sex entirely due to pregnancy fear — a direct chilling effect of the legal risk now attached to sexual activity. The anxiety surrounding accidental pregnancy in states with total bans has severely hindered spontaneous intimacy and driven a massive surge in demand for LARCs among young, childless individuals who view dating in their state as legally hazardous.

The broader sex recession predates Dobbs but has accelerated in its wake. The percentage of young adults aged 18 to 29 reporting no sexual activity in the past year has doubled, from 12 percent in 2010 to 24 percent in 2024[^55]. One in three young men[^56] reports no partnered sex at all. Weekly sex among adults 18 to 64 declined from 55 percent in 1990 to 37 percent in 2024[^57]. The rate of young adults living with a partner fell from 42 percent in 2014 to 32 percent[^75] in 2024[^58] — a collapse in cohabitation that directly maps to declining births.

The Gender Chasm

The 51-point gender gap[^59] among young voters in the 2024 election reflects a generational rupture that extends well beyond electoral politics. Young women overwhelmingly prioritize reproductive autonomy and view the conservative movement as an existential threat to their physical safety and economic trajectory. Young men are drifting toward the right-wing reactionary channels that frame female empowerment as a zero-sum loss. The resulting ideological chasm between men and women under 30 is producing measurable changes in relationship formation, cohabitation rates, and the willingness of women to enter partnerships with men whose political identity signals danger.

According to Pew and YouGov, 25 percent of Americans would not date someone[^60] with opposing political views. Political homophily in marriages has increased from 50 percent in 1960 to 77 percent in 2023[^61]. Thirty-five percent of Gen Z women[^62] say they would not date a Republican-voting man. OKCupid data shows that liberal-conservative match rates fell 30 percent since 2020[^63]. The policy environment has not merely chilled sexual behavior — it has fractured the ideological conditions under which heterosexual relationships form in the first place.

The 4B Movement — a South Korean feminist framework of no dating, no sex, no marriage, and no childbirth — surged virally in the United States after the 2024 election, adopted by American women as a form of reproductive protest. Women are not merely choosing fewer children. Some are choosing to exit heterosexual partnership entirely, as a conscious political act in a political environment they experience as hostile to their survival.

Gen Z is 40 percent more likely to be single[^64] than Millennials were at the same age. Roughly 30 percent of Gen Z adults[^65] have never been in a romantic relationship. Forty-five percent of adults aged 18 to 34[^66] report recent "situationship" experience — intimate contact without commitment, driven by economic precarity and a deliberate avoidance of the entanglements that once preceded parenthood. The median time to relationship exclusivity has doubled since 2014[^67]. The dating app infrastructure that was supposed to facilitate connection is instead generating fatigue: 79 percent of users report frustration[^68], and 48 percent say apps make it harder[^69] to find a partner than meeting someone in person.

Meanwhile, 25 percent of young men[^70] aged 15 to 35 report feeling lonely "a lot," and 15 percent of American men now report having zero close friends[^71] — up from 3 percent in 1990[^72]. The friendship recession is feeding a male loneliness crisis that the manosphere exploits and the policy establishment ignores. The men who cannot form friendships become the men who cannot form partnerships, who become the men who resent the women who will not partner with them, who become the electoral base for the politicians who restrict women's rights — completing a cycle that produces fewer families at every turn.

The Loneliness Epidemic

The Surgeon General declared loneliness a public health epidemic — and noted that its health effects are equivalent to smoking 15 cigarettes per day. Social isolation increases the risk of premature death by 29 percent[^74][^73], coronary heart disease by 29 percent[^74][^73], stroke by 32 percent[^75], and dementia by 50 percent in older adults[^76]. The economic toll is equally measurable: $6.7 billion[^77] in additional annual Medicare spending attributable to social isolation alone.

Women bear a disproportionate burden: they report higher rates of social isolation, carry the weight of caregiving that fragments social networks, and now, in ban states, face the additional isolation of navigating reproductive decisions in a surveillance environment where disclosing a pregnancy can trigger investigation. The collapse of what sociologists call "third places" — churches, civic organizations, bars, union halls — has eliminated the infrastructure where adults once formed the relationships that preceded family formation. Church membership dropped below 50 percent for the first time[^78] in recorded polling in 2020, down from 76 percent in 1976[^79]. Civic organization participation has fallen more than 30 percent since the 1990s[^80]. The bar and tavern count in America dropped from 190,000 in 1970 to fewer than 70,000 by 2020[^81]. The places where people once met, courted, and built the social trust that underlies family formation are gone.

The policy architecture is creating the loneliness it claims to cure. The "childless cat lady" rhetoric that defined the 2024 Republican campaign explicitly frames childlessness as moral failure and civic deficiency — while the policies of the same political movement make childbearing financially devastating, medically dangerous, and legally surveilled. Women are not choosing loneliness. They are choosing survival in a system that has made motherhood the riskier option.

The International Lesson

The United States is not the first country to attempt to legislate its way out of demographic decline. It is merely the latest to discover that coercion does not produce babies — it produces resistance. Hungary, under Viktor Orbán, has spent more than 5 percent of its GDP on pro-natalist subsidies — tax breaks, housing grants, loan forgiveness for mothers of three or more — and its fertility rate remains below replacement. South Korea spends $270 billion[^82] on fertility incentives and recorded a fertility rate of 0.72 in 2023[^83], the lowest on earth. Japan has thrown subsidies, workplace reforms, and dating programs at its demographic crisis for two decades and still cannot move the needle above 1.2[^84]. Poland's aggressive pro-natalist policy produced a brief uptick in births that reversed within three years.

The consistent finding across every country that has tried is this: women do not have more children because governments tell them to. They have more children when they have affordable childcare, paid leave, economic security, affordable housing, and confidence that the healthcare system will not kill them in childbirth. The United States offers none of these. It offers instead the world's highest maternal mortality rate among wealthy nations — 32.9 per 100,000[^85] — a cost of raising a child that exceeds a quarter million dollars[^86], zero weeks of federally mandated paid leave, and a political movement that responds to women's refusal to reproduce by calling them selfish. The international evidence is unambiguous: you cannot punish women into motherhood. You can only support them into it. And the United States has chosen punishment.

The Workforce Spiral

The demographic collapse is not merely a social phenomenon. It is an economic death spiral. Fewer births today mean fewer workers in twenty years — fewer taxpayers, fewer Social Security contributors, fewer consumers, fewer caregivers for the aging population that the current political class will, by then, have become.

Abortion access within 50 miles is associated with an 8 percent increase[^87] in women's labor force participation and a $4,500 annual earnings[^88] gain, according to research published in the Journal of Health Economics. The effect is strongest for Black women, who see a 12 percent increase[^89] in labor force participation when a nearby clinic opens. Every clinic that closes, every ban that takes effect, every provider who flees a ban state for a jurisdiction where practicing medicine is not a felony — each one subtracts women from the workforce, subtracts earnings from the economy, and subtracts tax revenue from the states that imposed the bans. Ban states are already showing a divergent trajectory: female labor force participation is declining 3.2 percent[^90] in ban states while rising 1.1 percent[^91] in non-ban states.

The workforce implications extend beyond the women directly affected. The caregiving crisis forces women out of paid work to perform unpaid care. The childcare crisis makes paid work financially irrational for many mothers. The student debt crisis — women hold approximately 65 percent[^92] of the $1.77 trillion[^93] in outstanding student loans — delays partnership and parenthood by years. Each $10,000 in student debt reduces marriage probability by 1.4 percent[^94]. The housing affordability crisis pushes the average first-time homebuyer age to a record high of 38[^95], and 47 percent[^96] of young adults cite housing costs as the reason they have delayed committing to a serious relationship. These are not separate crises. They are the same crisis, expressed in different ledgers — and they all terminate in the same outcome: fewer families, fewer children, a smaller tax base, and a nation that is eating its own demographic future because it would rather control women than support them.

What This Means for the Trump Administration's Legacy

The pro-natalist agenda has produced an anti-natalist outcome. The administration that positioned itself as the champion of the American family has presided over the lowest fertility rate in American history, the highest sterilization rates since the introduction of modern contraception, a collapse in marriage rates, and a generational gender divide that is reshaping the electorate.

Whatever they thought Dobbs was going to accomplish — it has accomplished the opposite. You cannot simultaneously make pregnancy more dangerous, more expensive, more surveilled, and more legally precarious, while cutting the safety net that supports parents, dismantling the childcare infrastructure, and criminalizing the medical decisions of pregnant women — and expect women to have more children. You cannot propose $8 billion[^97] in reproductive health funding cuts, threaten to eliminate Medicaid coverage for 9 million women[^98] through block grants, and defund the family planning programs that prevent the unintended pregnancies you claim to oppose — and call yourself pro-family. You cannot slash the teen pregnancy prevention programs that serve 120,000 adolescents[^99], let the ACA premium subsidies expire so that maternity coverage becomes unaffordable, and propose excluding Planned Parenthood from Medicaid for the 2.8 million patients[^100] who have no other provider — and pretend the resulting babies were the plan.

The women have answered. The answer is no.
